“THE BULLETIN.”
(Published at Sydney, N.S.W.)
The Unique Weekly—Australia’s Nonpareil
Illustrated Newspaper.
Bulletin,”
Premier Australian Literary Journal,
Is the only paper which has a consistent and dis-
tinctive Australian National Policy.
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government.
Une Person, one Vote.
Comnlete Secularisation and Freedom of State
Jnjducation.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
Australia for the Australians.—The cheap
Chinaman, the cheap nigger, and the
cheap European pauper to be absolutely
excluded.
A State Bank, the issue of bank-notes to be a
State monopoly.
The direct election of Ministers by Parlia-
ment, instead of Party Government or
rather Government by Contradiction.
A new Parliamentary System—one House to
be elected by constituencies as at present;
the other to be chosen by the whole
country voting as one Constituency.
A Universal System of Compulsory Life
Insurance.
The entire Abolition of the Private Owner-
ship of Land.
The Referendum.
The Abolition of Titles of so-called “nobility. ’
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter that is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started 13 years ago with no capital but brains,
and has become the representative Australian
journal, because it possesses a vitality lacked
by the countless news paper-ventures in which
Australian capitalists have unavailingly spent
large fortunes.
The Bulletin's conductors claim that every
progressive paper-on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to-profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after th
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-,
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form—printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.-
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride,
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress; it is the forceful summons to the
crusade' against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the claims of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, against the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushman of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the Maoriland
Ranges. A paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based.
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact—
easily ascertainable from any postal official—that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other-paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.

With an ordinary tape measure determine the width
round the joints AA, round the instep 88, round heel CO,
round the ankle DD ; next ascertain the length and width
of tread by placing the foot on a sheet of paper, and
running a lead pencil round the heel and toes. Cut the
outline thus made with a scissors, and enclose with the
other measurements, stating what size you generally take,
what are your peculiarities—a weak ankle, perhaps a pet
corn, ingrowing nails, or a blooming bunion. All these
little matters attended to, if you have a long thin foot, or
a short thick one, with very high instep, send to ELLIS’S,
where you are sure to get a fit. The proprietor, with a
view of introducing his new and simple system into the
coum ry dis: riots where practical bootmaking is unknown,
has decided to supply his patrons at the lowest cash price.

There is to be another big bazaar boom in
Melbourne. Recently, St. Brigid’s R. C. Church
made .£6OO in three weeks in an old skating-rink.
St. Patrick’s Cathedral holds a three weeks fair in
the Exhibition in Nov., and there are rumours of
a huge bazaar and variety show to be held in the
Exhibition for a University club. The Chair of
Music bazaar in the Exhibition, seven years ago,
made a net profit of nearly <£3500, and if this
University one comes off now, it will, with good
management, beat that.
An instance of God-implanted thrift on the
part of a Yankee bookseller :—“ He takes a paper-
covered novel, published at 50 cents, but which he
has bought for 30, and sells it for 35 cents, with
the understanding that the buyer is to return it
when read. When the book comes back he pays
20 cents for it and puts it up for sale again at 30
cents. The second buyer returns the book and
receives 15 cents, and for a third time the book is
on the shelves, this time marked 20 cents, or so.
Again it comes back, the reader takes 10 cents,
and the book will change owners half-a-dozen
times. Books have often paid 2000 per cent, on the
original investment.”
Dear Bulletin, —Well do I mind the time
O’Mulligan was appointed J.P. When he started
from home to be sworn in at Bungletown Quarter
Sessions, his hair and beard were long and
straggled, and he wore a frowzy grey slop-suit
that might have been new ten years before.
While he was in Bungletown he visited a tailor
and a barber with results that will explain them-
selves hereafter. To commemorate the event, dur-
ing his absence Mrs. Mulligan killed a sucking-pig
and two “turkey-roosthers,” and invited half the
neighbourhood to a free-and-easy. She also
scrubbed and dressed all her children—there were
twelve of them—and made them feel straightlaced
and clean and miserable ; and on top of this she
ordered them, under pains and penalties, to fall
into line on the approach of their father and all,
in one voice, exclaim, “Welcome home again,
Your Worship ! ” At 4 o’clock on the fourth day
—the day of the expected return of O’Mul-
ligan— all the dogs rushed out of the
house and started barking. All the guests
rushed out also, though they didn’t bark.
Amid the uproar the voice of Mrs. O’Mulligan
could be heard commanding her terrified offspring
to “sthand in foine. ohildther,” and a strange,
clean-shaven man, dressed in a black swallow-
tailed coat and tall, black “ billy,” could be seen
riding up to the house on a grey mare which some-
how seemed familiar. “ Mebbe it’s the new
praist,” said Mrs. O’Mulligan. Nearer came the
“stranger,” and more intense grew the excite-
ment. Suddenly Mrs. O’Mulligan seemed to lose
her head, and, turning to her “ kids,” exclaimed
in awe-inspiring voice, “Give the salute, childther,
for its none else than his majesty ! ” Patrick
O’Mulligan, J. I’., had returned from Bungletown.
—Yours, &c., H. J. Mac., Burragorang, May 14.

THE GOLDEN AGE.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Heu fugaces ! sings the Roman lyrist of the olden time,
Under tangled vines he sleeps to ripples of the river’s
rhyme,
Breathed upon the mossy walls that saw the singer in his
prime.
Eighteen centuries have flakM their grey ashes on the
earth,
Rich the harvest of the mind that hides the old, barbaric
dearth,
Till the ancient planet shudders at each new titanic
birth.
Gomes unbidden from the sunny lanes where lie the
sleeping years,
Hued by Memory’s white wake, a yearning for the joy and
tears-
Under Tempe’s groves where faun and nymph were man’s
heart-easing peers;
Rebecks, flutes and dulcimers, faint echoes of a musio
lorn
Circling in deep-bosomed forests from the hunters’ matin
horn,
Hvmnfed to the sun ere he has lit the bloom upon the
corn.
Wellington, M.L.
HUBERT CHURCH.

SATURDAY, JUNE 9, 1894.
Some Legal Anomalies.
The many anomalies—some of them comic,
most of them tragic—of New South Wales
law have been pointed out over and over
again with very little effect. A certain dull
and soulless indifference is one of the most
conspicuous features of the Australian
character, and the old laws of the Yande-
monian era linger year after year merely
because the mass of the people suffer from a
fixed conviction that nothing matters much
and very few things matter at all. The
recent case of Montgomery and Williams
drew attention for the moment to a few of
the more glaring vices of the criminal code,
and for the moment there was manifested, as
on many previous occasions, a faint interest
in the workings of the vicious old system.
But with the execution of those criminals
the interest, as on previous occasions, faded
away. The province was roused to a feeble
desire to do something towards repairing
this particular wrong ; the idea of demand-
ing a complete repair of the whole worn-out
and rotten system was entirely beyond it.
Therefore, the old, callous, brutal code, im-
ported from the gaols of Yandemonia, goes
on for ever, no one seriously interfering
with it. It is so brutal, so foolish, so cum-
bersome, murderous, lopsided and corrupt
that forty newspaper articles would not give
space enough to pass even its main features
in review, but a few of them, which have
a direct bearing on the recent trial, may
here be touched upon.
# # •
The case of Montgomery and Williams
turned entirely on the manner of the indict-
ment. Their crime, apart from the original
burglary, was distinctly that of assault with
intent to resist arrest, for which the law
awards life-long penal servitude ; the Attor-
ney-General and the Crown Law Depart-
ment chose, for some unfathomable reason,
to indict them instead for assault with intent
to commit murder—of which intent there
was clearly not sufficient evidence ; and they
were convicted and executed accordingly.
There was no second accusation laid, to
enable the jury, if it considered the intent
to murder not sufficiently proved, to return a
verdict upon the minor charge ; the twelve
men in the box were ordered either to find
the prisoners guilty of the capital charge—
which was utterly illogical—or to find them
altogether innocent— which was equally
absurd. Trial by jury has always been more
or less of a farce; the Crown Law Office this
time turned it into a burlesque. The com-
mon assumption is that the jury has
power to decide the guilt or innocence
of a prisoner according to the evidence ;
the reality is that the jury is not
allowed to take any notice whatever
of the crime which is proved by the evidence
U «: 6 - 8 S * 3 a^so cr i me which a certain
official has set down in the indict-
ment. In the present instance, the crime
proved by the evidence and the crime
mentioned in the indictment were about as
far apart as if the accused had been charged,
on the same evidence, with bigamy, forgery,
and horse-stealing. And if they had been
so charged the verdict would probably have
been just the same. Owing to the workings
of the good old Yandemonian system the
average jury has learned to take most of the
case for granted ; it simply decides that the
accused have done something, and seldom
inquires whether the thing they have done
and the thing they are charged with doing is
the same or not.
• * • *
Provided the prisoner is too poor to hire
an able advocate who can expose the dis-
crepancies of the case, it is only necessary
to be able to prove clearly that he has done
something, and the Crown can procure his
conviction upon almost any charge it pleases
to select. The average jury consists of
twelve sheep driven blindly over a precipice.
The consequence of the system is seen in
their frequent efforts to undo their work
when it is too late. In the Montgomery-
Williams case, as in many others, the jury-
men afterwards did their utmost to secure a
reprieve, and most of them expressed a
tardy regret at their own illogical verdict.
The apparent reason of their regret was that
the verdict didn’t express their convictions ;
only it expressed them as nearly as the
indictment would allow, and as it had to
be that or nothing they let it go on the
assumption that it didn’t make much differ-
ence. The difference, as they afterwards
discovered, consisted of two lives,
• » •
A partial, if not a complete, remedy con-
sists in dividing the crime of murder or
attempted murder into various degrees, ac-
cording to the surrounding circumstances,
with a sliding scale of punishrrtents attached,
so that the jury may not only decide that
the prisoner is guilty, but may also decide
how guilty he is, and fix his punishment
within certain limits. This is the system
adopted in the United States. The American
law recognises that homicide, like any other
crime, is an offence which varies according
to the attendant circumstances ; the law of
Australia regards it as a cast-iron fact which
knows no variation whatever, and prescribes
the same punishment for the man who, cold-
bloodedly and slowly, kills off a near relative
by poison for the sake of the insurance-
money and for the person who, in a sudden
access of passion, shoots a brutal enemy under
the most extreme provocation. Then, having
decided that the crime is the same in either
case, it decides further that it is not the
same by allowing the jury to tack on to the
verdict a recommendation to mercy, and
having done this it gets back again to the
old position by allowing a despotic Executive
to tyrannically disregard the recommendation
at its own sole will and pleasure.
* • •
Other anomalies turn up at every step of
the proceedings. The Crown professes to
have no craving after convictions ; it doesn’t
wish to win an unjust cause and is as
anxious to procure the acquittal of the inno-
cent as the punishment of the guilty. Its
practice, unfortunately, doesn’t square with
its theory. The penniless prisoner who is
tried for his life is allowed, in New South
Wales, the miserable sum of fifteen guineas
(until within the last six years it was jive
guineas) for the purposes of his defence.
This amount is barely sufficient to pay for
some cheap solicitor and some low-priced
barrister ; if any lawyer of eminence and
capacity takes the matter up on such terms,
it is only through the rare emotion of
charity. The case for the Crown, on the
other hand, is prepared (while accused is
locked up in gaol) by some of the ablest
lawyers in the land, backed up by the police,
the detective department, and the whole
resources of the Treasury. The odds are
the entire consolidated revenue against £l5
155.; an unlimited quantity of the best legal
talent in the country against one cheap
practitioner; all the facilities for collect-
ing evidence which the detective force, the
railway and telegraph service, and the whole
Government machinery can afford against—
nothing. A Maxim gun against a bow and
arrow is nothing by comparison with this
ghastly discrepancy. The miserable allow-
ance for defence purposes is rather a loss to
the prisoner than a gain ; he loses the
limited amount of sympathy usually extended
by the jury to an undefended man, and he
remains practically undefended (or even
doubly-prosecuted) all the same.
* • •
Had Montgomery and Williams been
more adroitly defended their lives would
have been saved—always assuming that
among the jury were some men possessing
faint flickers of reason; for a verdict of
acquittal on the capital count would not have
saved the criminals from re-arraignment
under the clause prescribing life-servitude
for the offence of wounding while resisting
legal arrest. What happened in the Wil-
liams Montgomery case has happened in
many others. If the chances are to be
equal, so that the innocent man who is
accused by mistake will have as good a show
to get off as to be hanged, the legal talent
must be something like on a level. There
must be a Public Defender as well as a
Public Prosecutor, with the same resources at
command; and the system whereby the State
cheerfully supplies £2OO or £3OO to pre-
pare the facts against the accused, and
grudgingly puts up £l5 15s. to pay for
the preparation of the case in his favour,
must be abolished as an insult to
justice. As matters stand at present the
arithmetic of the matter is this : Barristers,
like other men, are sportsmen, and a law-
yer s business is to win his case whether he
is in the right or in the wrong. When the
public gladly pays one barrister thirty guineas
tor a day’s work and will only pay another
eight guineas, it simply means that, so far as
the public can estimate, the former is four
times as likely to win as the latter, the cir-
cumstances being equal. Therefore, when
the State hires the expensive lawyer and
gives the prisoner the cheap one, it simply
means that, apart from the evident merits
the case, the State gambles for the
prisoner’s life with four chances to one in its
favour. Any pauper arraigned for his life
should, at State expense, have a Queen’s
Counsel to defend him.
* * *
The position of the Executive as a Court
of criminal appeal is another quaint feature
in the complicated anomaly of the criminal
law. The second and informal trial which
the Cabinet always holds in capital cases is
as essential a part of the proceedings as the
formal hearing before a Judge and jury, for
the Cabinet is the only Court which is
authorised to take extenuating circumstances
into consideration. In the second trial the
defendant is not represented, and the
prosecutor the Attorney-General acts
as one of the Judges. The tribunal
holds its sittings absolutely in secret,
and gives no reasons for its judgments.
Whether the evidence is considered in detail,
or how the decision is arrived at, the Court
itself only knows. In a case like that of
Montgomery and his companion, where life
or death depended entirely upon the tech-
nical question as to the fairness of the
Attorney-General's indictment, the Attorney-
General, as the legal adviser of the Cabinet,
sat, for all the world knows to the contrary,
as one of a Court to decide an appeal against
himself. The members of the Cabinet have
mostly no legal training ; and they are not
even supposed to sit as an unbiassed tribunal,
as attempts are openly made to influence
them by petitions, political deputations,
torchlight processions, and the like. And
until Australia establishes a court of criminal
appeal and transfers to it the prerogative of
mercy now held by the Cabinet, this hap-
hazard method of administering justice is
without remedy.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
State Interference.
The tendency of the Labour movement is
always to run to extremes. The friends of
democracy are for ever inclined to exaggerate
its benefits, to forget that it has defects only
less than those of the systems of government
which it aims to replace. They ascribe to “the
State” a magical power, a supernatural honesty;
forgetting that it is only the sum of the units
which compose it, and that the stream of
government cannot possibly rise higher than
its source. They are perpetually endeavouring
to drive the nail further than it will go, to
cleanse the outside of the platter before the
inside, to substitute public legislation for in-
dividual morality. Democracy is just as faulty
as despotism, and contains precisely the same
element of human nature ; but it is juster,
and hence we have a sounder, broader basis to
work upon in our efforts to improve human
nature. The worst foes of democracy are those
which lurk within its own household in the
shape of ignorance and prejudice. Universal
suffrage does not create a millennium at a blow.
Municipal government in England may be more
tyrannical; but in America it is mere corrupt.
And if Jack Cade came again to abolish poverty
by selling seven halfpenny loaves for a penny,
giving the three-hooped pot ten hoops, and
making it a felony to drink small beer, it is
certain that many would flock to his standard.
One of the candidates before the Willoughby
(N.S.W.) Labour Electoral League has issued
a leaflet announcing his “views.” Among
other things, he believes that people should
travel free on the State railways ; that free
meals, as well as free education, should bo
given to State school - children; and that
“ beyond a miner’s right mining on private
property Bill ” all mines should be nationalised
and worked for the benefit of all. It is
evidently believed that this is only a question
of passing so many Acts in so many chapters
and so many clauses. It does not strike this
democrat that, taking the whole field of mining,
a profit is only secured by unremitting toil
and watchfulness unknown in the civil service.
Many mines now do not pay the owners : how
then could they pay the State, “ the Govern-
ment stroke ” being notoriously ineffective ?
Most railways are run at a loss; free
traffic would increase the loss. Eree meals
to children are an additional loss, besides
taking away the parent’s incentive to work,
and thus demoralising industry. But who
will bear these losses ? Oh, the State—
i.e., the taxpayer. Thus, side by side with
continually increasing nationalisation of i n .
dustry we are to have continually - in.
creasing taxation. The issue must ho
national bankruptcy. Obviously, such
programme, however inviting, is practically
absurd. Yet there are doubtless many who
believe its realisation possible, in spite of tho
lessons of history. Certainly the State can do
much. It can make just laws and reform unjust
ones. It can open and keep open to all the
avenues of industry. It can protect the weak
against the strong. It cannot make all equal
but it can give to all equal opportunities, ft
can place the poor man’s child and the" rich
man s on the same footing of natural ability
It can tax the inheritances of the wealthy for
the benefit of the labourers by whose efforts
they are chiefly amassed. It can prevent by
legislation the evils arising from the monopoly
ofW it cannot ensure human happiness
while human egoism remains a constant factor
The world can only be bettered by better men!
The Imperial Destitute.
In the first half of the current century the
colonies were regarded in England as pro-
videntially-devised retreats for impoverished
aristocrats, or the mawvais sujets of genteel
families possessed of a little backstairs influ-
ence. Downing-street and its representatives
across the seas were kept busy looking after
the welfare of these interesting emigrants, and
seeing that they duly obtained the billets
suited to their station in life. When the
“dependencies” grew too big for this sort of
patronage, and insisted on stocking their own
civil service preserves, Mrs. Mother Country
found herself suddenly cut off from a profitable
assortment of colonial perquisites. For a time
she bore the hardship as philosophically as
possible until, in the year of Queen Victoria’s
jubilee, a hungry London club coterie con-
ceived the brilliant idea of establishing, in the
metropolis itself, a magnificent institution in
honour of Imperialism which, it was thought, the
colonies might be humbugged into supporting.
Having the private ear of the Prince of
Wales, they easily persuaded him to give then-
precious conception forth to the world, and,
with a lot of appeals to loyalty and patriotism
and profuse flattery of “Greater Britain,” the
ingenious scheme was launched. For a moment
it looked as if the notion would catch on, and
the colonies be gulled into providing the funds
for a superior order of British benevolent
asylum. Initiatory thousands of current coin
rolled in from Australia, the Cape, and Canada,
and a big building, filled with sinecure offices,
sprang into existence and was gorgeously
inaugurated by royalty. But the colonies soon
discovered what fools they were making of
themselves, and, spite of the exhortations of
insinuating travelling touts, and the frantic
protests of their own bebaubled General
Agents in London, buttoned up their breeches-
pockets. Now it is threatened by cable that,
unless our Pactolean stream : s set flowing again,
the imposing Imperial Institute shall be con-
verted into a head-quarters for Masonry or
something of that kind. Let it.
Are Australians Cruel ?
Francis Adams and other critics have charged
Australians with having on their underside the
taint of cruelty. We have some characteristics
which go to confirm this view, many which
oppose it. The bush lad who carelessly kills a
horse by hard usage becomes, perhaps, no rarer.
But the same lad, grown to a bushman, loves
his horse as his father, his dog as his brother.
Mature bush opinion is dead against the man
who is cruel to tame animals. Wild animals
are different; they bring into play the old
savage hunter s instinct, which is not special
but racial. And what wonders of suffering and
self-denial will not the men on the outskirts of
Australian civilisation, the pioneers, perform
for a mate in distress ! In our towns, the larri-
kin, the young man, is again the type of cruelty.
But cruelty is innate in youth. The youth of
man corresponds with the youth of the world.
I here was a time when every man was an
Ishmaelite, fighting for his own hand, hardly by
constant warfare keeping at bay the forces of
armed Nature around him. When we are
young the memory of that time still lingers
with us. The youth has not learnt the lesson
of civilisation. But what becomes of the
larrikins? Comparatively few go to gaol : our
crime-average is remarkably low. '’Ask the
police, and they will tell you that many, most,
become hard-working, respectable citizens. The
touch of a woman at the right moment and all
is changed. The man asserts himself, with—
Eyes full of fatherhood and tender care
For small young children,
putting behind him early frolics and follies.
And, against the charge of cruelty, note the
keen public sense of private wrong reached in
Australia partly, perhaps, through universal
education ; partly, perhaps, through our raised
status of comfort, making contrast with misery
more keen ; and a little, perhaps, through the
revulsion from the horrors of our early history.
Compare Australia with England. Reprobation
of the worst class of assaults—those against the
person is far stronger here than there ; and
sentences are much more adequate. The
constant crucifixion of the English poor by
English property-owners pilloried every week
in London Truth—a remnant of the time when
the poor were property, in life and limb
absolutely at the disposal of slave-holding,
slave-breeding masters—could not happen in
Australia. Or take this case, from a letter
recently written to an English paper by one of
a coroner’s jury who inquired into the death of
a Dartmoor convict:—•
The prisoner was here about nine months, and for
ciyht months out of th&t time, as far as my memory
serves me he had been complaining of illness, and had
been punished for complaining. In the month of Novem-
ber alone he was, I think, punished seven times for com-
plaining of being ill and for not doing his work, his
punishment being confinement in a separate cell, bread
and water, and loss of marks. Ilis last punishment was
on November 27th when be was again sentenced to three
days separate cells, eight ounces of bread and a pint of
Indian meal mixed, to drink morning and evening, and
a plank bed. About mid day on the 30th he finished this
sentence, and was again ordered for work (reclaiming
ia asserted that he could not
walk out to the works, and had to be assisted. lie then


told the warder he could not work, and was (as is usual)
handcuffed, with his hands behind his back, and had to
remain there until the afternoon work was over. Next
morning he was found dead in his cell 1
Here is a record of the official murder of a
sick man which shows only too well how close
the England of to-day is to the England which
established the convict system in Australia. It
passed over unnoticed. Everybody was afraid
to lift a protesting voice; even the juryman
who exposed the cruelty did not dare to sign
his name. But conceive the whirlwind of fury
which would sweep over Australia were such a
thing reported here. Parliament would demand
instant inquiry ; and press and public would
not rest till justice was done. Perhaps public
sympathy occasionally errs. But it is the
charge of cruelty we are concerned with ; and
' certainly that does not agree with the strong
feeling against punishing even the worst
criminals, unless the legality of their trial and
the justice of their sentence are absolutely clear.
Cruelty craves for blood ; the majority of Aus-
tralians have a horror of bloodshed. Can the
charge, then, be sustained ?
♦
The Origin of a Hanging Law.
Hansard, that grim record of the lies and
corruption and imbecility of the political past,
tells an instructive story about the origin of the
New South Wales written law which makes
hanging the penalty for “ attempted murder,”
ami consequently of the unwritten law which
makes it also the penalty for an unpremeditated
free-fight where murder might have ensued but
didn’t. The Act in question was passed by the
Stuart Government in which the dreary H. E.
Cohen was Minister of Justice, During the
debate it was moved that the death-penalty
should be changed to life-long penal servitude,
but the proposition was extinguished in the
course of a violent speech by Mr. Cohen on
behalf of the Ministry. The following is an
instructive extract as showing the methods of
Cohenian logic :
On reading the clause honourable members will find
that there is in the offence the important ingredient of
attempting to commit murder. So far as the offender is
concerned he may have done all things in his power to
kill and with a view to commit murder, and the offender
may administer poison assuming that what he does will
be fully effectual in accomplishing his diabolical intention,
but owing to some accident beyond his control death does
not ensue. Will honourable members say that in that
case so far as the Act is bound up with so wicked an in-
tention the moral character of the offence is less heinous
or atrocious than if the offence has resulted in death ?
. . . We are only perpetuating the law as it exists in the
colony, and so far as we can gather we have not found that
the public have ever evinced any sense of injustice which
has led them to call for a change in the law such as is
suggested by the honourable member. He says the only
crime punishable in England with death is murder, or
vr tere life is actually sacrificed, and I believe that to be
i.ue case ; but I saw in a late edition of the Times, I think
it was, a review of the recent work entitled, “The History
of Criminal Law,” by Sir Filzjames Stephen (a relative
of Sir Alfred Stephen), and if that review was correct this
eminent gentleman has given expression to the opinion
that for some of tfte crimes which were formerly punished
by death, but with respect to which that penalty has
been abolished, it ought to be re-imposed, and he refers
not only to murder but also to attempts to murder, and
goes so far as to say that where persons become incor-
rigibly dishonest, and even in cases where extreme
violence was habitually used, the death-penalty ought to
be imposed. If we take this view as a reflex of a growing
opinion on the part of the British public, it appears that
some change is coming over that opinion, and there are
facts which favour the presumption.
This shows, in the first place, that the clause
was specially devised with a view to attempted
deliberate murder by poison—and in the case of
wilful poisoning, the intent is almost beyond
question; not for damage done in a chance
affray. And it shows, in the second place, how
the Stephen influence has lain heavy upon all
re.ent New South Wales history. The English
law, to which Australia generally goes with
slavish reverence for its precedents, pre-
scribed and prescribes death for actual murder
on'y. But its authority was over-ruled by the
obliging Cohen because a baleful relative of
that old, grim Yandemonian, Alfred Stephen,
had expressed his own unsupported opinion
that men should be hanged, not only for mur-
der, but also for attempted murder, violent
assault, and incorrigible dishonesty ! If the
dictum of old Mr. Stephen’s relative was of
any value, then it should have been accepted
in its entirety, in which case at least six of the
membersofParliament(piofethedivision-list)who
voted with Cohen would since then have been
suspended by the neck till they were dead for a
large assortment of callous swindles. But as
the House unfortunately didn’t accept Stephen’s
relative’s views in their entirety, it thereby
tacitly acknowledged that Stephen’s relative
was no authority to go upon. Mr. Cohen,
Ijpwever, blandly assumed that because
/tephen’s relative announced this blood-stained
and ghoulish doctrine, it was probably “a
reflex of a growing opinion on the part of the
British public,” and the House took his word
for it !!! Attempted murder is a capital
crime to-day in N.S. Wales partly because
Cohen said that he thought he had read
somewhere that somebody related to Alfred
Stephen had said it should be so, and because
he considered, on his own responsibility, that
the statement of Stephen’s relative was possi-
bly a reflex of British sentiment. As it proved
to be otherwise, and as Stephen’s relative is
exploded now, and Cohen is politically extinct,
it is about time to consider the matter afresh
from an entirely new standpoint, in which
Cohen, and the bloodstained traditions of the
Stephen family, will be left entirely out of the
question.
Jhe S.M. Herald’s recent ably-written article
insisting that a community should be governed
by law as existing in cold type, instead of by
the excited sentiment of the day, goes to the
very point the consideration of which has made
some of the deepest thinkers recoil from written
law altogether. The question comes thus to a
vigorous intellect: “Am Ito do so-and-so,
because someone, or a number of people, said
so-and-so before I was born ?” But, apart from
this tenable philosophical objection to being the
slave of the dead, the Herald dogina is er-
roneous on other grounds. Take any Australian
1 arliament. As likely as not that body was
partially drunk (or absent) when it placed any
law on the statute-book ; if it wasn’t partially
tlrunk or mostly absent, it is ten to one it was
dragooned at the time by some Minister,
who would capsize the Government and drive
members to the country if he was not allowed
lus way with his little Bill. In such circum-
stances a law is passed. When, however, the
application of that law requires to be made—
on the gallows—manhood takes the place of
expediency and voices rise in protest. The
men who were drunk are sorry they put the
Act on the statute-book ; the men who were
absent are sorry they were not present; the men
who were dragooned are sorry they obeyed, and,
with the last vestige of soul they own, they yell
to have the law set aside. And the yell is the
only saving trait about them.
Laws dealing with life and liberty should be
reviewed in every Parliament and confirmed or
modified in the light of added experience.
1 hose acquainted with legislative procedure
know what chances govern many votes and
how the majority of one year may be the
minority of the year following. It is often
worse than futile to say that simply because a
law is on the Statute Book it should, apart
from the especial circumstances of the time
or event, be executed to the letter. There
are scores of unrepealed enactments on
every Statute Book which no Government
would attempt to put in force—which
proves the hollowness of the protestation that
until an Act is repealed Ministers are bound
under oath to put it in operation. The main
body of N.S. W. criminal law is now eleven
years old, and in the myriad changes of opinion
in all other directions it is talking blindly to say
that the public mind is in this matter as it was
then. Parkes and Melville have both repu-
diated the doctrine of capital punishment since
that time, though both voted for that doctrine—
yea, voted to make capital even attempts to
murder ! —when the measure was before the
House, How many other members have also
changed ?
At Maryborough (Yic.) Queen’s Birthday
carnival a banner was carried in the pro-
cession with the inscription “ Back to the
Land.” The day previous, by the local bench
(theP.M. presiding), seven lads, ages from 16
to 20, who had wandered from various towns
“ back to the land,” were sent to gaol (for the
first time) to do six months hard. Their offence
was going into an untenanted house and cooking
a feed of cabbages “supposed to have been
stolen.” This scandalous sentence is an apt
commentary on Premier Patterson’s boast at
opening of Parliament that he had drafted
many of the unemployed from Melbourne to
profitable industries in the interior.
♦
Considine, at Broken Hill, was one. Hali., at
Parkes, is two. Two deaths in three months,
besides a score of indiscriminate wounds and
misses, is the price New South Wales pays for
Premier Dibbs’ hasty blunder in arming the
police. But for that both would probably be
alive to-day. Considine owes his death directly
to the edict; Hall probably owes his to the
reflex action on the burglar who shot him.
Against the advice of the head of the police
force, against the wish of its coolest officers,
Dibbs yielded to a personal impulse ; and,
having been clearly put in the wrong, stays
there because he is too obstinate to acknow-
ledge error. The blood of Considine is hardly
cold before an armed burglar, disturbed in a
country store, wounds John William Hall,
who merely “lingered in great agony ” till he
expired on May 29. Probably there is another
hanging in prospect, with another stirring-up
of public excitement, a fresh creation of ten-
dencies to crime, and a new determination on
the part of every burglar, present and prospec-
tive, to shoot rather than be shot; with more
slaughter and more hangings in increasing ratio
until somebody with a little more brains and a
little less vanity steps into Dibbs’ shoes. The
gallows is the father and the mother of murder.
Says The Queenslander (cx-Courier) :
The Kanaka is finding out his real market value
and refuses to re-engage on the original terms'
Why should he? He should get all that he is
worth. The idea of obtaining labour from New
Guinea on the seasonal system is finding favour with
the cane-growers. Six months in Queensland and six
months in New Guinea. This system would get rid of all
or most of the objections to the existing supply. The
natives would be recruited under the British flag. From
first to last they would be under Governmental super-
vision and regulation. And we may be sure that they
would soon discover their real value and exact it.
In the foregoing effusion there are several
remarkable statements. Firstly, the Kanaka
is to be got rid of because he has found out his
“ real market value and refuses to engage on
the old terms ” (coarse, unsuitable food ; conse-
quent chronic dysentery and cdrner in plantation
cemetery). To remedy this the Papuan is to
be imported, inasmuch as he doesn’t know his
real value. Secondly (for the comfort of object-
ing humanitarians), he will soon find out his
real value and exact it. Thirdly, and lastly,
the traffic will be perfectly clean and respectable,
inasmuch as the Papuan will be recruited under
that glorious British flag, beneath whose shadow
the heathen waxeth fat, even from Vandemonia
unto Matabeleland.

A FEW years ago some admiring friends got
Parkes’s portrait painted, and Parkes’s col-
league, Carruthers, bought the garish pre-
sentment out of the public funds without
Parkes’ official knowledge, and sent it to the
trustees of the New South Wales National
Gallery, where it was duly hanged. Now
Dibbs’s portrait is going to be hanged there
also, to keep it company. Dibbs’s portrait
was painted by a Sydney artist and purchased
by some enthusiastic admirers, who pre-
sented it to Dibbs with a suggestion that
Dibbs should present it to the gallery,
and Dibbs presented it accordingly. The
gallery - trustees are supposed to reject
pictures of insufficient quality, but these two
got through all right. Very likely they got
through on their merits, but at the same
time it is difficult to judge the portrait of a
Premier on its merits seeing that the original
can, if he likes, eject any dissenting trustee, or
make himself beastly unpleasant in other ways.
Therefore, no Premier with any tact or any of
the decent self-consciousness which ordinarily
characterises men who are regarded as gentle-
men, should send his own portrait to the
Gallery or let any of his colleagues send it
without his knowledge. He should let it be
sent by an admiring nation after he is dead—a
considerable time after he is dead, if possible.
lo hang your own portrait in a public place of
memorial is as grossly egotistic as to carve with
your own hands a flattering inscription for your
own gravestone. Anyone who likes to draw a
fancy sketch of Dibbs tearing around and damn-
ing Shikargo when his picture came back with
aji intimation that the subject wasn’t of suffi-
cient public interest, or some other such message,
can understand how difficult it is to take an
unbiassed view of a Premier’s portrait when
it is forwarded by himself.
«.
A Melbourne jury, the other day, found two
men guilty of a serious offence, but recom-
mended them to mercy. Thereupon Chief
Justice Madden explained to the accused that
he had intended to give them a long term in
gaol and a flogging apiece, but in view of the
jury’s recommendation he would leave out the
flogging and reduce the sentence to two years.
This common-sense procedure was evidently
based on the fact that Australian law
is built on a mechanical principle which
takes no account at all of extenuating cir-
cumstances, and the only way in which a
jury can take extenuating circumstances
into account is by the above form of “recom-
mendation.” In New South Wales, according
to the Montgomery-Williams precedent, the
convicts would have got the flogging and the
long sentence just the same. The principle of
Botany Bay law, as laid down by the latest
executions, is that extenuating circumstances
don’t count for anything, and that a jury’s
recommendation to mercy is so much wasted
sentiment. The Bulletin advises future
Botany Bay juries to dole out their own mercy,
and to trust neither Judge nor Executive.
A Victorian clergyman has touched a respon-
sive chord in the soul of the aggregate preacher
by denouncing the cheap advertising parson.
Many of the Melbourne shepherds now make a
speciality of cheap marriage to suit the times,
and announce in the lost-dog column of the
Age that they are prepared to fix up, for half-
a-sovereign, or even less, all young men who
desire to give away half their victuals in
order to get the other half cooked. The
parson aforesaid abuses these individuals
as “self-styled clergymen,” and declares
that the Government which allows them
to follow their holy calling is “ legitima-
tising immorality.” It is difficult to follow the
logic of this angry expounder from Kyneton,
but apparently he represents the large and
infuriated section of the church which charges
from £2 to £5 for the marriage-service, and
he utterly loathes the “ scab ” preacher who
threatens to cut down rates. Why a 10s.
marriage should be immoral when a 40s. one isn’t
is not apparent to the lay intelligence, nor does
it appear very distinctly why the cheap preacher
is any more “self-styled” than the expensive one.
It the margin between morality and immorality
is just 305., then the two are so very much
alike that the difference isn’t worth discussing.
The principal considerations in the conduct of a
marriage ceremony are that it shall be per-
formed by a clean clergyman, and that there
shall be someone at the door to keep out the
dogs and small boys who want to enter and
prowl around. These conditions being ful-
filled, the rest doesn’t matter much. The
10s. marriage and the 40s. marriage and
the £5 one are, in most cases, all
failures alike, and the boiling - over of the
Kyneton pastor seems to be a purely selfish
yearning after his vanished coin. It is the
unhappy peculiarity of the parson that he
always professes to regard his lost coin from a
high spiritual standpoint, but to the general
public the shepherd who denounces his cheap
rival on religious grounds is just as sordid and
as commonplace as the sixpenny barber who
swears at the threepenny barber next door.
Marriage is, in any case, a tolerably ridiculous-
looking function and a source of ribald mirth
to the crowd at the door, and even if the tariff
were put up to £lOO it wouldn’t be improved to
any great extent.

MTlwraith was much impressed with Canadian
railway management, which has brought the
system of political jobbery to perfection, put
hundreds of millions of dollars into the pockets
of promoters, syndicators, and land-sharks, and
draws the whole wealth of the country into the
net of private monopoly. He thinks he under-
stands now how to develops Queensland re-
sources by building more railways, while
millions of acres of the best agricultural
land in the world lie barren on the Darling
Downs, Peak Downs, and elsewhere, with
a railway running through the middle of them,
locked against cultivation by grabbers holding
for a rise. Clap a land-tax on monopoly, and
force it to sell at a reasonable price to the
farmers, who are now breaking their hearts
over hard ground far from the arteries of traffic—
that is the way to develops Queensland re-
sources. As to the mineral lands, if they are
alienated the State must reserve the right of
royalty on products ; and if the line is built in
exchange for land-grants the State must own
and control it.

believer; every State must be able to say who is married
and who isn’t. The civil marriage should be the only
legal marriage—leaving the ecclesiastical flummery to be

verdict of the jury in the Montgomery-Williams case was

hang. It so, why didn’t they find both men not guilty of
intent to murder, and leave them to be arraigned for
wounding to prevent arrest—the penalty for which is life-
imprisonment? Because, it may be answered, they didn’t
know such a course was in their power. If they didn’t
know it, whose fault was that?
Tom Bent graced the re-opening of the
Vic. Assembly before the session’s business had
started by observing that Patterson’s knighthood
had been well deserved. There are moments in a
great man’s life when he experiences a warm
glow of satisfaction with himself pass up and down
his backbone. Such a moment was Patterson’s,
we imagine, when he heard his true knightly
character attested to by his distinguished friend,
Bent. His historic under-lip trembled, his
nostrils gently dilated, and the veiled glance of his
frank, ingenuous eyes spoke as follows: “ For
all particulars concerning my integrity of purpose
and brilliant achievements, up to date, apply to
Thomas 8., who understands my intellect and
reads my heart.”

June 5,1894.
My Dear Moorabinda—
A small dispute cropped up in the papers the
other day over the tobacco question—why should
the smoking section “pollute” the atmosphere
that “ God has made pure for me and all ” ? That
was what “ Non-smoker ” wanted to know—
egged on by the letter of “ Young Englishwoman ”
who had to give up a good view of Colquhoun’s
calves at the Tennis Tourney because a nasty
brute was puffing smoke down her ears. “Is it
the custom,” she asked indignantly, “for Aus-
tralian gentlemen to smoke in the vicinity of
strange women ?”
If she had asked me that weird question I should
have distinctly answered, “ Yes, they do it in self-
defence.” Strange women frequently take about
with_ them an atmosphere of stale stephanotis
fighting with a liberal douche of fresh Frangipani.
And even if they are unperfumed they generally
manage to stifle you with various strange-smelling
moth-paralysers that hang to their furs and bring
a keenly-smelling man to his cigar-ease to drown
the overpowering bouquet of the ladies in front of
him. For man is a sharp-nosed animal—much
keener on the scent than his female half. I know
a Sydney man who sits in his upstairs office and
“smells ” you when you enter the main entrance,
at the foot of a reasonably long flight of stairs.
You needn’t be specially perfumed, or specially
anything—he simply knows the individual aroma
of a large circle of people, and knows before they
knock who is going to enter, and whether to go
under the table or not.
Ik seems to me that stale scent versus fresh
tobacco would make a very even squabble—if any-
body has any business to quarrel over the state of
the atmosphere. Bub I really don’t see why all
the violent adjectives should be heaped on to the
smokers. My little crusade is going to be against
the women who eat peppermint lozenges in any
compartment not specially set apart for the purpose
and the men who breathe whisky down my back
when the curtain goes up in the third act.
In court, ladies are privileged to write dubious
evidence in blushing pencil, but it’s not always
only their natural modesty that demands it—it is
sometimes the maiden shame of their legal adviser.
Only the other day an Australian Judge went out
of his way to congratulate a divorce-plaintiff on
the “'chaste and reserved way ” she submitted one
or two overproof details in faint pencil. “Any-
oody could see” (even a divorce-court Judge!)
“ that she was a chaste and respectable woman.”
And all the time it was her “ reserved ” solicitor
who had heard how freely her tale of woe out-
poured itself, and who had given her a strong tip to
olushingly write what she might otherwise have
reeled off orally in answer to questions. When
the cab is out of the bag men, to put it mildly, are
sven more bashful than women.
A really clever divorce-lawyer wants to be a
£ood stage-manager as well as a bond-breaker.
“You can’t appear in court in that dress,” lately
screamed a Sydney lawyer at a too stylishly-
ittired plaintiff who was got up more for the
Block than to tell a woeful story to his Honor.
And, what’s more, the lady went back post-haste
ind changed her garments ! And there is one
ocal shark with a good eye for effect who turned
i smart-looking, up-to-date divorcee into a meek,
lowdy victim simply by advising the lady to
out her hat on back-to-front!
Among the variegated crowd who worried “ The
Cabinet Minister,” at Sydney Lyceum, Emma
Temple wore the most up-to-date garment, to my
mind. A sort of Russian blouse gone to seed on
:he skirts, of pale grey stuff cleverly trimmed with
steel passementerie and silver fox, and toque hat
punctuated with violets, was very neat and dis-
tinctly the most fashionable rigging in a rather
garishly-apparelled play. For example, Mrs.
Brough in a parrot-pink and lettuce-green court
Jress (I don’t think Queen Victoria, who is fond
af seeing a lot of her lady subjects would have
been satisfied with the moderate display of collar-
bone at the Lyceum, by the way) looked like the
Queen of the May, Mother. Jenny Watt-Tanner
(who gabbled so that I couldn’t make out what she
was saying half the time) unconsciously sported
Carbine’scolours; Grace Noble’s white brocade was
insipidly debutanteish, and I thought the most
astonishing thing in the much-prattled-of Court-
scene was the way the he - actors steered their
course amidst yards and yards of stuff without
treading on any “cats’ tails.” Apropos, savants
say that everything has an equipoise, which
accounts for the fact that while all the women in
the “Minister” were positively gasping under
yards and yards of stuff, the clothes of the men
had rushed up above their knees. I think Frank-
lyne made the most symmetrical show when the
kilts came on, though none of the B. & B. he-
actors suggested potstick similes when they aban-
doned trousers for the nonce.
There was more or less pretty dressing in the
other acts, but I seemed to scent sartorial resur-
rections in more than one instance, and I only pin
my complete approval to a red geranium (the
fashionable flower of the moment) growing perkily
out of Mrs. Brough’s black-jet bonnet. By the
way, it was lovely to observe how society strained
its ears while Mrs. Brough catalogued the table
solecisms committed by Dot Boucicault, and
I was really sorry “ Lady Tworabley ”
didn’t add a genial tip about sucking up
soup like a human sponge. Because there
was a representative man who is built that
way hanging over the circle edge, and I did so
want him to have his guilt brought home to him,
so to speak. It wouldn’t be half a bad plan if the
B. and B, Co. laid itself out to give a little
sugar-coated lesson on manners and tone in every
comedy. Sydney society always listens so breath-
lessly when vulgarities are being catalogued (lest
it shouldn’t know when to laugh) that it wouldn’t
miss a pointer, I’m sure.
Renouf played the femininely-unattractive part
of a man wandering in thick-soled boots among
groves of patent-leather, and the only thing he
said that interested me was when he complained
of the floury flunkey who froze him on the mat.
A man who has been the guest of dukes in Lon-
don told me that he never felt nervous about any-
body in any great house, except the overpowering
servants. The duke or marquis, or whatever he
might happen to be foregathering with, was
always delightful, but the footman invariably
made him feel as if his neat pasteboard was a
begging letter. If dukes were in the habit of
answering their own street-doors society at ’Ome
would be ten times less exclusive.
“The Flying Scud,” at Her Majesty’s, isn’t a
vehicle for the display of much wardrobe—there
are nineteen men to five women in the bill. The
ineffable Mr. Westmacott’s soup-plate bouton-
niere of white ’mums rose up and obscured the
collar-plants of the other men in the 4th act, bub
why didn’t Mo Davis rush into a willie-wagtail
suit as the rest did, when the action of the play
demanded it? A Hebrew who lends to the aris-
tocracy generally gets into his shiny shirt and
diamond studs before the sun sets —and so does
one who doesn’t, for that matter. The Children
of Israel are given to generous displays of naked
neck and wide, deep visions of boiled shirt-fronts
with a red silk-handkerchief thrust in for effect
four inches lower than the big diamond stud, so
Mo quite failed to convince me when he attended
a swell card-party in an ostler checked suit that
didn’t even match itself.
Talking of the Chosen, Semitic circles in Bara-
taria have been stirred to their uttermost depths
by the wild conduct of two young ladies who both
fell in love with Gentile youths, contrary to the
by-laws and the meaning of the Act. The first
damsel (well-known in society) has eloped with
her Gentile, and her people have steeped them-
selves in deepest mourning to show what they
think of her goings-on, but I think the elopee is
the one to wear the weeds. By her rash act she
forfeits a substantial income left her by a near
relative.
The career of the second damsel is more compli-
cated. She also loved a Christian, but papa dis-
covered the amourette, showed Adolphus out, and
packed her off to a distant place for sober reflec-
tion. But he reckoned without the postman.
However, the lady is married now—bub nob to
Adolphus. The whole story is one of the funniest
I ever heard.
Tattersall’s races at Rand wick, on 2nd June,
appeared to be a hansom-cab function alleviated
by Gov. Duff in a carriage-full of top-hats only—
no ladies gracing the V.R. party. By the way.
His Ex. passing through the street is a singularly
chilly performance—nobody says, “ ’Ere’s the
Guv’nor!” and the vision of a silk hat flashing
official salutations to the multitude, whether the
crowd nods back or not, is a vanished spectacle at
present. The potentate who bowls smoothly at
an old-gentlemanly pace through the streets, look-
ing fixedly at his Jehu’s shoulder-blades, and
with his hat nailed on with tacks, apparently—
well, he may keep his head warm on a crisp day.
But he doesn’t dazzle the populace, nor does he
truly represent royalty—(which is always curving
its spine at its subjects)—by effacing himself into
the semblance of an ordinary citizen who has no
legal right to flourish his hat at persons to whom
he hasn’t been previously introduced.
Talking about vice-regality, I certainly find it
amusing to read in London society papers that at
a reception given by some ambassadress or other,
the list of “ fashionable beauties ” who were pre-
sent leads off with Lady Carington who, at recent
advices, was peroxided to the last curl of her
frizette and persistently pink-cheeked.
If “ Lily ” really is a beauty on her native
cobble-stones and no deception, ladies and gentle-
men, the secret of why she used to so amiably and
unerringly snub a certain genuinely good-looking
Sydney woman is fully explained—beauty doesn’t
like a successor near the throne, especially one who
doesn’t paint her ears pink. And all this tittle-
tattle about good looks reminds you that the hus-
band, perhaps, of the handsomest woman in
N.S. W. is a son of the plainest!
But being handsome isn’t everything nowadays.
For one thing, it doesn’t mean a rush of excited
jeunesse in the wake of the idoless, this
side of the line at any rate. The moon of Austra-
lian beauty has its male satellites certainly, but
they take all sorts of care that their adoration
doesn’t leave them out of pocket when it’s all
over. At a recent Southern charity-ball (to give
an example of what I mean), a very pretty matron
got up a fancy dance, in which a posse of youths
and maidens of her acquaintance took part. But
rounding up the men was the hardest part of the
drafting. The number of sprained ankles and
weak knees that suddenly broke out among her
worshippers must have suggested to the pretty
matron aforesaid that it wouldn’t be safe to trot
any of her slaves down-hill without consulting a
vet., and as for expecting them to dance without
crutches ! Nevertheless, a marvellous cure
took place when the lady gently insinuated that
the necessary pasteboard would be supplied gratis
by her to all taking part in the dawnce; and when
the ball came off, not one of the set of johnnies
was missing ! It strikes me that the youth who
joins in a dance on condition that the organizeress
pays his footing for him, is in a straight line with
Tommy Atkins, who charges the British slavey a
shilling an|hour for walking her out.
The Mayor of Auckland and the Mayor of
Onehunga (Mrs. Yates) danced together at St.
George’s Rowing Club ball, in Auckland last
week. The first partnership of the kind in
British dominions ! On the evening in question
the lady mayor was presented to the Governor,
who tendered his congratulations to Mrs. Yates —
who, when all is said and done, seems to have
filled with much success a position which the
Onehunga men have lost no opportunity of making
difficult.
A certain Arcadian retreat of vice-royalty pos-
sesses (or did until lately possess) a servants’ club —
a highly respectable institution, which occasion-
ally breaks (or broke) forth into festivities parody-
ing the doings of the Hite. When his Excellency’s
orderly appeared the music burst forth wildly into
“ God Save the Queen” to give tone to the pro-
oeedings, and I believe that her Excellency’s maid
is or was invariably received with a “bokay.”
They had a ball at the Servants’ Club nob an
age back, but it will be long before relations will
become unstrained in one or two drawing-rooms in
consequence. The story goes that certain
“masters,” in their capacity of guardians of the
household and encourager of innocent amusements
among their retainers, called at the hall to make,
sure that the pretty housemaid was provided with
a proper escort home. Mine, &c.
In point of feminine interest, the most thril-
ling divorce-case on record is Russell v. Russell,
which had been entered on at San Francisco just
before the departure of last mail. Plaintiff was
the husband, who, in no small degree, rested his
case on his wife’s alleged cruelty, which is
itemized, and in one particular specified thus (I
quote the Argonaut) :
Bleached or artificially-coloured hair is easily distin-
guished as such, and does not appear natural, nor does it
deceive any person, but it is perfectly patent and notice-
ably conspicuous. It is regarded by the majority of
right-thinking persons as an indication of a loose, disso-
lute, and wanton disposition, and is regarded as, and
commonly held to be, a practice never affected by modest,
pure, and respectable women.
After expressing this general view of the practice
of women who bleach their hair, Mr. Russell
takes up its particular application to him. In his
document he avers :
Mrs. Russell is a brunette naturally. Her hair is of a
chestnut-brown colour, which, in its normal state, is
modest and becoming, and harmonizes with the natural
colour of her skin and eyes. Since we married she has,
against my wishes and protests, and with intent to vex,
annoy, exasperate, and shame me, dyed her hair and
changed its shade toaconspicuousandshowy straw oroanary
colour. As a consequence of this artificial colouring, she
has been obliged to paint her face to secure an artificial
complexion in keeping with the artificial colour of her
hair. The combination has given her a giddy, fast, and
sporty appearance.
This, Mr. Russell deposes, constitutes cruelty,
inasmuch as the bleaching “ greatly mortified,
shamed, and humiliated him, and caused him
great and grievous mental suffering.” The de-
cision of this point, remarks the Argonaut,
will be a most interesting one. If the
court holds with Mr. Russell and decides that
bleached hair gives a “ giddy, fast and sporty
appearance,” and thereby constitutes cruelty,
will not the court find itself in an embarrassing
position? There are other devices besides the
bleaching of hair which women employ to add to
their attractiveness in the eyes of men. Paints,
powders and plumpers, false hips and false hair-
all of these and many other mysterious things
might be brought under judicial notice. If it is
“ cruel” to a husband for a wife to bleach her hair,
is it cruel for her to wear a false bang ? If she is
semi-bald, is it cruelty to him for her to wear a
wig? And if it is not “cruelty” for a woman to
bleach her hair, are not the terms “ bleached
blonde,” “soda-water hair,” and “chemical
blonde,” when applied to her, actionable as
libelous ? Also, is a woman’s hair her own pro-
perty or her husband’s ? And if she is unmarried,
does her hair belong to her guardian ?
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.
Melbourne Confidences.
Melbourne, June 5, 1894.
Queen’s Birthday, though decidedly damp, saw
its usual programme pursued to the bitter end.
The review was to have been politely abandoned
if the weather had given any valid excuse, and I
believe the prayer that it would pour cats and
dogs was going up all morning from the Big House.
Nevertheless, the clouds rolled up.
His Excellency abhors dampness, and at the
first rain-drop upon his scarlet and gold he
wrapped a long, blue, martial cloak around him.
’Tis said, a local chemist has a substantial source
of revenue in vice-regal custom, apart from the
items of vaseline and cold cream. The chieftain,
in spite of a whole hour’s massage every morning,
frequently lies low with a stunning fit of indi-
gestion.
Which reminds me that some of the toniest
guests at a fashionable wedding on Queen’s Birth-
day eve, complained bitterly that, owing to exces-
sive overnight hilarity, the expensive marriage-
feast was as tasteless as bran-mash.
To return to the review, Lady Hopetoun, in a
tall hat and habit, much longer than has been
worn of late, a basque jacket and white vest, rode
ignominiously at the fag end of the military staff.
Some people thought she looked lonely with no
other skirts to keep her company, but I don’t
fancy the exclusive matron felt her solitude very
keenly. She would fain be alone in many things.
Once, at a vice-regal dinner-party, the woman
guests suffered a deal of torture through her in-
considerate behaviour. The “ both sexes ” part
of the feast was nearly over, but as the .hostess,
apparently, hadn’t begun putting on her gloves
preparatory to a drawing-room move, the unsus-
pecting sisterhood thought there was no hurry.
Suddenly the Countess rose, and they saw, to
their horror, that she had surreptitiously slipped
on a pair of silk gloves under the table. Though
secretly fuming they gathered up their paw-pro-
tectors, bangles, &c., as best they could and
followed her from the apartment looking, as they
said afterwards, like a lot of bare-handed washer-
women. It was no better in the drawing-room,
where they had to struggle into their raany-
buttoned kids, while their hostess, in her loose
silk gloves, gazed critically upon the scene. Ever
since, when any one of that batch of women dines
at Govt. House, she wears gloves a size too large
and sneaks them on ere the Countess has added
the final to her meal.
The arrangements for would-be spectators of the
review were on a strictly military system. To
each sentry-guarded acre (more or less) of barrier
was an admittance-wicket, so narrow as to recall a
certain Bananaland cave-entrance, known to the
vulgar as “ Fat Man’s Agony. ”
A visitor, chancing on one of these few and far
between posterns, was passed on to the next, until
late in the afternoon, if sufficiently persevering, he
arrived at a gate inscribed with the same letter as
his card. Some of the sentries, being good-
natured, winked hard if anyone tried to shorten
this procedure by a short out through the barrier.
But red-faced “superior officers” invariably
rode up on snorting steeds, and made such a war-
like to-do that one was glad to retire under mute
protest. In fact, “ the reserve” was a snare and a
delusion, and non-ticket-holders, who didn’t waste
time hunting a myth, came off best.
The Grammar School cadets led the show by
“ trooping the colours,” which solemn ceremony,
performed to the strains of their bands, looked
like a dancing-class practising the first step of the
minuet—“ Brandish the leg well from the hip,” as
Mons. Light-toe always says. The Oft. 4in.
drum-major, executing wild hkton antics, evidently
laid himself out for theatrical effect, and was
popularly hailed as the funny man of the circus.
Just such another is the black-gowned Cathedral
verger, whose duty it is to escort the preacher to
and from the pulpit, carrying for purposes of de-
fence en route a quaint trident, which looks as if
its proper place were a Xmas pantomime, where
made red-hot and applied to a policeman’s ana-
tomy by the clown, it might win a smile from the
Uas6 cherubs of the circle.
In the march-past the horses trotted well to a
lively Highland sohottisohe, though some of their
riders were too practical to make good soldiers.
Instead of sitting tight in the saddles, they rise in
the stirrups in purely civilian style, contrary to
all the rules of “military trot,” The culprits
were generally stout ancients, who perhaps begin
to weary of waiting for the foe, and have lost
their youthful ardour for soldierlike appearance.
Everyone still chants the praises of Belle Cole
and party, who were so delightfully American
don’t you know. This undying popularity may
be traced to their Salon visit, passed over at the
time as unimportant.
On that occasion, Belle and her companions
didn’t say much, but they beamed wholesale. A
doctor’s wife, the hostess for the day, had arranged
a throne of chairs for the distinguished visitors
only to find at the critical moment that the Holy
of Holies had been “ jumped ” by a posse of
maidens, who chattered away in possession as if
the whole shivoo were a private tongue-tourna-
ment. As soon as more seats arrived, the lum-
inaries were welcomed to the Salon, and presented
with its honorary membership on white satin.
The feature of the afternoon’s entertainment
was a young girl’s clever violin solo. Newbury,
conscious that everyone was thirsting for his
opinion, jerked out, at intervals, “Clever!”—
“ Clever!”—“ Clever!” His bump of self-esteem
seemed to trouble him rather that day.
Conspicuous among the throng wasj,the jimp
Melb. girl whose stage career promises so well.
It’s a mistake for an actress, especially a young
and pretty one, to go in for daylight “ make up ”
off the boards, though it may be all very well for
people like the Irish lady in the song, who “put
the pouther on, in case the bhoys should say that
she was schwarthy!”
Good folks differ so widely nowadays, that one
doesn’t know how to be orthodox, especially on
the Sunday question. I hear even of complaints
that the judges of our courts are not blameless.
One is led away by “pleasant Sunday ” pastimes;
a second, on his own showing, devotes the “ Day
of Rest ”to Speight v. Syme. Another, while on
circuit sometime back, spent a good deal of his
Sawbath in practising the Barn Dance on the
bridge of a Murray steamer, aided and abetted by
a young lady passenger. Talking of the Murray,
the Mildura Water Commission (rather a mis-
nomer, by the way) which travelled up the river
lately, had a particularly festive Sunday en route.
A Mildura news-organ remarks, that the week of
the Commissioners’ visit will be remembered as a
“ wet ” one for the settlers, “ most of whom (the
paragraph goes on to say) will drink with im-
punity, or any other man that asks them, as A,
Ward says.”
“ A Life of Pleasure” positively scorched some
Westralian friends of mine who ventured into the
Princess’ the other night. Among the party was
a tall and handsome engaged couple both belong-
ing to “old families ” of the West, and neither of
whom had ever left home before. The interesting
pair sat together in front, their married friends,
almost equally unsophisticated, chaperoning from
the rear. As the temperature of the play went
higher and higher, the women began to writhe
like so many Cranmers at the stake, and though
the men tried to look as if that sort of thing was
quite usual in Perth, the perspiration stood in
beads upon their brows. When the agony con-
cluded and these artless strangers were able to
cool their feelings in the outer darkness, the
betrothed damsel vowed in a stage whisper that
next time she went to a Melb. theatre she’d have
no male friends to witness her confusion. Modesty
is very much a matter of circumstances, after all.
The Westralian vice-regal couple still prefer to
hunt singly. When “His Ex.” is in London
getting a song published and so forth, especially
so forth, her Ladyship fills his shoes at Perth.
When the flag flies once more over Government
House, the devoted wife resigns her responsibili-
ties and seeks rest and change of air at “ Govern-
ment Cottage ” (Rottnest). Local gentility
complains now that a young person need only
warble “Remember me no more” tunefully,
within hearing of its volatile composer, to be at
once hoisted into the highest circles and put upon
the same footing as “ birth.” They are sticklers
for birth in that one-horse region.
I hear that one of the first women to appear in
the Far Western Golconda went there from Haw-
thorn (Melb.) as the bride of a pioneer store-
keeper. Her papa, formerly a policeman in
Westralia, became a martyr to the hope that his
gal would marry a real gentleman, with which end
in view he came to Victoria, and started afresh in
a suburban villa. After all, the young woman,
wiser in her generation than the paternal con-
stable, fell back on an old sweetheart, and now
she watches him rake in goldfield profits and
famine prices, with the cheering consciousness
that he is qualifying for the position of “gentle-
man ” at the rate of .£2OO weekly.
Young Groom, Queensland barrister, formerly
of Ormond College, Melb., and son of ex-Speaker
Groom, of Queensland, is engaged to a Miss Bell,
of South Melb.
Charity balls, plentiful as blackberries just
now, are sometimes engiheered so as to provide
cheap, and, at the same time, select, amusement for
“reconstructed ” notables. The promoters of a
recent dance of this kind blackballed all ticket-
applicants not known to the committee. “Really,
my deah fellah ! ” said one of the latter, “could
we admit Tom, Dick, and Harry to a superior ball
like this, for four shillings?” He evidently for-
got that a .£ls hall had been granted free, to raise
as much as possible for a charitable object; not
merely for the economic entertainment of himself
and cronies.
Nearly all the men wore uniform at the Birth-
day ball, and more than one girl left with the
scratched cheek that testifies to the epauletted
partner. An ingenuous generalisation, you will
say. There was grumbling, too, because swords
weren’t removed at the end of the first dance.
Professional militaires can, as a rule, manage
these murderous superfluities with safety, but the
amateurs, whose cold steel wobbles between their
knees at critical moments, are perils to be dis-
creetly avoided. Any woman who has been mixed
up in a ball-room accident of this kind trembles
in the custody of a whirling warrior for the rest
of her dancing days. A grass-widowed fire-eater
figured as one of the most “ dangerous ” creatures
in the whole display, and 1 heard him described as
“a sweet man” by several spinsters who
forgot that there was a wife in the case.
Lady Hopetoun looked handsome in yellow satin
and diamonds, and the Chief Justice-ess had the
felicity of sporting the same colour, trimmed with
black velvet, black roses and a large display of
diamonds also.
Out of compliment to Premier Patterson’s new-
made glory, perhaps, the sun was good enough to
shine upon the opening of Parliament, and the
Governor’s old-time chariot had a fine day out.
