As future pro-consuls are to be “retrenched,”
State-coaches can’t be reckoned upon for the
future, hence we gloat over the Hopetoun specimen
while we may. It was trimmed with blue and
yellow ribbons as of yore. As for the fab coach-
man, footmen and postillions, in powdered hair
and flesh-coloured stockings, they had, seemingly,
all been invested with a touch of rouge to give an
effect of “old English” ruddiness. His Ex.’s
uniform appeared dazzling enough until compared
with the raiment of M. Poutiata, the Russian
Consul, when it degenerated into a mere carica-
ture of pomp and bewitched us no more. Lady H.,
who drove up to the function with Secretary
Ralston and little Lord Hope, was in black with
dashes of blue and a black hat and feathers.
Inside the House every nook was packed to
overflowing, so that if you weren’t on the spot
ever so early you got no farther than the steps.
The struggle was too much for yours truly, and I
ultimately retired with a wounded spirit. Con-
solation came afterwards in the form of Mr.
Zeal’s afternoon tea. This was also a mad rush,
but the average woman is a hero where tea is con-
cerned, and very few candidates went away with a
dry throat. One fair swiller followed up her
success by putting the spoon in her pocket.
Dame Madden’s violet velvet was one of the
handsomet gowns to be seen, and her daughter’s
fawn-corded silk, its velvet shoulder capes lined
with pink, was almost equally effective.
Van M.
SUNDRY SHOWS.
SYDNES SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Her Majesty’s .. “The Flying Scud.”
Lyceum “ The Cabinet Minister.
, MELBOURNE SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Princess's “Vicar of Bray.”
Theatre Royal “ True as Steel.
Bijou ... Montague-Turner Opera Co.
Alexandra' “ Bucephaleon.”
Alhambra “A Parlour Match.
Pinero’s “ Cabinet Minister,” which put in
a somewhat confused appearance at Sydney
Lyceum on Saturday, is not an unmixed success
by a long, long way. There are too many charac-
ters in it, and an effort has been made to crowd
three distinct farces into one, with the result that
all three have been considerably squashed. Alto-
gether, it is a good comedy badly spoilt. It
wants sequence, and doesn’t hang together
artistically. Also, it is one long-continued insult
to Jews and Scotchmen, and at every interval
Celts and Hebrews held impromptu indignation
meetings on the pavement outside, and cursed it
by the bagpipes, and the Talmud, and the brose,
and the Urim and Thummim, and whatever else
came handy.
*• , •
The principal narrative concerns the em-
barrassed wife of a Cabinet Minister, who secretly
borrows money off a small Hebraic cad, and, being
thereafter haunted and.driven mad by him and his
dreadful sister, tries to repair her fallen fortunes
by marrying her daughter to a huge, red; freckled
and comatose Highlander whose hairy knees are a
blot on the 'smiling landscape. The rest is all
promiscuous confusion and kilts. Even the small
Hebrew gets into kilts, and tries to tell sporting
stories, in the midst of which his gun goes off and
shoots the morose Celt somewhere in the petticoat.
Another episode which stands out of the general
chaos is the enthusiastic shaking of the Jew, in
his brief skirt, by Mrs. Brough, but otherwise
there is little that can be got hold off. The
characters are mostly frantic caricatures, and at
least six of them are altogether superfluous; the
dialogue is ineffective in many places ; and the
action mostly resembles a skurry of wooden
ghosts through a high-class furniture emporium.
Most of the persons concerned are reasonably
suspected of lunacy, an exception being made in
favour of the Scotchman who seems only to have
anchylosis all over the body and to have been born
asleep.
• * *
The company tackles the comedy with an
evident appreciation of the difficulties of the
position. Titheradge’s minister is an ancient
and sketchy dodderer who is weighed down
by circumstances ; and Brough’s freckled
Highlander is damaged by the worst Scotch
accent on record. Mrs. Brough confesses her
sins to Titheradge in a prostrate attitude
while beating the palms of her hands on
the carpet, and otherwise conducts herself
wildly, though her performance in her calmer
moments is excellent. But her part is one which
can’t be played upon any recognised principles
any more than a earn'd can go down-hill with a
jug of water loose on its back and not spill the
contents. Among the leading characters, the
only one which runs on straight and intelligible
lines is Boueicault’s Jew in kilts, and Dion G.
evidently lays himself out to make of him the
most memorable little cad on record. He
succeeds so well that Israel gnashed its teeth
at him from the circle, and Israel’s pretty
daughters, with the unredeemed jewellery on
their heaving bosoms, viewed him with evi-
dent disfavour. Miss Romer, with another Scotch
accent in only moderate repair, appears to advan-
tage in a bony character which is a further insult
to the Caledonian contingent. There is only one
altogether bright and restful spot in the whole
uneasy production, and that is occupied by Miss
Noble, as daughter Imogen, This pretty little
lady forms a pleasant background to the heaving
chaos of the show, and when she is violently
shaken in the last act it creates a general senti-
ment of indignation. If all the rest of the comedy
were shaken instead it would be more in keeping
with poetic justice—if there is such a thing. A
baby which is dragged in for no good_ or coherent
reason is hereby dismissed as too insignificant for
execration.
* * »
“Flying Scud,” a drama which has at-
tained to a green and not much respected old age,
was put on last Saturday at Sydney Majesty’s,
under circumstances which showed it at its best.
On its own merits it is a sufficiently respectable
drama, but it has been a resource for so many
battered scratch companies, and has been played
in so many dingy barns with sackcloths and dish-
cloths for scenery, apd so many spavined and
fiddle-headed cab-horses have feebly professed to
be the famous racer, that it has fallen into un-
deserved disrepute. The present version at Her
Majesty’s does a lot to rehabilitate the misused
production. It is perfect in every mechanical
detail, and is run by a good company for all it is
worth. Therefore, the old piece looks as if it had
solemnly sworn off the husks and the swine and
started a reformed existence. Mo Davis, in the
person of Albert Marsh, is a reasonably clean and
persuasive Jew, and one who might possibly make
a living on the turf, whereas the usual Mo is an
unclean horror with a glazed earthenware head.
Miss Emma Chambers is a plump and pleasing
stable boy, and Scot Inglis turns out _ Captain
Goodge as an affable and polished villain whom
it is a pleasure to meet. The rest of the co. i s
satisfactory, and the general aspect of things i s
altogether magnificent. Even the race-scene has
a possible look about it, which isn’t the charac-
teristic of stage race-scenes in general.
** * m
Miss Jennie Maynard, an English lady of
considerable repute, opens at Sydney School of
Arts next Saturday with a humorous entertain-
ment built on the lines of Corney Grain and the
famous Geo. Grossmith. The company includes
Miss Naylor, pianiste; Harry Fitzmaurice, a
baritone gentleman ; and Miss Clare Navette, an
English dancing lady who is alleged to be built
somewhat on the plans and specifications of the
lost, but not forgotten, Bartho; and the co.’s
printed programme looks all that can be desired.
** * *
Sydney Town Hall will be occupied on
June 28th by Miss Ada Bell’s “Flowerland”
show, a medley of art, music, botany and fashion.
Miss Bell is a lady artist who has pluck and
energy enough to run a big art exhibition entirely
off her own bat, and for that reason alone she
deserves to succeed. The programme announces
that she is under the patronage of the Empress
Frederick of Germany, Governors Duff and
Hopetoun, Mayor Manning and other notabili-
ties. The Empress Frederick, under these
conditions, is rather a long range dignitary, but
her name, at all events, looks profoundly impres-
sive. The show will be opened by Mayor Manning,
and the musical entertainment includes the names
of Kowalski, Harry Leston, Albert Marsh, Franz
Mauer, Rivers Allpress, Harry Nathan, Misses
Charbonnet-Kellermann, Colborne Baber, Lottie
Hyam and many others. Also, there is a most
imposing committee, an art union, and a lot of
other attractions.
*
After a brief season, which at one time
promised very well, the new Federal Minstrels
petered out dismally and suddenly. Two very
small child • artists belonging to this transient
show, Nelly Finlay and Fred Saunders, ought to
be heard of again on some more favourable oppor-
tunity. The rest of the co, were old and
hardened minstrels of the kind who used to make
a furore seven or eight years ago, but for whom
the demand seems now to have altogether died
away.
** * *
Mr. A. J. Daplyn’s lecture on “An Artists
Wanderings in Samoa,” given in Y.M.C. A. hall
last week, was worthy of a much larger audience
than turned up to hear his chatty reminiscences
of that distressful country. Amongst other things
he told those present that there were no poor
there; no beggars. Other travellers say the
Samoans are all beggars - they are always begging
for something. He testified to the good work
done by the missionaries, also to the amiability of
the Samoan girls, and the nasty habit the natives
have of carrying severed human heads around
the town after a fight. The description of the
preparation of kava by mastication didn’t seem to
arouse much enthusiasm, although Mr. Daplyn
said it wasn’t as bad as it looked, and the girls
who “chawed” were of the first families. Some
allusion was, of course, made to R. L. Stevenson.
We are tired of Stevensonolatry.
** * *
If the new wine needs new bottles,
Let the singers young and gay
Pour fresh music from their throttles
Into ballads of to-day ;
But what time the glory lingers
In their strains that sweetly flow,
We wou'd fain hear old-time singers
Trill the lays of long ago.
This appears to be popular sentiment (neatly put)
concerning the Montague-Turner treatment of
certain perennial operas. There, was a large crowd
at Melb. Bijou, on Saturday, to hear “Maritana”
sung by artists who have breathed its airs for
many years past, and who drop into their
accustomed places with the confidence of ever-
welcome friends. The principals were mostly
voice-weary from hasty rehearsal, yet they
did so well that a large percentage of “Mari-
tana” had to be repeated, and although the
drinking festival in Don Caesar’s dungeon was
even a drier function than usual, and the
Kins of Spain wore cheap trimmings and a
close-cropped head, and the chorus might have
dissembled its antiquity with another pound or
two of pigments—although these and other details
admitted of improvement—there was a general
feeling among the audience that “ Maritana 5 had
little to com plain of. Miss Montague, Miss Cecily
Staunton, Turner, Farley, and Gordon form a
strong backbone to a promising musical season at
the Bijou. On Wednesday they were to deal with
“Faust.” Also, the “ Bohemian Girl” may be
confidently expected at an early date. One can
safely reckon on the Gyurl.
** * *
“A Life of Pleasure” is wending its
naughty way to a brilliant finish at the Princess’s,
for after Friday night that notable spectacle must
be piled upon several lorries and taken away with
the rest of Bland Holt’s stock-in-trade. It has
proved a solid success, as times go, and the
memory of its indifferent morals and Burmese
bloodshed will not soon fade from Smellbourne s
heart. On Saturday the Comic Opera Company
return to re-open proceedings in “ The Vicar of
Bray ” Bracketed with this frisky Christian
will be “Penelope,” a one-act operetta new to
Australia. Special and solemn interest is attracted
to “ Penelope” by the statement that it will in-
clude six farewell appearances of Ryley, whose last
solo should bring the tear of woe to many a wist-
ful eye.
“ The Shamrock and the Rose ” is extend-
ing over a fortnight at Melbourne Royal. On
Saturday last Charles Holloway began to alter-
nate characters with W. J. Sweeney, therefore
the man who pays his money takes his choice
between witnessing Charles as a virtuous young
hero, or a comic old sinner. Each part seems to
suit him a little less than the other. Holloway
made his one thoroughly satisfactory effort years
back, in the character of a surly tramp, the
play was “The Naked Truth,” and George Dar-
rell, so some rascally liar avers, had got
himself so entangled in his own plot, that
a desperate expedient was needed to get him
clear again. At this juncture C.H. strolled
irrelevantly across the stage and knocked George
senseless with a soft brick. He had no other kind
of connection with the story ; he merely came for-
ward, heaved the brick, and went away for ever.
He performed this duty in a prompt, decisive
manner, and gave satisfaction to all. We wander
from the point, however. The bill for Saturday
next is “True as Steel.”
** * *
The Circus at Melb. Alexandra has pub up
a new programme, replete with daring achieve-
ments in the somersault line and startling horse-
feats. The lions, too, are groaning in a different
key, as though they hungered for a change of diet,
and would prefer a sirloin of Dan Fitzgerald, or a
corner off Brother Tom’s brisket. There is some
absurd hesitation in the matter of letting anyone
enter the den. Several tamers are waiting for the
job, and John Burns frets for human companion-
ship ; yet the circus will probably resume its tra-
vels without supplying this long-felt want. The
show is advertised to close on Saturday.
A popular concert contains 35 per cent,
of music and singing, and 65 per cent, of coming
upon the platform, leaving the platform, coming
back again to bow, departing for a second time
and finally “yielding to the inevitable encore.”
It is little wonder that theatre-goers as a body
fight shy of these harmony meetings.

Prof. Marshall Hall had reason for his
latest wild gibberings at Melbourne musical
criticism which so overrated Belle Cole and Co.
that it could not do comparative justice to the
IJrso-Sapio concerts, coming immediately after-
wards. The “critics ” sounded the whole gamut
of praise for massive Belle Cole and a repetition
of the same old adjectives created very little advt.
effect. It doesn’t say much for the “music-
loving section of the public ” that a daily news-
paper notice should be taken seriously, nor for
Mr. Hall that he should eternally rave upon such
an absurd subject. Besides, Hall himself gets
more slushy comment than he deserves.

At Poverty Point.
Roberts, the stage-manager and principal young man of
Maggie Moore’s co., was recently presented with a hand-
some gold watch by treasurer Jimmy Moore, in recogni-
tion of the very successful Melb. season. The ceremony
was performed in the privacy of Roberts’ dressing-room
at the Royal. On the same evening, it is said, a presenta-
tion of another sort was made to Johnny Forde, who has
travelled with the show this long time past, and always
played Stofel in “ Struck Oil.”
David L“e, the Melb. city organ-grinder, has long
nursed a grievance against Guenett, a music-teacher, who
writesnoticestor the Argus and revels in the verbiageof the
musical dictionary. Guenett passed unfavourable remarks
uoon David’s organ recitals, and then the mighty founder
of the Melb. Philharmonic Society took to “saying things”
about the other fellow when he passed him in the street,
At last David said something so extremely unpleasant
that Guenett, who is taller and younger and less fat,
knocked him prostrate in the gutter. Fined 10s.

Probably no play ever experienced greater difficulties
than the “Life of Pleasure ” on its second night at Melb.
Princess. The boat containing Henrietta Watson and a
waiter turned turtle as soon as she entered it, leaving her
to walk away on the surface of the water while the
menial was dragged off sitting on the bottom of the boat.
In the next act Bland Holt rode off with despatches to
the main body of the army, but was caught by the neck
with a portion of the jungle and had to return to dis-
entangle himself, but the following scene was the chef
d’oeuvre. The Maxim guns having mown down theDacoits
satisfactorily, the curtain fell to about eight feet from the
boards and then stopped. A carpenter ran on from the
wings and tried to haul it down, but instead it began to
haul him up, he dropped off and retired amid the howls
of the house. Then the rag fell properly while the dead
Daooits scrambled to get within its line. In the final
scene the only mishap was when Harrie Ireland banged at
a “looked ” door which opened wide before her thumps.

tions down South he made money and plenty of it, but it

awful woman, because she was about as thin as Sara Bern-
hardt, thought she must be about as good an actress ; so,

Bernhardtish roles. Result: unanimous absence of audi-

Spagnoletti: “On Sunday night I went to Melbourne
Alhambra and Gaiety. Large audiences. Frank Clark
ought to be a Presbyterian. He was round with a silvery
salver covered with a laced napkin and heaped up with
silver bits. ‘Don’t be cheeky,’ said Frank, when a
pittite touched this bland churchwarden on a tender
point. At the Gaiety the performance was just the same
as the first part on a week night, except jokes and danc-
ing. The ladies were similarly made-up, costumed,
wigged, and painted. If anything could make me a Sab-
batarian, this would.”
Dear Bulletin, —Talking about “ much-travelled ’’
people, there are plenty of them nowadays. I have done
a little in the line myself. I began 40 years ago and have
kept it up since. My first 20 years’ travel were as an
amateur Bohemian. My last as one. The
most part of the first was Heaven. A great part of the
last was hell—the law of compensation. I began by
walking, driving and railway through Great Britain-
then, time after time I crossed the Channel (from London)
till I had seen every capital in Europe. Then Asia
Minor, Egypt and along the North of Africa to Algiers.
Then the West Indies and North America. Then South
Africa, from Cape Town to the Limpopo, where I spent
three years “big game” hunting—Caffre trading and
gold digging ; then Madagascar, Mauritius, India, China,
Japan, and the “ Far East" generally another three years;
then Australia, every part of it except the “Never
Never”; Maoriland three times, New Caledonia, Fiji,
and “ the Islands." I don’t remember when I ate my
Xmas dinner in the same place two consecutive y ears. I
have made about 20 long sea-voyages, hundreds of short
ones, and innumerable journeys by road, river, and rail.
I have never been sea-sick, nor ever saw a rope blown
away, a skylight smashed in, or an accident of any kind.
How does that count ?— Duncan Macallum.
One of Dan Fitzgerald’s trick ponies at Melb. Alexandra
fell over into the orchestra the other night, and after
beating “ Home to our Mountains ” out of the big drum,
calmly trotted out of the stalls entrance, round through
the stage-door, and back into the ring. Dan says he
learned the trick in half-an-hour.

“The Bulletin’s” Missing Word
Competition, No. 14.
This competition closed with 6138 entries,
the pool being consequently <£3l6 18s. The miss-
ing word, “RIVEN,” was sent in by two com-
petitors only, who receive a dividend 0f_.£153 9s.
apiece. The principal run was upon “ disunited,”
“chaotic,” “split,” and “effete.” One competitor
varied the monotony by staking his shilling on
the hitherto unknown word “Bifed.” Another
sent in the equally original “Duple,” and two
more pinned their faith to “Secant’’and “No-
madic.” The names and addresses of the winners
are as follows: —
Jambs M'llveen, Inverell, N.S.W.
T. Scanlon, 60 Riley-street, Sydney, N.S.W.

will be found on the inside of the red cover at the
back of this issue.

The N.S.W. revenue tariff party must
fail at the polls and elsewhere while it has two
leaders. Reid is only its leader in name. Parkes
would be its leader in reality but for the fact that
the party is divided in itself. Parkes sees that
the revenue-tariff game is up, and feels his way
per medium of Mr. Jackal Wise, who talked of
fourpenoe in the £ land-tax, which is, later on,
diplomatically whittled down to a half-penny or
twopence or something or,other; but Reid and
M'Millan still follow the old banner of the calico
drummers.

“ IMITATION
is the

The articles most imitated are
CLEMENTS TONIC and
FLETCHER’S PILLS.
BECAUSE they have the best reputation and
are most in demand by the public, consequently
svery dishonest trader tries to trade on their fame
and renown.
It is really wonderful that the Australian’people
are so slow to see the truth as regards the unserup-
ulous quacks and charlatans who prey on the
credulity of the Australian public. Scarcely a
week ever passes but what the press exposes the
barefaced and shameful practices of some of these
harpies, yet, in face of all they seem to thrive, and
the paper who hounds them down most thrives
fattest on their advertisements and cracks charn-
pagne over each balance-sheet. Clements Tonic
and Jf letcher sPillshaveneveryetstooped to decep-
tion and misrepresentation. They are scientific
therapeutical agents, and evidence as to their
value can be found in every district, and such
being the case it is unnecessary to further extol
their virtues.

At the opening of Vic. Parliament last
week, Speaker Bent remembered, for the first
time in his life, that he must not sit down until
Victorias representative gave his per-
mission. On previous occasions he has seated
himself immediately after bobbing his head
which was gross sacrilege in the presence of so
many sycophants.

A Bulletin correspondent points out
that there are cases of Australian aborigines
living more than seven years in gaol. As an
instance, Jacky Whyman, who was to have been
imprisoned for life, but was released after serving
15 years. He afterwards died in Cobargo (N.S. W.)
district. His crime was the murder of Paddy
Haddigaddy, another blackfellow.
f( Somebody said to a Sheriff the other day :
Tell me, is it true that you give brandy to men
before they walk to the gallows?” “Well,”
replied the Sheriff, “I have seen it done in some
cases.” “How much do you give them?” “That
depends. Mostly a couple of ounces- just enough
to prevent fainting, and not enough to encourage
them to turn the drop into a hustings.”
Shiels is becoming an adept at double
entendre. Two neatly-turned phrases, one in re-
ference to Billies and the other to Princess May,
convulsed the Vie. Assembly, but they were not
reported. The Argus merely remarked that, “as
usual, Mr. Shiels availed himself of opportunities
to be coarse.” All the same, in one of the jeux
a esprit, the wit more than redeemed the lubricity.

in other words, that the knightship came as a
thunderbolt out of the blue. Now, does anyone

herself to being snubbed by a Higinbotham or
Francis refusing a knighthood—plumped on him ?
Taney Sir W. Trenwith thus insulting Her Maj.
or Sir J. Hancock—or (you can’t imagine this)
ex-slaughterman Patterson!
Historic houses of Melbourne would fur-
nish a book, Ihe great ones of the earth build
desolate places for themselves ” There are con-
sp ouous mansions, such as Macarthur’s, at
Heidelberg; Glass’s, at Flemington; Khull’s, Too-
ronga; O Shanassy’s, Tara. Shorn of all their
glories, they were built with the idea of founding
great families, as with Scott’s Abbotsford—and,
maybe, Shakespeare’s New Place.

A little time ago Bishop Saumarez Smith,
of Sydney, took a hand, in his own profoundly
impressive fashion, in a school cricket-match, and
made live. Later on he looked at the board, and
COTldn t see on it any allusion to his dignified
office. My boy, he said to the scorer, “ I think
you forgot to put down my score.” “Oh, no ”
cheerfully said the irreverent youth, “ that’s all
right, there it is Smith, 5.”’ Then the bishop
went sorrowfully away, *

As illustrative of the art of

how hopelessly a lawyer of their

When Gov. Daly ruled in
Adelaide, he vainly attempted to
persuade society to receive the
wife of a certain legal magnate,
and on one occasion when she was
invited to Government House the
high dames of Adelaide marched
out in a body with their noses in
the air. Because his better half
was suspected of leading the
Revolt of Woman” in this in-
stance, the second Speaker of the
S. A. Assembly, who has been in
Parliament almost without inter-
mission from ’57 to the present
Jay, goes through life without a
title. Premier Kingston seeks to
break down official prejudice in
this regard, but so far has been
unsuccessful.

The Tookey’s Flat man was James De Hirsch the
younger brother of the “ Baron.” He was at the
lhames,.and once kept a store, but sank a large
fortune in a chlorination process, under a German
expert, on the Moanataiari Creek. He married
in Maonland, but is now a widower, lives in
1 ans, and has one son. He was a dashing, devil-
may-care sort _of a fellow in those days-the
seventies.—H.O.

Victorian ; If we want to retrench, let the
harmless people out of Pentridge prison.

representatives (or a-quarter of the whole voting
power) in the Legislative Council.
Amongst “Democracy’s Devils” are the
numerous parsons who want to get into Parlia-
ment.
No man should be appointed to the
judicial bench of any country who is chronically
insolvent, and has been ca-sa’d by his vegetable-
Jobn.

in nothing so much as the emancipation of man
from woman,” says Schaeffle, the anti-socialist
author.

a s he looks.
A Sydney Evening News scribe; “ The
full force of the (electioneering) conflict is realised
by the journalist who goes forth on behalf of a
great newspaper." Great Scott!
The Tasmanian “army” turned out for
review on Queen Victoria’s Birthday. Total
strength of the four “ regiments,” 212—including
40 officers, 20 bandsmen, and seven buglers !

between MTlwraith and his colleagues are not
clear. They appear to be somewhat strained.”
Strange ! Even when strained they are not clear.
A Western U.S. paper announced recently
that a revolution had broken out in Maoriland.
“ Mr. Stout is leading the Government forces and
a man named Seddon is at the head of the rebels.”
Report saith that certain Melbourne
anarchists save carefully the Birthday-levee lists
of presentations, as being fairly reliable guides to
action in the event of riots or other possible
“upsets.”
Melbourne 'Herald : “It is as the
Sovereign, the great figure in the constitution, a
veritable bulwark, that the Queen stands out pre-
eminently from her predecessors.” And how true
it is, too !
The idea that women
are solid in favour of
liquor prohibition is going
to join a lot of other ex-
ploded delusions. Most
of the recent Cold Tea
victories jin Maoriland
seem to have been set
down to the female vote
on no sufficient evidence.
At Timafu a poll was
taken, arrangements
being made to distinguish
between the votes of the
two sexes, and the result
was that 531 women
voted for prohibition and
562 for drink. There-
fore, it is beginning to
be suspected that it is
really the working-man
vote which is bursting up
the liquor business, or at
least that one sex has as
much to do with it as the
other.
The other night
Parkes got off his chest
one of these aged com-
monplaces which sound
almost like wisdom
because of his hair, and
which would sound like
abject foolishness if de-
livered by a clean-shaven
man with bristles half-
an-inch long : “ Before
you abolish party govern-
ment,” said the ancient
sage, “you must abolish
human nature.” It is a
good old wheeze which
covers a multitude of
sins. But party govern-
ment is practically only
a thing of yesterday,
and human nature got on
somehow or other with-
out it for an seon or two
before it was invented,
and it will probably pull
through without it after
it is gone.
When last heard from Forrest & Co. held
1,300,000 acres of Westralian land. Now Premier
Forrest proposes to float a £1,300,000 loan.
Exactly £1 an acre. Somehow, John “ doesn t
believe in a land-tax.”
During 1893, that year of bitter economy,
N.S.W. got over £2,000,000 further in debt. In
a year of ordinary administration, without exces-
sive economy, the province gets £4,000,000
further in debt. That is all the difference.

versity consists not of buildings, but of men.”—
B. W. Griffiths, M.A., Sydney University .
A POLITICAL QUERY.
Would it matter a rap to the martyrs of Fox
If Jawbone were beaten by Adrian Knox?
Young Australia’s decided that bigots must go—
Now will Knox’s knockout be the opening blow ?
The establishment of the N.S.W. Protec-
tionist-party was one nail in the political coffin of
Parkes; the establishment of the Labour-party
was a clincher. Parkes could drive a pair pretty
well, but he could never learn to drive “ unicorn.”
Labouchere, of London Truth, states that
in three recent libel-actions he has lost £l5O in
damages and £6OOO in costs. The people who
won these actions spent their verdicts in costs
also, and were probably many hundreds out of
pocket as well.
Cardinal Moran points out that only in
Turkey, among European countries, is feloniously
wounding a capital offence. As Premier Dibbs
upholds Turkish law, the Tamworth electors will
have their choice between Turkey Dibbs and
Turkey Piddington.
A Legislative councillor asserts that no
Premier dare form a Cabinet in N.S. Wales
without submitting the names of the members
beforehand to his party in the Council, and main-
tains that there has been no Cabinet for over s*
decade even substantially as the Premier drafted
it.
There are savage dissensions in the N.S.
Wales Foreign-trade Parliamentary-party re the
proposed picnic to Opposition leader Reid.
M‘Millan, who is now almost as powerful, Parlia-
mentarily, as Parkes, is going to stick to Reid,
whereas the Wise contingent wish that G. H.
Reid aforesaid would depart for Heaven in a fiery
chariot.
Graham Berry steps down though not out.
Born with a lust for notoriety in his bones, it has
been his ruin—from a worldly view-point. A
sensible man hates to see his name in a newspaper
—he knows it impairs his genuine power. Had
Berry started life with this idea and his ability,
he would doubtless be rich, instead of worried
as to how his family is to be left.
“ The Labour Platform,” by Arthur Grif-
fith, published in the Workman Social Reform
series, can be recommended to men who think as
a valuable and suggestive exposition of current
democratic tenets, though the paragraph on the
nationalisation of mines is an example of shallow
reasoning, and the reference to British and other
navies an instance of reckless assertion.
HadParkes taken N. S. W. Assembly in hand
on Haynes’s adjournment motion rethe condemned
burglars, and pressed his convictions to a vote, it
is about certain that the Ministry would have
been defeated. In that event, it is supposed that
the House would have been prorogued, the men
would have been hanged, and an appeal to the
country made on the hanging as a test-question.

OF Liberty is the long name of a little paper

A pamphlet by “ A.J.C.,” printed a
Hobart Mercury Office, and entitled ‘‘The Caus e
of a Crisis,’’ gives a compact view of Elurscheim’s
theory of rent and interest, and is well worth
reading. The argument involves too many assump-
tious to have more than a theoretical value, but its
aim and ideal are high.

If bookmaker Kinnane insists on contest-
ing Randwick, Toby Barton may be beaten by
Mr. Storey. Mr. Kinnane’s claims to a seat in

that he has made money at laying the odds that

for a lot of doubles.
“ The Use of Camels Regulation Bill,” a
fantastic measure which deals another underhand
slap at the ship of the desert, couldn’t find one
supporter in N.S.W. Council. One member
agreed to introduce it as a matter of courtesy ; but
he was so ashamed of himself that he failed to
turn up, and denunciation was adjourned.
“The will of the people is seen in the
statute-book, and not in the excited cries of a
sympathetic multitude or an unscrupulous faction
of opponents,” says Premier Dibbs. The will of
the people isn’t necessarily seen in a twelve-year-
old statute-book, any more than a man’s present
sentiments are seen in the love-letter he wrote
ten years ago.
* ‘ The activity of Governments, with their
antiquated and merciless methods of punishment,
their galleys, prisons, gallows and guillotines, so
far below "the general plane of morality, tends
rather to lower the standard of morals than to
elevate it, and, therefore, rather to increase than
to lessen the number of criminals.” —Tolstoi, in
his latest hook.

Whatever technical interpretation may be
placed on the words defining the relation of
N.S.W. Governors towards prisoners capitally
sentenced, Gov. Duff is satisfied that no preroga-
tive rests with him, and a Minister lately said
that if a Governor attempted to arrogate the pre-
rogative of a few years ago the Government would
promptly cancel his instructions.
Alfred Deakin is said to have been angry
at the appointment of Gillies to the Agent-
Generalship in preference to the re-appointment of
Graham Berry. If this yarn contains a grain of
truth Deakin should at once quit politics for his
country’s good. The idea of sending Berry back
to London, where he is wrongfully supposed to be
implicated in the Mercantile Bank failure, was
always out of the question.
A Melb. newspaper correspondent suggests
that every church member be invited to subscribe
for the relief of distress, “ Is. per week or more,
according to the good fortune which God has
chosen to bestow on them.” The idea is good,
but the snuffle isn’t. The man who has a pile
seldom gets it from Above, but from Below. Good
fortune of the kind that is expressed in hard cash
mostly comes from the Other Fellow.
John Tighe Ryan obsequiously greasing
the axles of the Dibbs Ministry :
Instead, therefore, of wildly condemning the (N.S.W.)
Ministers, we should rather be thankful that we have a
strong Government which can be relied upon to put down
lawlessness and to discourage here such outbreaks of
scoundrelism as are possible under popular government
in America.
“ Popular government in America!” A sneer
truly worthy of a man whose name, if it means
anything, means that he comes front a country
which has for centuries been the spittoon of an
aristocratic caste. Mr. John Tighe Ryan is
apparently not at home among democratic institu-
tions, but he “ feels good ” amongst the Australian
ex-Republicans who have sold God for cocked-
hats.

Legislative Council, on May 26, sounds the death-
kned of Wm< Copley, ex-Minister for Lands in
j) oW ner’s Cabinet. Copley headed the poll id
1885 afJ a Liberal, but he got into bad company
aud swa ]l oW ed his political views for office. Hid
defeat is a big blow for the National Ass, which
spent much money to get him in. O’Loghlin,
re-elected at the head of the poll, is a consistent
Democrat, as is Bice, the second man. Jhe elec-
tion gives Kingston a Council majority, as against
a minority of two last year ; the Baker party will
consequently be far less chirpy in the ensuing
session.

The Scotch bankers who back up Henniker
Heaton’s penny-postage fad, in recommending a
plenipotentiary to treat with the Australias, name
Marquis Dufferin, Baron Oarington, and the noble
Randolph Churchill. Possibly “ Randy ” would
be the most acceptable of the trinity; but does not
Scotch “ canniness ” stick out in the limitation of
acceptables to the British Peerage? ’Cute men
study, not the strength, but the weakness of those
they deal with.
After repeated denials, it turns out that
two of the precious South African “heroes ” who
slaughtered the Matabele did steal the ransom
sent by Lobengula, and did suppress his offer to
surrender —thereby driving him and his wretched
followers to death. This gives point to the shriek
of the Queensland poetess, who yearned for Aus-
tralians to take part with the band of robbers
and murderers whose names have been duly in-
scribed on the scroll of England’s greatness.

Q. Registrar of Trades Unions issues some
suggestive figures dealing with Q. labour unions,
whose membership for 1891 was 16,542; for 1892,
3413; capital—end of ’9l, .£9632; end of ’92,
<£5545; net local income—lB9l, <£20,247; 1892,
<£5092. Payments, 1892, were £3968 for manage-
ment, |£1752 strike expenses, £916 unemployed
relief, £296 sick and accident relief, £154 funeral
expenses, &c., £207 excess of outside contribu-
tions, £509 written off. Between “management”
and strikes, the money soon goes.
The old Pulsford crowd of N.S.W.
Ereetraders announce, as their programme, the
abolition of all customs duties save those on
alcohol and tobacco. This will mean a loss
of something over £1,000,000 per annum. Then
they announce that they only propose to tax
land to the extent of Jd. in the £, which
will bring in, according to their own esti-
mate, £150,000. The discrepancy of £850.000
per annum simply means that the old Parkesian
“Freetrade” tariff would have to remain very
much as it used to be, and that there is no more
Freetrade to be had from Pulsford than there is
from Dibbs.
Contempt
for your ailments is simply committing suicide.
Every man is the centre of the universe to himself,
yet how many do we daily meet who complain of
weakness, liver complaint, neuralgia, and so on ?
Hundreds of such sufferers are met with every
week who neglect their cases when there is a
reliable remedy in Clements Tonic at hand. This
preparation is distinct from the many disguised
purgatives called indigestion cures, the use of
which are most harmful to the patient. Clements
Tonic does not give relief by drastic catharsis, but
aims at curing the cause of the disorder and then
the unpleasant symptoms cease. If you suffer,
remember that without treatment the disease
must increase, and that it is criminal on your part
to neglect your ease, at any rate your life depends
on your getting proper treatment and maybe the
lives of a wife and family. Therefore do not delay,
get Clements Tonic and do not be put off with
vile purgatives or substitutes, and you will meet
with the same success as Mrs. Elizabeth Luther-
borrow, North Rocks, Oarlingford, Parramatta,
N.S.W., who writes on December 26th, 1892:
Having found such wonderful benefit as I have
from the use of Clements Tonic I think it my duty
to forward you this letter so that you may publish
it, as it may be the means of getting some poor
sufferer like I was to try Clements Tonic. I had
suffered fearful pain for seveu years, caused by
poverty of blood and indigestion. The pains all
over ray body, especially at my joints and under
my heart, were So severe that I could not rest
day or night; I became so weak and debilitated
that I could only with the greatest difficulty attend
to my home. I spent pounds with doctors, but
daily grew worse. The agony I suffered after
eating even the smallest quantity of food was more
than I could bear. I saw Clements Tonic adver-
tised and determined to give it a trial. I have
taken seven bottles, and am quite a new woman,
all pain has entirely left me. I sleep well at night,
and can eat any food without feeling the least
inconvenience, and do my work without any
trouble.—l remain, yours, &c., Elizabeth Luther-
borrow, Norlh Ricks, Oarlingford. Parramatta,
New South Wales.

Society
Tli6 Soudan ass, which represents some-
thing like <£300,000 of legal cornstalk tender, is
now to be on the Colonial Sugar Co.’s
estate at Fiji, where he is having a lofty period of
it, eating, drinking, and being the father of mules.
If this story is true, then a <£300,000 asset has
been surreptitiously removed from N.S.W., and
entrusted to the care of a bloated corporation
and the security of the British bondholder has
been unlawfully reduced to that extent. The fact
that an asset of this size could be removed without
Parliamentary sanction is another evidence of the
horrible confusion of the public accounts; had the
donkey been properly audited at regular intervals
by the Government auditor this gigantic embezzle-
ment of mule would have been impossible. As it
is, the only thing remaining is to’ find out who
made away with the ass and sue that person for
the price of the lost animal. The N.S.W. State
ass was the most expensive, romantic and much-
travelled ass on earth, and its loss, in a national
sense, and altogether apart from the damage done
to the public credit, is irreparable. The money,
may, perhaps, be replaced, but the associations
which surrounded the lost animal can never be
restored.
* • ♦ *
Until the State pays as much for the
defence of a person accused of a capital crime as
it pays for his prosecution, it should be a point of
honour with the junior Bar to decline being
assigned to the job. Cases repeatedly come up in
which a Q.C. is fee’d at £3O or £5O, with an assistant
at half that, both of whom, with first-class solici-
tors’ aid, lay themselves out to convict and, con-
sequently, to hang an unfortunate suspect, while
the whole shield and buckler of that person’s
defence consist of, very often, the intellectual and
moral weakling of the court-table. Native modesty
should make these men decline; but the fact that
their opponents are paid thus handsomely while
they are offered next to nothing, should make
them on business grounds reject the appointment.
• • •
Melbourne Charity Organisation Society
takes credit for having prevented the waste of
much money on undeserving persons by the care-
ful enquiries it makes into the characters of people
seeking relief. Charity discovers a multitude of
sins.

Dear Bulletin, — Melbourne Metropolitan
Board of Works is seriously asked by the finance
committee to charge the men in its employ at
Werribee 2s. each a week rent for the sites on
which their tents are pitched. The proposal is
influentially supported. You are holding a mean-
man competition just now.— C. M‘Gee.
Pastor Fitchett recently
counselled the good young men of
Adelaide never, on any account,
to read Voltaire’s “Candide”—
the book which a lady read over
Charles Lamb’s shoulder one day,
and presently went away in a
hurry, “but whether the blush
(there was one) was the property
of the swain or the nymph,”Lamb
declined to say. The result was
that every copy in Adelaide was
bought, begged, borrowed or stolen
within twenty-four hours.

“ The Governor went out
for a ride yesterday,” Vide Vice-
regal news in the S.M. Herald
any day. Great are wigs and
fustian, and greater still is solemn
humbug. When will the grand-
motherly and chapel-keeping press
shake itself free from the bewigged
and phantasmal spectre of the
days of “ Royal George, our
King?” “ His Grace has sneezed,
how well ’twas done,” cries every
little bootlicker, while that
patient ass, the public, really
takes credit to itself for paying a
man from “ ’Ome” ,£7OOO a year,
and pickings, to inculcate deport-
ment d la Turveydrop on the
gasping descendants of the broad-
arrow.

Premier Patterson expresses
a belief that “ the general effect
of civil marriages amongst a cer-
tain class of people has been to
lessen in their eyes the sacredness
of the marriage tie.” The sensible
view is that the less a man spends
on his wedding the less likely he
is to repent it.
One result—or, at any rate, an incident
of the Speight v. Syme battle seems to have been
the dissolution of the old firm of Melb. solicitors
who managed defendant’s case. The recon-
structed firm hangs on to Syme’s dreary cause,
whilst the seceding partner starts a new business,
with a fixed determination to avoid colossal libel-
actions. It is said that the profession doesn’t get
any “ fat ” out of a case of the Speight v. Syme
kind. The leading barristers must miss briefs
which, in the aggregate, would yield more money
than the one big job. The attorneys have to
upply about ten shillings’-worth of labour for
every guinea they charge, and that is unproduc-
tive business—-for an attorney. The plaintiff and
defendant are both of them bound to be ruined
more or less, whichever way the verdict goes.
The junior counsel at the best only jog along com-
fortably, from the expensive legal point of view;
the witnesses consider themselves badly treated,
and the Judge, although his salary progresses as
umal, would gladly give anybody £lOO to settle
the accursed matter out of Court. Legal costs are
so sinfully exorbitant in ordinary cases that they
can t be fully maintained in extraordinary cases.”
* # #
In Melb. Divorce Court, the other day, a
petitioning wife said that nothing would please
her husband—“he even grumbled at the way his
white shirts were got up.” Judge Madden grew
pensive. Since the lady was unable to produce
one of those shirts in court it was hard to say
whether the husband deserved a thousand lashes
or a lot of sympathy. They might have been got
up with the collar on the bias.

Mr. Justice Stephen, who tried Mont-
gomery and Williams, executed last week at
Sydney, has some reason for not liking burglars.
It was in the grounds at his Honor’s residence
that the mysterious shooting of Constable Slater
took place a year or two ago. For this the police
had two men capitally arraigned, but the jury
didn t convict. The police were certain that they
had the right men, but all the same they hadn’t.
In this connection The Bulletin has a pretty
story to tell—some day.

Whether or not Australian women yearn
to wear trousers in public, there is no denying
that, in private, whenever opportunity affords,
they are eager to don them. At an occasional
dance amongst the female trainees of a certain
N.S. W. State institution, it is quite the recognised
thing for a number of them, in the absence of
proper partners, to disport themselves in the attire
forbidden by Scripture. The lament common in
“society,” anent the lack of dancing young men,
is unheard in the institution, as all the fair trainees
are only tod anxious to “ try ’em on ” and be. for
the nonce, the Man. A story current in the
institution concerns a trainee who had borrowed
a middy’s uniform for a special fancy-dress affair.
A MELBOURNE BELLE.
The clothes were uncomfortably tight-fitting, the
young lady was anything but slim, and, of course,
but one thing could happen. And, during some
violent calisthenics, 'it did happen ; and that girl
has never since been able to look that middy in
the face.

An ex-prison warder, writing in the Sun-
day Times, says that in Darlinghurst Prison R.O.
choir are several most tuneful convicts.
One woman in particular is remarkable for a good voice.
She is very seldom out of gaol, and on festival occasions,
if she is at liberty, will convict herself to get back again
so as to sing her solos, “O, Salutaris," &c. The men in
the yard, of other denominations, waiting their turn for
church, get as near as they can to listen to this woman’s
singing, such power has she.
“Better reign in hell than serve in Heaven.” If
this vain creature were always carefully muzzled
when “ in quod” she might be induced to earn an
honest living outside. Another gaol-item from
our esteemed contemporary is a suggestion that
the English system of using for executions not the
hangman’s knot, but a metal button fixed over the
jugular, should be adopted in Australia. Here
you see the cockney Mr. Evans, only half-a-
dozen years from England, instructing the
authorities of Botany Bay how to “scrag’’ people 1
This is a mad world.
* * »
Most of the basis of a current divorce-
action is the alleged fact that, though the injured
husband found no stranger on the premises when
he unexpectedly returned home at 2 a.m., he, while
groping in the dark for matches, accidentally put
his hand on a very hot pipe which the affectionate
stranger was smoking when he found it was time
to clear out.

S.A. Ministers are growing frugal. On
their inland trips of glorification they now make
the reporters who boom them pay for railway fare
and provisions and generally leave them to look
after themselves. A change from the good old
days of cheap cigars, champagne and chicken !
* • #
A Bulletin correspondent avers that at
Terowie, Broken Hill line, S.A., a poor fellow on
crutches got into the train with difficulty. No
sootier was he seated than an official entered the
carriage and insisted on the crutches being duly
way-billed and paid for, holding that crutches
were not “ passengers’ luggage.”

Amounts as low as ss. are offered by the
N.S.W. Education authorities as full recompense
to the victims of that recent disgusting accident,
whereby some school-children were precipitated,
holus bolus, into the school’s cess-pit. So far, the

for the bath.

A shocking instance of banquet grovel
occurred some weeks ago at Castlemaine. The
local horticultural society was filling itself with
refreshments under the patronage of Lord Hope-
toun, and the president rose to blow his little
local trumpet. This deplorable man said of the
Oastlemaine Horticultural Society that “it was a
very young society, but it had already blossomed
out into something very large indeed. Their first
show was opened by the Mayor of Oastlemaine.
the second by the Premier of Victoria, and now
this one was being opened by his Excellency,
•they would not be satisfied until a show was
opened some day by a Prince of the Blood-Royal.”
to associate horticulture with such an aspiration
as this is to tempt the wrath of Heaven, and to
assume that a rapid growth of importance within
one year is represented by the moral superiority
°i r ?, p^oun over Patterson is to incur the scowl
of J.8.P., one would think.

In Melbourne, recently, a deserted hus-
band proceeded against his young wife for steal-
ing his jewellery when she eloped with the other
fellow. Then some one dug out the curious fact
that a husband could only prosecute his wife for
theft with her consent, and the girl in the dock
was requested to “consent ” accordingly. Natur-
ally she wanted to know what she would gain
by it, but when she learned that her only recom-
pense would be any moral benefits which might
accrue through being sent to gaol, she intimated
that she wasn t on.’’ The Bulletin is waiting
now to hear from the lady who is on
* * *
That awkward passage in the N.S.W.
divorce-law which grants a judicial separation
where the husband has, during a period of five
years, been “ frequenUy convicted,’’ was settled
the other day by Judge Windeyer on a plain,
horse-sense basis. In a divorce-case it tran-
spired that the respondent had been twice con-
vioted, getting 18 months on the first occasion,
and six years on the second. Twice isn’t
frequently ” in any ordinary acceptation of the
term, but it was as frequentlyasthegentleman could
I . n fc be tune, so Mr. Windeyer ruled that
it filled the meaning of the Act. If he had been
sentenced to ten years the first time, that one con-
viction would also have been as many as he could
crowd into the specified period, and Mr. Windeyer
would probably have thereupon decided that once
also meant frequently. Under the circumstances,
it certainly ought to.

HE GOT IT ALL.
A QUESTION.
Breathe a whisper, Bishop Smith,
Breathe it o’er the waters blue;
Is she of your kin and kith,
Tres petite, or tall as you ?
Is she young or middle-aged,
Blonde or raven-tressed dame?
Bishop, if you be “engaged,”
Let us know the lady’s name.
May a bishop marry twice?
Doth he any rule offend
If he call a person nice
By a fonder name than “ friend ? ”
Lawful love is no disgrace,
Vide good apostle Paul.
Never heeding rank or place,
Cupid winks at one and all.
In a whisper, Bishop Smith,
Tell us—is the rumour right—
Is the lady but a myth
Or a living presence bright ?
Are you flitting like a moth
Round a pure and holy flame ?
May we bless your plighted troth ?
If so, whisper what’s her name.

DOMESTIC ECONOMY!
WHiVT IS IT ?
DOMESTIC ECONOMY is primarily the art of feeding and clothing the family at as low a rate as possible,
without impairing the family digestion, or taking away the character of its members for being well and com-
fortably dressed. Domestic economy is not comprised in buying a bad article for sixpence when better can be
got for sevenpence ; but if the ruling price for the best is eightpence, and somebody comes along and offers it for
sevenpence, then it’s domestic economy to close with the merchant so offering. By all means study this great
and glorious science of social life, because its due and proper observance cannot fail to make poor people rich, and
rich ones richer. What say you? Will you do your business at
ANTHONY HORDERN'S ?

may do your shopping, through the medium of the post, on the same terms and getting the
same goods at the same prices as if you lived in town.

M’Burney, Wallis & Co.’s Report.
The last fortnightly series of Sydney wool sales opened
on Wednesday, 30th May. The usual number of buyers
for this time of year being present.
Opening prices were about equal to last auctions. On
Thursday the sales were continued, but if any alteration
it was in favour of buyers.
We hear from London that the May series of sales closed
on Ist inst. Superior greasy merino beinsr firm and
medium and faulty merino about 5 per cent, under late
rates. Lambs’ wool and crossbred may also be quoted
from id. to Id. per pound cheaper. The quantity with-
held from sale was about 115,000 bales. American buyers
secured 5000 bales.
On Thursday we brought forward a fair-sized catalogue
for the time of year, but withheld a number of lots
sooner than sell at prices which might be looked upon by
owners as unsatisfactory. We always make a point of
protecting wool up to our valuations. Among the brands
offered were the following:— AO/B from Bathurst, H'l
and C from Forbes, DE/Q from Gilmore RIVERSIDE
JNM/DUBBO from Dubbo, SH/COOMA from Cooma, WG
from Mudgee, BALLY VO R/H/030 from Rockley, TM
from Gilgandra, JS/BURROWA from Burrowa.
Sydney.

History Repeats Itself!
“I know of no way of judging the future but
by the past.”
This Remark was made by the greatest of
American orators. His words were ringing, andl
what he said is true. Let us see :
The history of the lives of most men and
women shows that they have some happiness,,
some misery, and much disappointment.
It shows that the majority of men and women
break down during their lives, and that few, very
few, die of old age.
It shows that carelessness and neglect have'
been the causes of this breaking down, and that
care, watchfulness, and intelligence prevent it.
It shows that the chief cause of breaking down
is due to some form of kidney, liver, or bladder
trouble, and to the innumerable complaints com-
mon to women.
The history of this country for the past ten
years, however, shows that these troubles can be
and are being decieased, and that they can at alii
times be controlled.
It shows that the means by which this may be
done is within the reach of all, and that it is a
purely scientific preparation endorsed by the
doctors and used universally. In fact, no other
than Warner’s Safe Cure.
If you wish to judge the future in your own
life,
If you desire to part the veil which may
separate you from the great unknown before you.
If you are interested to know what your future
life may be, whether health or happiness, sickness
or misery, look at the past. You will see that
care and the use of the right remedy has kept, has
preserved, has sustained people, and that the
neglect of these things has brought sickness and
untimely death.
If you are a wise man or woman, you will
ponder over these things and
PROFIT BY THEM.

How to Get a Piano or Organ.
yOU, of course, want highest quality of tone, action,
touch and beauty of exterior you 'can afford; but
you must have durability. Durability is an indispensable
condition, and however cheap an instrument may appear,
without durability it is worthless. Avoid the cheap
pianos, for which there are agents in every town, who tell
—and often through ignorance believe—that they are
equal to the Steinway or the Erard or some other first-
class maker. Therefore, no matter how big a bargain you
think somebody else offers, investigate

day, that it is now an easy matter for a couple to
be divorced in N.S.W. by mutual consent and
without any scandal to speak of. The first act is
for the woman to leave her husband. Then he
applies for restitution of conjugal rights. She
refuses, on the strength of the great English
Jackson case where the court held that a wife’s
person was her own property and not that of her
husband. Whereupon the husband applies for a
divorce and gets it, no imputation being made on
either side.
« • •
N.S.W. soldiers received their last uni-
forms with the proviso that, if they resign or are
dismissed within twelve months, they will be
obliged to pay £7 into the military exchequer to
atone for the clothes made to fit. They are thus
practically held to good behaviour for twelve
months to the extent of that sum.

Dear Bulletin, —In last issue, in the argu-
ment that Gov. Hopetoun’s was something
of a Jewish feast you give the names of Messrs,
George, William, and Robert levers mixed up
with Levy, Lazarus, and Israel. Now, the
Messrs. levers are ultra-Irish and ultra-Catholio,
like many others of their compatriots who bear
such names as Levey, Moss, Allen, and Isaac.
Rev. Rabbi Blaubaum lately told the writer that
the Irish were the only people who never perse-
cuted the Jews. Perhaps the seeming interchange
of names is merely a mark of sympathy.—Mao
Isaacs.
• • •
Japanese labour is largely employed on the
Herbert (Q.). There are about 150 of these saffron
heathens employed on the different plantations,
under conditions somewhat novel to Queens-
landers. They work under the control of an
inspector from Japan, who speaks English, con-
veys orders, settles disputes, inflicts fines, &c.
Under the terms of the original agreement it is
stipulated that the men shall be provided with a
warm bath each evening, wherefore a tank heated by
steam-pipes is supplied to the Japanese quarters.
Just fancy the imported British labourer, or even
the native-born worker, insisting on such a luxury
at the close of his day’s toil!

Society is the Cabinet in the world’s Par-
liament. The women of the world sit behind the
Treasury benches, and the Church sits in the
Ministerial corner. In the opposition are a few
fanatics and an innumerable host of angels, but
the angels have no votes. —Ghanet Boyd.

had a bad time of it, for a brigade was formed to
follow them up, and pray in their ring.”

A N.S.W. back blocks correspondent:—
The local police are fertile of resource. Act 1.
A female drunk leading small dog by chain
attached to her wrist. Act 2. Arrested —won’t
walk. “Cop” proceeds to carry her on his
shoulder, the dog hanging down in front; pre-
sently he trips over said dog and falls—female
considerably damaged and now threatens legal
proceedings for compensation for injuries. This
could have been avoided if the “ cop ” had had his
revolver.

Dear Bulletin. —An old Scotch salt re-
cently entered a butcher’s shop on Thursday
Island, and called for lb. beefsteak. The
butcher duly served the order ; and, in the act of
giving change, ordered his shop-boy to throw out
some beef-bones. “ You might let me have a few
of those bones to make soup,” said the Scotchman.
“ All right,” replied the butcher; “ take as many
as you like.” The ancient mariner at once spread
out his pockethandkerchief and crammed the beef-
bones into it. Then, turning to the butcher:
“I’ll no be wanting the steak noo; if ye’ll just
hand me back the money, I’ll thank ye kindly.—
True Bill, Thursday Island.

An advertiser in a Sydney paper seeks
“ good looking ladies to act as private detectives.”
The advertiser may get some good-looking women,
but he won’t get any ladies. It is difficult enough
for a male detective to be a gentleman, for a
woman detective to be a lady is—well, it’s just
about impossible.

Dear Bulletin, —“ Tay Pay’s’’ “mur-
derous inequality of our sexual code ” (re Mrs.
Tanqueray) is cheap mouthing. The “ nature of
things ” is “ murderous ” all round, and what we
call happiness is only a more or less successful for-
getting of the fact. Besides, sauce for the goose
is not sauce for the gander ! A perfectly continent
woman is none the less a woman ; a perfectly con-
tinent man is a sub-feminine anomaly. The sexes
are not mere modifications of each other. Their
moral constitutions are so specifically different
that they must be judged, in sexual matters, by
specifically different standards, — Brute.
Bishop Cowie (Maoriland) ascribes the
decline of pulpit influence to the spread of educa-
MADAME MELBA, as Elizabeth in “Tannhauser "—her latest portrait.
tion. Which is a weighty and memorable truth.
The more education a man has the more doubtful
he feels about Jonah and similar fish-stories of
the ancient days.
# • •
An amusing absurdity was, the other day,
incorporated in a “monster procession” to cele-
brate the opening of a new N.S.W. line of railway.
It was intended to have a van of blacks in the
cortege to represent “The Past.” But a goodly
number of the inmates of the aboriginal van were
half-castes! Moreover the “ gins ” were attired
in the cast-off habiliments of white women—
crushed-strawberry predominating. Clay-dabs
were in use by several members of both sexes, and
not a nullah or boomerang forced itself upon the
eye of the observer.

Dear Bulletin, —The meanest man I ever
knew of lived in Maoriland. He was in love
with a doctor’s wife. During one of their stolen
meetings the lovers were disturbed, and in his
eagerness to get away he fell over a roadside
embankment and broke some ribs, and got badly
hurt generally. The doctor attended him—got
sued for careless treatment, and had to pay him
£475 damages. — Yours, Marcia.

FROM SEA.
(For Tub Bulletin.)
As one come home from sea.
Where storms and stress prevail,
1 came to thee.
Through rims of raging foam,
By sea-lashed headlands grim,
Still, slowly home.
‘The livelong night afar,
Where sea and skies combine,
I saw thy star.
The sea-spume filled my hair,
The salt winds stung my eyes,
But thou wert there.
The gulfs beneath my feet
Had no affright for me,
My life, my sweet.
These arms in mine entwined
Were round me then as now,
So soft and kind.
These eyes that knew no sleep—
A light to guide me home—
So calm and deep.
Not all the wrath of sea
Could keep thy love from me—
This kiss from thee.
Brisbane.
K.
A BONE TO PICK.
[FOR THE BULLTIN.]
She was girlish, poor, but handsome ; he was crabbed,
rich and old;
Troths were plighted at the altar, and he bought her
youth with gold.
Ancient story—in tfie background, younger lover, jilted—
poor;
Stolen meetings, tearful moments—they must part tho’
love endure.
Time rolled on, but Love it came not to poor Winter
linked with Spring;
E’en the birth of little Elsie left their paths still widen-
ing!
Angry words and bitter quarrels, separate lives and
separate rooms—
Elsie constant mediator—still domestic storm - cloud
looms.
Dinner: mutton—cold and nasty. Husband sits in frown-
ing state;
Wife is sulking, scorns to join him, sends poor Elsie for
her plate.
Husband carves a bone ; then chuckles, “ Of her sulks
she’ll soon get sick ;
Take that to your mother, Elsie, it’s a bone for her to
pick!”
Mother gets the sneering message—eyes flash fire, heart
burns with hate;
But her words come tripping sweetly, be her passion e’er
so great—
“ Take the plate back, Elsie, darling; take it back to
father, quick;
Tell him that he’s not your father —that’s a bone for him
to pick I”

The Wild Cat Column.
A depositor in the Freehold Investment 00.
raises a plaintive protest against the cost of liquidation.
Two gentlemen are browsing upon the assets of that
ghastly 00. at a salary of £B5O each, and the depositor
asks: “ Where could these two individuals command £l7
a week each if suddenly they were bereft of their snug
billets?” Where, indeed-unless they could get another
dead Daviesian “tentacle” to operate upon! But the
creditors of the Freehold Investment scandal, having
acted like sheep from the outset, deserve to be shorn to
the bitter end.
♦ • • •
The liquidation of “Sir Matthew’s” English
and Australian Mortgage Bank has condensed the whole
history of the land-boom into one brief narrative. Messrs.
Embling and Kay report that they lately inspected one of
the bank’s properties —“ a farm which was sold during
boom-time for the purpose of creating a suburb of Melb.,
some i 2 or Ik miles away. It was originally sold for £7
per acre, and was then rapidly transferred from syndicate
to syndicate at augmented prices until it came into the
hands of the E. and A.M. Bank at £7O. It was supposed
to be cut up into allotments, but this was never done, and
finally the liquidators sold it back at £7 per acre again to
the son, of the man who made the first profit upon it."
An enquiry into the constitution of these intermediate
syndicates, which made among them the bogus profit of
£63 per acre, would doubtless reveal the entire working
of what has been called “ land-boom madness.” ’Twas
madness with a delightfully simple method in it.
* * * •
The Commercial Bank of Australia forged notes
are imitations good enough to stand cursory inspection,
but if looked into the fraud is at once palpable. The
figure in the centre of the note is out of drawing and
execrably engraved, looking more like wood work than
steel.
• • • •
The Australian Mutual Provident Society’s
retrenchment this year comprises stoppage of all
over-time pay and the hitherto annual increases in
officials’ salaries. But no salary is to be lowered.

The deadly financial state of Victoria is shown
by the result of the attempted sale in small lots of W. J.
Clarke’s Green-hill estate, Lanoefleld district. After a
dozen lots had been offered the sale closed, only three
having been sold.
• • • •
Labour-member Cook’s Truck Bill, which has,
so far, had good luck, will, if the Council sends it on,
dynamite many important N.S.W. business firms. How
many banks have tellers, accountants and managers in
occupation of houses which have come to them under
mortgage or as security for overdrafts, a proportion of
the occupants’ salaries remaining with the banks as rent?
Several soft-goods and hard-goods Sydney firms maintain
similar arrangements with their employes, one firm in
particular owning between forty and fifty houses on these
terms.
• • • •
The animadversions of the court, on the
habit of lending money to minors, as in the case
of a young Victorian Civil servant, the other day,
are mere waste of breath. No matter what legal
enactments be passed against lending money to “in-
fants " the latter will get there just the same. Take
the English system, for example. A, who is 20, and
who is entitled to moneys on his coming of age, wishes to
borrow £lOO. He is introduced by a mutual (commission)
“ friend ”to financier B who says: “ You are, of course,
of age and wish an advance of £lOO for 12 months. My
terms are 7} per cent, and a bonus of £lOO. Sign this
declaration before a J.P. certifying that you are of age,
and that you owe me for value received, £207 10s., pay-
able this day 12 months, and I will give you my cheque."
A signs. If he disputes his p.n. he is “shopped” for
perjury. And this is only one way whereby the intention
of the Act is daily overridden.

You must not in any case, and particularly in this case

In circumstances like these creditors and contributories

* • • •
According to the mysterious workings of the
Queensland Bank Notes Act, any bank can deposit
£25,000 with the Treasury at Brisbane, and get in ex-
change £lOO,OOO worth of legal tender notes, the other
£75,000 being credited to the Government as a deposit. If
the bank goes bung, the Government deposit presumably
takes its chance with the rest. Just here a serious ques-
tion arises as to what the Q. Government does with the
small percentage which the bank pays in gold. If it gets
£25,000 from a bank and issues to that institution
£lOO,OOO in notes in exchange, and then deposits the
£25,000 in that bank in the usual course, it is obvious
that the bank can presently call round a second time with
exactly the same little pile of sovereigns and get another
£lOO,OOO worth of legal-tender paper on the strength of
them. Then it can come up a third time with the same
identical money, and so on ad infinitum. There is no
definite evidence on the subject, but this it apparently
one of the alarming possibilities of the situation.

The deposit receipts of the Commercial Bank-
ing 00. of Sydney sold last week for 19s. 10Jd. in the £,
and there are now buyers at that figure with no sellers
under 20s. So far as this bank is concerned, the “ restor-
ation of confidence” is evidently genuine and permanent.
B.N.Q. deposits have risen to 16s. 3d., with no sellers, but
the Q.N.B. remains stagnant with no offers above 13s.
• * • *
The latest book-agent’s dodge. He calls at a
house and says that one of his canvassers—since dismissed
—had previously obtained an order for the book. “Not
from anyone in this house,” says the householder.
Agent’s face becomes solemn—“ But he was paid com-
mission on your order. I fear he must have forged it."
Then, in a pained voice, he tells the householder that it
will be necessary for him (the agent) to prosecute the
canvasser, and that the householder will have to attend
court as witness. In nine cases out of ten, if the house-
holder is a woman, she takes the book to save further
worry, and the agent promises not to have the canvasser
arrested. “It is his first fall from honesty," etc., etc.
Then he smiles softly as he goes off in quest of another
victim.

Editor Bulletin, —Re illicit distillation. It is

• • » •
B.H. South 00. is in liquidation. This, the
shareholders are told, “in order to conserve the interests
®‘ a “ concerned,” having been rendered necessary
through the unexpected and abrupt termination of
financial arrangements which had been entered into, and
also the action of certain creditors.” Bluff has kept the
cm going for a long time past. When the Hancock sul-
phide jigs got to work, last year, the directors gravely
announced that the mine had commenced a new era of
This might have been, only—no one would buy
the product of the jigs. Meanwhile, a 6d. call. Next the
machine was shut down “ pending negotiations for a
permanent and profitable market,” or something of the
kind. It was promised that extensive exploratory and
developmental work underground would proceed while
the market was being found. Next the closing of the
mine, and finally liquidation. A series of steps towards
the inevitable, and directorial bluff all the time.
* * * *
Block 10 directors, confronted with the practical
exhaustion of the rich ore and the advent of the sulphide
stage, decided to keep the cash in hand, about 1)29,000,
for development, to obviate future calls. A section of the
shareholders wanted the money distributed in dividends
—and hang the future.
• • * ♦
At Block 10 half-yearly meeting in Melb. re-
cently, Manager Warren, who is “ sanguine ” that he can
treat sulphides profitably, asked for the erection of a big
concentrating plant on lines which he recommended.
The chairman thought it inadvisable to spend money on
sulphide plants “until they had discovered some way of
successfully treating the ore.” Which was to say that
the methods of Warren’s proposed plant and successful
treatment did not coincide. Block 10 people have had
enough of Warren’s sulphide plants for awhile.
* * * *
Block 14 half-yearly report to March 31 is
depressing reading. A loss of £6443 7s. Bd., added to
£3366 19s. 4d. written off for depreciation, transforms the
former p. and 1. account credit to a debit of £678 16s. 4d.
The co., at the end of half-year, possessed £27,331 11s.
2d. excess of liquid assets over liabilities, of which £16,297
9s. 2d. was cash and bonds. That- was two months ago,
and the position is probably much altered for the worse
since then.
♦ ♦ ♦ *
The fate of the South mine with Hancock’s jigs
is, by the way, not the best augury for the future of Block
14, which has just started wuth a similar plant.

It has been arranged in Melbourne that the next
gold-rush and boom is to be at Glen Wills, Vic., in the
spring. As usual, the Broken Hill crow'd and their camp-
followers have “ snavelled ” all the best ground near the
United Brokers Co., a 3oz. show well-opened-up.
From the sharebrokers’ inflated ads. in the
Argus, you can’t tell whether a man is a jobber for him-
self or a broker for the public. Very likely half the sales
are not for clients. By the way, the Argus’ monopolv of
such ads. has been blown up. The Age now advertises
brokers’ sales at 6d. per line. The system of thus quoting
•ales made outside the room is rotten.
• * * •
Including bye-products for the half-year, Broken
Hill Proprietary, last week, turned out 675,9130 z. of
silver, 1822 tons of lead, and 595 tons of copper—a total
value of £106,773.
* * • ♦
Some Gobar (N.S.W.) mines are doing well and
many more would prosper were they not locked up in the
hands of syndicates, the members iwhereof have traded
up shares to most absurd prices. A much-moneyed and
speculative expert who was quietly looking round there,
the other day, says there is hardly a show in the district
which hasn’t at least 13 or 14 owners, and to do business
with them is well-nigh impossible.
• • * •
Gough’s battery, at Wyalong, started this week
on 200 tons from Hassett and O’Bnan’s White reef. The
Barmedman battery total crushings to date are 7320 z.
from 592 tons. Recent heavy rain has left plenty of
water, but several new typhoid cases are reported. There
is a fresh find at Trungley, eight miles from Barmedman,
on the Temora road, with 200 men busy on three well-
defined reefs.
• • • •
At Pambula, Mt. Gahan put through 258 tons
lor 1230z5. smelted gold.
• • • •
No less than four batteries are going up at
Wyalong, one or two of them rattletraps. The best
feature of the field so far is that the miners have needed
no explosives.
• • • •
Vic. mining returns for last year show that
about 20 per cent, of alluvial miners in that province are
Chinamen. Where Chinese miners are employed along-
side whites they work for 50 per cent. less.
• * * *
In Victoria the average mining wages per week,
for 1893, were:—General manager, £4 10s.; legal
manager, £2 13s. 4d.; mine manager, £4; engineer,
£3 6s. Bd.; miners, £2 7s. fid.; surfacemen (labourers).
£2; boys, £1 55.; Chinamen, £1 10s.
• • • •
The latest, wildest whoop from Westralia comes
on the authority of a Tasmanian miner named Cunliff.
This person claims to have discovered a reef in which
there are “bands of solid gold, 4in, thick," said bands
being already proved to extend for a distance of 60ft.
The locality is kept strictly dark, but the proprietor has
refused £96.000 for the property, and sleeps on the claim
to prevent brigands coming round in the night and taking
out vast hunks of pure gold with a pick. Altogether the
tale sounds very much like Calvert’s romance concerning
that mountain of gold which he found, but was too tired
to peg out and secure.
♦. * * •
Coolgardie has had the luck to get nearly half*
an-inch of rain, just as a mammoth condenser to turn out
an infinite number of gallons of water daily had been
completed and puffed in the local Miner—one of the
neatest and best of Australian goldfield papers, by the
way. The rush continues to the new alluvial diggings
near the 1.0. U. claim. Bayley’s battery is in full work,
and Cosgrove s and the Golden Bar report rich stone.
The bicycle express from Coolgardie to the Cross gets
through in 30 hours.
