The United Coolgardie syndicate, of Sydney,
has beaten off the Clyde jumper (a smart Melbourneite.
one Levinsohn, who had a great deal to say at the recent
pro-jumping meetings) with the warden’s decision in
its favour. _ The Clyde is a ten-acre block through
which the rich Ritanita (next block) reef runs strongly,
and both the Ritanita and lease No. 59 (next to Clyde)
also belong to the United Coolgardie Co.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin, —Your correspondent, “Taipo ”
(April 28), argues from altogether false premises when he
refers to the rainfall at Coolgardie. Students of Austra-
lian meteorology know that the influence of the tropical
cyclonic belt ceases, as a rule, about the 26th parallel,
and that this belt reaches its most southerly extension
during Dec. and Jan. The cyclonic belt of the Southern
Ocean, which influences the rainfall of the Southern coast-
line, reaches its most northerly extension (the 30th
parallel) about June and July, and ic is to this belt, not
to the tropical one which affects Kimberley and the
Murchison, that the Coolgardie district owes its rainfall.
In July, ’93, heavy rain fell all over this field, filling the
big claypan at Hannan’s Find; this rain could not
have been less than 2jin., and had no connection what-
ever with the tropical storms to which “Taipo” refers.
When, as happened three times during April and
May, 92, the late southerly extension of the tropical
belt meets the premature extension ot the southern
belt, a general rainfall from the western coast to the
Q. border is the result. “Taipo’s” arguing about
Coolgardie rainfall on the strength of a Kimberley ex-
penence is as absurd as a Queenslander arguing Victoria
dry because the Queensland storms travel no further
south than Sydney. The fact of there being no large
watercourses in this district is used as an argument
against the existence of a rainy season here. But the
fu* eme ro ktenness of this forest country accounts, I
think, sufficiently for this. It would take more than two
inches of rain (and steady rains are characteristic of the
southern belt, as contrasted with the sharp, heavy,
tropical falls) to soak the country sufficiently to allow of
water running ; and it is noteworthy that wherever the
country is hardened by the existence of mineral belts,
watercourses ofjconsiderable size are found, in most cases
running towards lakes or claypans. And in what other
way could those claypans and salt lakes have been formed
than by the continual deposits of rain-water, evaporated
during the summer, and replenished during the winter
months? With regard to the chances of rain this year at
Coolgardie, the rains which fell here in Dec. and March,
which were doubtless the “tails” of some of “Taipo’s”
tropical storms, would point to the tropical belt being
lower than usual this year, and, in consequence, there is
a probability of the intervening anti-cyclonic “ mound ”
preventing the northerly extensions from the southern
belt until August or Sept.-Yours truly, D.L.D. Cool-
gardie, May 10,’94.
• • • *
Editor Bulletin, —Sir.—Having read in various
papers strange and awful accounts about Coolgardie, I
now light my pipe and endeavour to give you some idea
of R, from a man to whom Coolgardie owes nothing, and
who owes what little he possesses to Coolgardie. This
place is mostly composed of galvanised iron and gimlet-
wood houses. The climate is splendid, and, taking all
things into consideration, it is not a bad place to live in
for a field a little over a year old and so far away from
civilisation. There are whips of reef gold here, and those
who have been lucky enough to drop on to it live like
fighting-cocks. Most of the gentlemen who lived upon
the princely salary of 40s. a week, and whodined surnptu-
ously upon raspberry-jam sandwiches “over the other
side,” have turned down the bottom-tuck of their pants
and returned home again to abuse one of the finest reefing
fields in all of great Australia. I will allow that many
good miners with small means have also been disappointed;
but at the same time I contend that it would be just as
reasonable for a man who had planked up his last sov. at
Kandwick and lost it to be for ever squealing about his
bad luck, as it would be for him to return from here and
do likewise. It is all a game of chance, and everyone
cannot draw a prize. Why, if every man came here and
got as much gold as he wanted gold would become as
cheap as pumpkins on the Hawkesbury. Scores of unlucky
men leave the field almost daily and scores come up
to take their places. But the man who comes here
armed to the teeth with a burning-glass, tack-hammer
ar >d a pair of sugar-tongs, Is not the man who will explore
and determine the extent and richness of our auriferous
deposits ;he had much better stay at home. Nearly all
the stores do good trade, buildings are going up when-
ever the materials come to hand to erect them, and you
couldn’t throw up a brick between here and Bayley’s with-
out it falling on an auctioneer. Our daily occupation is
abusing the W.A. Parliament; of course, making a crust
when we can, and spearing stray mice with carving-forks.
When rain falls and water is struck, as it will be, in the
shafts all round, and sufficient time has elapsed to prove
the field, then it will be time enough for people to abuse
the place, or come here, as the dictates of common sense
may indicate. At present no one ought to come, and it is
downright insanity of many who keep coming, to arrive
here without the price of a feed and no interest in Cool-
gardie whatever.—Yours, Harry W. Cheesbrouqh.
Bayley-street, Coolgardie. May 15,1894.
• * * *
Editor Bulletin,—At Cue, capital of the Mur-
chison (W.A.) goldfields, a paternal Govt, allows the post-
master (not a regular official) ten pounds per annum or
some such liberal salary. The weekly mail now averages
one ton, and is constantly increasing. Consequence is (I
presume it is the consequence) that the mail takes two or
three days to sort, letters are often only delivered after
calling several times, and newspapers-well, the less said
about them the better. My experience is that out of six
Bulletins which ought to have reached me, only one has
come to hand. The other day a friend of mine posted a
letter addressed to himself, called several times, but has
not had it delivered yet. Complaints are general and
bitter. Again, all stamps have- to be paid for in advance
by the postmaster, the result being that the township is
often stampless. How different this from the early days
of Broken Hill I At the Four Mile, a rapidly-increasing
township, the P.O. has been placed in the hands of a firm
of Afghan storekeepers, although there are plenty of
European business people who would be only too glad of
the office.—Yours, &c., Miner, Four Mile. Cue W A
10th May, ’94. ’ *

The Ooolgardie country is certainly a moat un-
favourable place for moneyless working miners, but its
worth as a reefing-field (prima-facie established by the
success of Bayley’s), seems now assured. Anyhow, Cool-
gardie will soon know its own fate. Within a month or
two it will have the telegraph-wire; within eighteen
months it will have a railway from the coast, and it will
then be as easy to get there as it now is to get to Charters
Towers. Food will be as cheap, plenty of water will he
got by sinking, and labour for the development of the
mines will not cost more than 70s. a week, which will be
£1 more than Vic.’ current rates. At the present time,
lOdwt. mines are paying at Southern Cross. If there are
any big bodies of stone at Ooolgardie which will go loz.
(to say nothing of strong reefs, up to Bft. and 9ft., which
old and level-headed miners swear will run 30z.), they
will mean a pile every time.

According to a private letter received in Sydney,
mining is being pursued under difficulties in Matabele-
land. Three prospectors take with them ten men and
a Maxim gun to scare off the niggers. Rain falls,
on a monthly average, six days a week. At Fort Salis-
bury, the Chartered Co.’s headquarters in Mashonaland,
food prices, during the month of March, were, seemingly,
higher than at Ooolgardie. Milk, not procurable; eggs,
4s. to Bs. per doz.; butter, 4s. to ss. per lb. ; and potatoes,
3d. to 7d. per lb. During same month one Patrick Flynn
died up there. The price of “ murphies ” probably killed
him.
* * * *
The cheap steamer-fares to Maoriland have dis-
gusted the stewards, who say that not one in fifty
travellers is good for a tip, and that the manners of the
saloon passengers are “ perfeckly sickenin’.’’ And one
steamer-captain says that nine-tenths of those who now
travel in the saloon ought to be slung out into the steer-
age, and those in the steerage ought to be in gaol ;
another officer swears violently that the passenger-traffic
to and from Maoriland makes him yearn to get back
to the pig-carrying coastal trade of N.S.W. Who wouldn’t
be a tourist !

A Queenslander writes from Coolgardie to i
Northern paper : - “The sensational finds are not reported
correctly. A 15oz. slug is 30oz. in Perth, 60oz. in Ad<-
laide, 90oz. in Melbourne, 180 oz. in Sydney, and God
knows how much in Brisbane. I met one of the findeis
of a 17oz. slug and had a yarn with him. He showed me
a Dubbo paper, and his find was reported as 170 oz.”

From Oct., 1893, to April, 1894, the Brilliant
(Charters Towers) crushed 91,319 tons stone for 130,8680 z.
gold, valued at £460,555, and paid £214,370 in divs.
That very useful machine, known as the
National Cash Register, is now to be seen in
almost every mercantile establishment and leading
hotel in Australia. In conjunction with the
Smith Typewriter, Messrs. Fisher and Lingham,
sole agents for the Australian provinces, have suc-
ceeded in a short time in creating an extensive
business. They have recently opened branch
agencies at 432 Collins-street, Melbourne, and 177
Queen-street, Brisbane.
Some of Minister Slattery’s satellites are
giving it to the world that that politician stood
out strongly in N.S.W. Executive against the
hanging of Montgomery and Williams—which
hanging, we read in a S.M. Herald telegram,
was “ strongly approved of ” in Mr. Slattery’s
constituency, Burrowa. This sort of balancing
must be resented. If Mr. Slattery—who, as a
member of the Executive, is sworn to secrecy, and
must therefore be presumed to have made no
statement on the matter—felt strongly on the sub-
ject he should have resigned. As he didn’t resign,
he is just as accountable for the hanging as
Parkea (who didn’t resign on account of the
Treason-Felony Bill being adopted by the Cabinet
of the day) was for that infamous measure.
A pleasing novelty in cigars has lately
been introduced to the Sydney market by Messrs.
Heyde, Todman & Co. The product is from the
celebrated factory of Messrs. Spencer & Co.,
Madras, who are reputed to be the largest manu-
facturers of cigars in India. This branch of the
firm’s business has developed rapidly during
recent years, so much so that the name of Spencer
& Co. is familiar to almost every traveller
throughout the Indian Empire. At the last
exhibition of the Agri-Hortioultural Society of
Madras, held on the 24th Feb., they succeeded in
securing the highest awards for cheroots and the
best Dindigul and Lunkah tobacco. The
Victorian commissioners, who were recently dis-
patched to India for the purpose of opening up a
trade between the two countries, visited the
premises of the Messrs. Spencer and were very
much interested in what they saw. It is merely
a matter of time for these cigars to become
thoroughly popular with Australian smokers.
N.S.W. Premier Dibbs’ effort to make the
British Lion roar in connection with the Costa
Rica Packet case didn’t work. The phlegmatic
mynheers of the Hague weren’t scared, and flatly
refuse to pay more than £2500, and this only on
the understanding that they recognise no claim
but that of the unfortunate captain of the blubber-
hunting ship. Possibly had the demand been
more moderate a better result would have been
attained. The original claim was, in all, £25,000-
ten thou, each for the captain and Burns, Philp,
and Co., and five for the crew. As the case stands
at present, Captain Carpenter can either take his
£2500 or leave it, and the crew—who, although not
knocked about and ill-treated by the Netherlands-
India Dutchmen as Carpenter was, lost heavily
by the delay of the vessel—get nothing.
The poor devils of sailor men, who came home
to Sydney with nothing but a shirt and trousers
each and a firm belief in the hoary old fiction that
Britannia rules the waves and would see them
righted, are entirely ignored.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Manton, North Melbourne footballer, walked a
distance ot 160 mile* in order to be down in time for the
first match of his club. Esprit de corps like this is un-
common among Melb. players, who generally howl bitterly
if their train fares everywhere are not paid.
* • • •
No one can tell what was the matter with Abe
Willis in his fight with Hegarty. The referee inferred
that he didn’t try, and the sporting papers endorsed that
opinion; nevertheless, Willis toiled in the stoke-hole of
the Arrawatta coming over, and a man doesn’t do that
without meaning business. Neither he nor Hegarty oared
to take up the challenge of Jack M'Qowan, the sturdy
little footballer, who wanted to meet the winner.
* * * •
The boss of one of the public department# of
Somewhereia is also president of a big football team there,
hence when it came to his ears that one ot his employe*
was a crack junior he called that young man aside and
suggested the desirability of his playing for his (the boss •)
club. “ I can’t do that," replied the footballer, “ because
I’ve promised to play for the —,” naming the suburb
he lived in. “ All risrht 1 all right 1 You can go,’ said
the boss, and a week later there came to the youth of
kicks a notice transferring him to Mulgatown, a God-for-
saken waste of sand, 1000 miles “back.” But happily
these orders are liable to countermand, and, on the trans-
feree telling the boss how sorry he was that he oouldn t
stay to play for his club, he was allowed to remain in the
capital. Thus is the country made the oatspaw of the big
football teams.
« • • •
Since the English courts, some years ago,
decided lhat welshing was “larceny by artifice,” prac-
titioners return the stake deposited and cannot be
prosecuted for refusing to part with the money won.
Hence the names “balancer” and “balancing.”
♦ • • *
So far, the Maoriland champion pugs have been
an ill-fated lot. A few have met violent death*, some are
stonebroke, others in gaol.
* • • •
Nothing more forcibly shows the extent of the
depression in Melbourne than the number Of bookmakers
who have left to permanently live in Sydney.
* * * *
The coming Vic. football season promises to be
interesting. Footsoray, formerly the most despised club,
is as yet unbeaten, and has just astounded the public by
making a draw with South Melb., which two days before
was only beaten by the last three-seasons premier
Essendon. Four out of five who saw the Essendon-South
match agree that next time South must win, while about
six out of seven spectators at Footscray agree that South
was lucky to escape from the city of smells with a draw.
Under the circumstances, the most exciting match of the
season —upon which the premiership will perhaps rest-
will be Footscray and Essendon, on the first Saturday in
July ’
They say that Tom Cannon will have a big look-
in for the next Derby with a despised outsider called
Reminder.
* * * •
Mr. J. J. Corbett, pugilist and actor, writes
Tub Bulletin’s London correspondent, is one of those
who have suffered from the trumpeting of friends. We
were all prepared, after reading “Gentleman Jacks
newspaper sayines, to see a blustering, noisy bully, foul
of tongue ; instead of which, Mr. Corbett turns out to be
a somewhat modest young gentleman, of nice manner,
rather handsome, and, lastly, a better actor than a good
many who take high rank in the profession. It is thought
that after all, he will meet Peter Jackson at the National
Sporting Club, and as many good sports have taken a
fancy to him he will not lack staunch support when he
steps into the ring. Good judges think he will give Peter
a good deal of hard work to take down his number, if the
nig. does it at all. Corbett’s punching of the sack in the
scene at his training-quarters in the Drury Lane play is a
treat to witness. Certainly I have never seen a more
active big ’un, not even Black Perry. Of course, the
boxing is all “fake,” but, for all that, he shapes like a
man who knows his business.
♦ ♦ • •
Tattersall’s Birthday Cup sweep of was
won by a syndicate of eight clerks in Vic. Audit Office.
They subscribed 2s. fid. each and took one chance,
Fiorrie’s victory means £560 apiece to the speculators.
Eight civil servants, anyhow, can afford to be indifferent
to Patterson’s threatened further retrenchments.
* * * .
Recent S.A. race-meetings establish the fact
that to spot winners the only method is to study, not the
condition of the horse, but of the owner. This applies
particularly to one notorious sport who ought to be aimed
out of respectable meetings.
The South Adelaides are far superior to any
other Adelaide football team this year, and public interest
went down to such an extent lately that people wondered
how footballers would tide over the winter without
working. Fortunately, at this stage, the Sowths lost,
and matters are again normal.
* ♦ ♦
London Sketch : “What a pity we have never
seen Moses, the Australian batsman, in England. The
completion of the intercolonial matches shows him to be
far and away tbe best batsman in Australia. This latter
remark is based on the N.S.W. averages alone, which the
Sketch innocently publishes as representing the per-
formances of all the Australian willow-wielders.

Penance was recently sold for 321} guineas, after
costing 2500 guineas as a two-year-old. On the whole,
his was a worse .case than Titan’s. Even his third in
Melb. Cup is discounted by the fact that Ronda, who

, It is related of a very small Maoriland jockey,

♦ » * *
Jockey Hayes’ law-expenses amounted to >£Boo,
but barrister Duffy generously returned a large sum and
advised Hayes to drop further proceedings “as there was
too much influence on the other side.”
• • * •
The choir-boys of a certain Adelaide church have
a grievance against their parson. On the Sunday before
Adelaide Cup was run he solemnly adjured them, in com-
mon with the rest of the congregation, to “ Hold Stead-
fast in their faith.” The boys, of course, accepted it as a
tip, and when the time came Steadfast ran fifth.
* * * *
Bookmaker owners won three out of the five
races at Randwick on Saturday.
• • * *
The reading of the Griffo-Johnny Griffin eight
rounds, fought at Boston, U.S., on 23rd April last, makes
it appear that Griflo is getting back to his old form. For
the eight rounds Griflo played a tattoo all over Griffin,
almost without a return, and the onlookers thought
Griffin finished the eight rounds on sufferance only.
* » • •
How long do Yankee oarsmen last? It looks
like going back to the dark ages to read that Haitian,
Teemer, Hosmer, Lee, Ten Eyck, and Wallace Ross have
entered, amongst other oarsmen, for a regatta in Texas.
Our Ned Trickett, Mick Rush, and Elias Laycock are of
their generation.
♦ * • •
Allen and Green, Australian bookmakers, prac-
tising in India, send their friends here some large photos,
to show how they do business on the courses. They work
in little wooden sheds, like bazaar stalls, with their names
blazoned up.

htfw good the performance was may be gauged from the

Stakes.

Mount Morganaire Hall will be getting himself
disliked by the racing public. Accepting for Florrie for
Tattersall’s Winter Stakes, scratching her at the last
moment, and her stable companion, Trenchant, starting
a hot favourite, is hardly a good example for racing men,
coming, as it does, from a millionaire and a steward of
the A.J.O.

on the Sydney side of the Murray.

Dear Bulletin,—“ Wombat ” asks what
causes the bare patch on the rump of the opossum?
Wombat could never have lived in the bush, or
he would have known. The ’possum lives in the
hollow ot a tree, and always sits upright. Another
reason is that when he has taken his fill, he sits in
the fork of a tree. These reasons show clearly
why the fur is worn off. —Yours, J.P.O’B. Win-
derradeen, Cobargo, 22/5/’94.

about. The accompanying illustration depicts a
scene at a wreck off the coast of New Guinea. A
box of St. Jacobs oil has been washed ashore, and
the natives gleefully welcome this marvellou-
household remedy as an old friend. Thanks to
the missionaries, the great and wonderful virtues
of bt. Jacobs oil are now known to the gentle
savage. The master of the missionary schooner
Ellangowan, and the captain of the Governor
Cairns have written of it in the most enthusiastic
terms ; and the genial Captain Liljablad supports
his testimony on the subject with a reference to
the popular Tamate (the Rev. James Chalmers) :
brom Greenland’s icy mountains to Afrie’s
coral strands” this wonderful specific is recognised
and valued as being without a peer for pains
bruises, rheumatism, neuralgia, sciatica, lumbago’
mumps, backache, faceache, headache, toothache’

bunions, abrasions, and kindred ailments, St’

SNAKE POISON AND HUMAN POISON.
Ihe bite of a rattlesnake is almost always fatal to
man. Yet this snake is never affected by the
injection of its own poison into its own blood,
neither when enraged it bites itself nor when its
venom is injected by artificial means to test the
w e?t ", S P eak,n £ on the latter point Dr, S.
, it l / Mitchell, an expert on snake poison, says,
i have oyer and over tried this experiment, but
this beT” laVe 1 SGen death reSulfc ‘ Wh y should
Dr. Mitchell further says : “The many noxious
compounds man carries in his liver and gastric
g ands (the stomach) are fatal if they enter the
blood in any large amount. There is scarcely an
organ. m man s body which is not a possible source
ot poison to him. Small doses are constantly
passing into and out of his blood. The question
is whether he can get rid of it as fast as it ac-
cumulates.”
Alluding to death by rheumatism, gout, and
™ney disease, an eminent London physician says ;
body 1 ” man 18 P°* sone( i by the products of his own
Now let us cite a passage from a man’s experience
as related by himself. The time extends over a
period from March, 1888, to May, 1889, more than
y?\\ He says: My head used to whirl around,
and 1 had frequent bouts of faintness and giddi-
ness; and at such times I used to fall down, no
matter where I was. This would occur two or three
times a day. For three weeks I was confined to
my , bed - / grew gradually weaker and weaker
and lost a deal of sleep. I felt more tired in the
morning than when I went to bed. After a while
1 became nervous, and my legs trembled and shook
under me to such a degree that I feared to walk
out. 1 had great pain in my kidneys, and the
secretion which I voided from them was thick and
yellow as the yolk of an egg. Month after month
passed and I failed more and more, and could
hardly crawl about.
I had a doctor attending me, but his medicines
did not benefit me. He said my liver and kidneys
were in a bad way, and that he never saw secretions
passer in such a state. After treating me six
months, he told me that medicine could do no
more for me and advised me to go to a hospital.
1 went to Peterborough hospital, but got worse
while there. Ihe hospital doctors refused to tell
me what ailed me. Having spent two months
there 1 got anxious and returned to my home,
utterly disheartened. I continued to send to the
hospital for medicines, which I took for three
months longer. I was now so emaciated that my
get well Wh ° ° ame t 0 866 me Sa ' d 1 WOuld
“In this condition I continued until May, 1889
when one day an umbrella vendor called at my
house, and, seeing how ill I was, said his wife had
been cured of a serious illness by Mother Seigel’s
Curative Syrup. He spoke so earnestly of it that
f J e^ enn u ned b r fc l y , sfc .- After takin £ the Syrup
for ten days I felt in better spirits: my food
agreed with me, and from that time I gained
strength daily. Persevering with it, I was soon
able to return to my work as healthy and strong
as ever. Since then I have been in the best of
7? U a , r , e at liberty to publish the above
facts, and I will gladly reply to any enquiries.
Yours truly (Signed), Read Welfare, Ramsev,
tit. Mary s, Hunts, February 18th, 1892 ”
No brief comment can do justice to this remark-
able case. What the public needs to know amPto
remember is this : Mr. Welfare’s whole system
was poisoned by the products of a torpid and
inactive digestion. These had entered his blood,
as Dr. Mitchell describes. The nervous system
was disordered and half paralysed; hence the
faintness and falling fits. Rattlesnake poison
kills by paralysing the nerves which actuate the
lungs; it kills by suffocation. Human poison
arising from indigestion and dyspepsia, always
operates in the same direction, causing asthma
m its worst forms. It then attacks the
heart and kidneys, causing the state of things
Mr. Welfare mentions. Nothing more noxious
or in the end, surely fatal exists in any
reptile. And yet people trifle with the disease '
an « dootors seem not to understand it.
Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup cures by stimu-
lating the kidneys, skin, and bowels, and toning
the gastric glands. h
Who, then, is man’s most deadly enemy? Care-
less and ignorant man himself. Use the remedy
then when the earliest symptoms appear.

HERR RASSMUSSEN, PH.D.
A Few Sketches of His Interesting Life and
Work.
Herr Rassmussen, who is known throughout
the world as “The Celebrated Danish Her-
balist,” was born in the little kingdom of Den-
mark, shortly after its last disastrous war with
Germany. His parents being wealthy, and
belonging to the most influential class, he
received a first-class education, and displayed
exceptional talent in the science of botany and
botanical chemistry, and very soon surpassed
everyone of his fellow scholars in those sub-
jects, and eventually made the science of botany
his life-study.
Finding his native country too small, and its
flora too limited to extend his botanical re-
searches, he left for Australia, and landed here
in the beginning of 1879. His first object was
to learn the English language and become
familiar with the customs and habits of his
adopted country, as well as with its botanical
history and medicinal flora. Herr Rassmussen
bad quite his share of the troubles and difficul-
ties which usually befall a newcomer in Aus-
tralia ; but he soon overcame every difficulty,
and after five years’ travelling through all the
colonies, investigating every known Australian
medicinal plant, and discovering many new
ones, he eventually settled down permanently
in Sydney in 1883, and then commenced a
most noble mission—that of turning his most
wonderful botanical discoveries to the benefit
and relief of suffering humanity. Herr Rass-
mussen succeeded in this gigantic undertaking
far beyond all expectations, and his name very
soon became a household word, not only in
every Australian camp, village and city, but
also throughout Europe, Africa and America.
Space will not permit to mention the
numerous cures effected by his now world-
famed Alealine Remedies, suffice it to say
that thousands of original testimonials may be
inspected by anyone at his Head Depot, 547
George Street, Sydney, many of which may
also be seen in his celebrated work, “ Ihe
Natural Doctor.”
There is no doubt whatever that Herr Rass-
mussen deserves all the success and praise which
he has met with, because he is a most scientific,
conscientious and genuine herbalist —-his last
ten years record being so wonderful that his
remedies must have cured more human suffering
in that period than is usually effected by a
hundred average doctors in a similar number of
years. He does not hold any consultation
similar to a doctor ; his whole business is
purely a Proprietary Herbal Remedy Business.
He simply prepares a genuine herbal remedy
specially for the nerves, and another for the blood,
and so on, which can always be relied upon.
Ho prints on the box containing the remedy its
price and all advice as to doses and diet and
other particulars, and advertises same, which
is the fairest way possible to conduct a business
of this kind. Any information or particulars
desired are given or sent absolutely free of
charge. Pamphlets are sent post free to any
person desiring one, containing all particulars
and many uselul and valuable hints on health.
Herr ilassmussen has very large branches
of his extensive business in Melbourne,
Brisbane, Adelaide, Perth, Wellington (N.Z.),
and tlrroughout the world. is an
honorary member of several scientific socie-
ties, a gold medalist, diplomee and Hon.
Corr. Mem. of the Academie Parisienne des
Inventeurs, France ; last year he received the
Honorary University degree of Doctor of
Philosophy ; in 1887, he was presented with a
magnificent gold medal by several of his cured
patrons in Sydney, and also awarded a silver
medal in Denmark, 1888, a bronze medal,
Melbourne, 1893; and in 1891 he issued Ins
“ Natural Doctor,” a herbal medical work of a
high standard for which he received great
praise from the press, and which is now trans-
lated into many languages.

Editor Bulletin, —A mountain friend of
mine lost three sheep. After looking for them for
a week, he gave it up himself, but sent his dog
out. The dog returned in four days with two
sheep and the skin of the other. He’d used that
one for rations.—Yours truly, Ringtail. Yass. —

Governor MacGregor’s Sorcery Ordinance
for the New Guinea niggers :
1. White men know that sorcery is only deceit, but the
lies of the sorcerer frighten many people. The deceit of
the sorcerer should be stopped. 2. It is a forbidden act
for any person to practise or to pretend to practise sor-
cery. 3. It is a forbidden act for any person to threaten
any other person with sorcery, whether practised by him-
self or by anyone else. 4. Any person who practises
sorcery or who procures another person to do so, or who
threatens anyone with sorcery, may be tried by a Magis-
trate. Punishment: 5. If the person tried is found
guilty, he may be sentenced by a European Magistrate to
imprisonment not exceeding three months, or by a native
Magistrate to imprisonment not exceeding three days.
6. If the person is sent to prison he will be made to work
without payment.
The Bulletin has just received an ordinance
issued by the boss New Guinea nigger :
1. Your chief knows that the new religious sorcery of
the white men can knock spots off nigger sorcery any day.
The deceit of the missionary must therefore be stopped.
2. I forbid any white missionary to practise, or pretend
to practise his sorcery.
3. Any white man practising sorcery may therefore be
caught and killed by anyone who takes an interest in his
country’s welfare.
4. It is distinctly forbidden to any man who may kill a
missionary (sorcerer) to bruise him with a stone club or
any other blunt instrument that may spoil the meat for
cooking. He may be either speared or shot with an
arrow.
5. No man must cut any piece or pieces off a dead white
sorcerer and cook them on the quiet. In all cases the
body must be sent to me for division.

Mr. C. C. Kingston, Q.C.
Charles Cameron Kingston, Q. 0., whose portrait
appears in this issue, has been more or less of a
puzzle to people all his life. His exuberant Demo-
cracy or was it a touch of genuine Republican-
ism . moved Samuel Griffith, at the ’9l Conven-
tion, to plaintively inquire : “Does he or does he
not intend to make the Queen a permanent part of
the 1 arhament ? Does he intend that the Common-
wealth of Australia is to be presided over by the
Queen ?’’ Kingston passed these conundrums ;
and, indeed, the chances are that the Queen, with
the best intentions of being permanent in this
Vale of Tears, will have gone to reign some-
where else before she is called on to serve
the Australian Commonwealth in any capa-
c>ty save on the principle that they also serve
who only sit and wait. Mr. Kingston was
a puzzle to his friends when, in 1881, his 30th
year, he entered S.A. Parliament for West Adel-
aide, which constituency has ever since returned
}Y as h® to bring the traditions of the foot-
ball-field and the Police Court into Parliament?
Ur was he to be the worthy son of his judicious
and weighty father—first S.A, Speaker? Perhaps
he was puzzled himself, for he simply combined
thetwo. Then there was another puzzle when, in
a John Colton—Pasquin Mitford’s “Pious
Saddler ” —made this young blood his Attorney-
general. Ministries change in S.A. as fast as
Dibbs policy changes in N.S. W., so that one is not
surprised to find that Kingston has been a member
of four Ministries since June, 1884, He is now
Premier of a unique Cabinet—one which, until
Playford grabbed the Agency-General, consisted
of a Premier, three ex-Premiers and two other ex-
Ministers. It has lasted a year now, but June is
a dangerous month for Ministries in Adelaide.
® Irish-Australian is—well, be is not cold-
blooded, and Kingston has shaved pretty closely the
rock upon which more than one great leader split.
But he has no small vices ; and this, with his at-
tempt to give the parliamentary suffrage to women,
keeps him solid with the W.C.T.U. and other
pious organisations. He is undoubtedly a strong
man. What other public man in Australia could
have held his head up against the storm of laugh-
ter which shook the Continent when he wanted to
fight a duel with an opponent ? Dibbs is still
Premier of N.S. W., though he walks about with a
variety of small arms in his trousers pocket. But
how long would he be in public life if he were to
send a revolver across to Parkes, with the polite
request that he would stroll down before
lunch to the Post Office Place and get shot?
• efc a ,f® w months after Kingston had been
ignominiously bound over by a police magis-
trate to keep the peace he became Pre-
mier of his province. Perhaps the fact that
the other fellow was a O.M.G. was taken as an
extenuating circumstance. But that’s Kingston all
over—the football part of him. He is popular out
of doors; not popular, but feared in the Assembly.
Few members care to stand up against him when
he is in a nasty mood. The Bulletin knows
only of one in the S.A. Parliament who would bo
his match —and that is the silver-tongued Gordon,
who wiped the floor so beautifully with Parkes at
the Convention. “The most polished and bril-
liant satire I ever listened to,” said Hori
Grey. But Gordon is in the Upper House
and Kingston’s Chief Secretary. In a set
«peech, Kingston is wearisome, patchy, legal.
As a debater, he is ready, quick and tell-
ing. In private life his eloquence is what may
be called forcible —almost John Robertsonian.
His sentences are punctuated also with sharp
interrogatory grunts, and the mystery is how he
gets rid of all these eccentricities when he puts on
his wig and goes into Court. For—another
puzzling thing—this most democratic of Austra-
lian Premiers is a lawyer. A very good lawyer,
too, and a Q.O. Of course, his practice is not as
large as it would have been had he not gone
into politics and mines and other expensive
things. The working classes have a good
friend in Kingston, Q. 0., and—after their
manner—they are none too grateful. King-
ston is now only 44. His greatest work
hitherto has been helping to establish Protection
for protective purposes in S. A. What he will do
in the future, given the ordinary span of life, is to
be measured only by his will. A little less of the
lawyer-politician, a little less of the football-
barracker, a little less of the —ahem!—rock. Then,
he has but to help the progressive party, to guide
it along with his keen perception of danger, his
hearty contempt of “respectable” traitors, his
bluff geniality and good temper. There is no
public man in Australia who has it in his power
to do more good to the cause of democracy than
Charles Cameron Kingston.
The patch of bad weather which struck
Sydney about the last day of May was foretold
by N.S.W. Astronomer Russell three weeks in
advance. This breaks Damp Wrague’s record.
A funny story is current in Melbourne
concerning the deal made by Speaker Bent for the
gorgeous gold-braided official coat of ex-Speake?
Matthew Davies—which, they say, originally cost
45500.
The Australian Star’s foreign authority
makes a ghastly mess of the late Bulgarian insur-
rection, which disturbance it locates in Servia.
Also, it is under an impression that the famous
Stambouloff is a Servian statesman, and that Sofia
is somewhere about Belgrade. Yet when an Eng-
lish paper thinks that Bendigo is in Tasmania,
near Cooktown, the Stab’s lofty contempt for its
ignorance is a spectacle for the gods.
Most of the erudite and profoundly incom-
prehensible law-books in Sydney Public Library
are stored on the top shelf. Consequently John
Norton can now mostly be found on the extreme
top of a tall ladder, where he is busy getting up
his case against Crick for the Privy Council. The
ladder isn’t strong enough to bear two, so John’s
friends, when they want to communicate, have to
coo-ey to him from the floor below.
It is said that the first week the Linotype
was working at Sydney D.T. office with raw
hands, the setting cost lid. a thousand; next
week it was 7d., and the following week s|d.
About 40 cols, a day were being set per machine
up to the end of last week, and the proprietors
ultimately reckon that the setting of the paper
will be done for fid. a thousand, instead of, as by
hand, Is. Id. —a saving of over 45100 a week,
after labour, supervision and interest on cost of
machinery have been paid for.
Vincent Pyke, of Maoriland, is dead. He
was an aged and bitter politician who made
sundry respectable failures in literature and left
much eloquent vituperation in Hansard. He
died partly of old age and partly, in all pro-
bability, of the Otago Central railway, which un-
finished white elephant preyed on his mind for
many years. The Bulletin has told many
interesting anecdotes about him, but he always
wrote and contradicted them in vigorous language.
Taking him all round Pyke’s strongest feature
was that he was the most contradictory man in or
out of Maoriland.

London Times has no sympathy with R.
. Stevenson’s Samoan grievances. There are
signsthat the cult of this much-beboomed author
is dying out. People are tired of waiting for the
great work which has been so long coming and
never comes. Stevenson’s fame was founded
mainly on the praise of a little knot of academic
critics like Andrew Lang who scratch one
another s backs in the London magazines, and
who have whittled away so long at the perfection
or form that they have lost the power to judge
matter. Stevenson’s best work is in his essays,
which give full play to his wonderful talent for
literary workmanship; his novels are childish, and
nis poems aren’t poetry.

The recently-deceased Marquis of Ailes-
bury, after “blowing” a million or so in a
very few years, died in humble lodgings in
xsnxton, where his board and his wash-bill were
defrayed by his sister and her husband, Sievier-
button, the bookmaker. He sold everything he
could sell save his liver, and had he known the
appallingly diseased state of that organ and its
value as a curiosity, as revealed by recent mail
advices, he would probably have raised money on
it also. Ailesbury’s liver, it now appears, is about
the most wonderful organ of its kind ever held
up on a fork for the observation of a medical class.

The drollest passage in the Vic. Governor’s
speech last week related to missionary Robert
Reid ; During the recess the Minister of Defence
has been entrusted with the negotiation in Eng-
land, the United States and Canada, of many im-
portant affairs connected with the commerce of
this country. Besides accomplishing other valu-
able work he has paved the way for important
undertakings connected with our surplus pro-
duce. The truth about Reid is that he was sent
forth on the pretence of doing things which he
never made the faintest show of meaning to do
when he arrived at his destination. All sorts of
strange suppositions are hazarded as to the real
why and wherefore of Reid’s palpable sham
mission.” Unless he went abroad to talk Free-
trade the reason of his pilgrimage is probably
known only to his gassy self.
Extract from the election address of King
O’Malley, a wild Irish candidate in S.A. :
I am for the total annihilation of rowdy hoodlum mules.
Theatres and public halls where citizens gather to discuss
political and other moral subjects should contain iron
cages for the incarceration of the arrogant, ring-tail
brainless mules who afflict and shock the audience by
their senseless vulgar braying at the speakers. These
lineal descendants of Balaam’s ass, devoid of all psycho-
logical attainments of their illustrious progenitor, must
be either reformed or muzzled. The political kicking
mule has assumed such stupendous proportions that it
behoves the citizens to array themselves on the side of
essential reforms and outraged privileges. These con-
tinually interrupting hybrids must be squelched, other-
wise the boasted expression, “Britons never will be
slaves," is a mockery, and free speech a thing of the past
in these colonies. An eminent anatomical analyst declares
that the brains of all the disturbing-public-meeting mules
on earth placed in the quill of an American humming-
bird and blown into the eye of a flea would not make it
wink. These self-opinionated selfish mules, with no pride
of ancestry or hope of posterity, boiling over with ..Ten-
sive disregard of other people’s rights, are an everlasting
pest upon mankind, a perpetual blot upon the bright
horizon of human existence.
Quite naturally, being mules, they have “no hop®
of posterity.” A mule never has any posterity.

expressed by the Executive, certainly, but that
oath is quite compatible with the endeavouring to
make the Executive go straight. M'Millan’s
bunday-school interpretation of members’ obliga-
tions makes them the dumbest driven cattle on
the most supreme of human questions. There is
no blood in softgoods.
A Queensland sugar expert recently told
the Courier that for a very long time to come the
Queensland output cannot affect the world’s
market appreciably. This year it will be about
100,000 tons, which is less than the annual varia-
tion of the Cuban crop, for example, which
averages from 700,000 to 900,000 tons 1 It will
take till 1897 before Australia can supply Austra-
lasian consumption. If the United States admit
sugar free it will give production a great impetus.
Queensland farmers now make about £6 an acre
profit ; and with 40-acre farms of good land, one
man with a family oi. n get along comfortably
without outside la'
Miss JESSIE MAYNARD, Monologuist.
The daily press, in its report of Mont-
gomery’s execution at Darlinghurst, was tolerably
unanimous on one point—that he maintained a
“ cold and callous demeanour ”to the last. When
the Emperor Maximilian, and his accomplices,
Miramon and Mejia, went out laughing and chat-
ting, to be shot at Queretero, the public was filled
with admiration at their fortitude ; when a retail
murderer goes out to be hanged in the same cheer-
ful fashion, it is merely remarked that he died
impenitent and conducted himself indecently in
his last moments. When Nagy Sandor was hanged
by the Austrians in ’49 and said nothing, he was
described as dying like a Christian ; when Mont-
gomery is hanged by Nosey Bob and says nothing
he is cold and callous. It seems to be almost im-
possible for a poor man to be hanged in a manner
that will satisfy the daily papers. How does the
daily paper want a man to be hanged, anyhow ?

parson, is doing Paraguay for S. A, Advertiser,
and was at New Australia as late as March.
From a recent letter :
We sit up on a stool, and lean not against the cracked
wall for fear of crickets. Large and slimy they seem
everywhere. We are told at once to be careful, as the
destructive insects not only eat into clothes but eat the
hair from children’s heads and tips from the fingers
The very next morning Miss Sibbald showed me her
forefinger, from which the outer skin had been nibbled in
the night, leaving the red exposed tip very sore. “ Be-
ware, too, of the jiggers,” said young Sibbald ; “ I have
taken 40 out of my feet to-day.” This was not very
cheering the first night. We enquire what to do and
how to avoid them. “ They are smaller than fleas, bur-
row in the skin, lay eggs, create swellings.” All you can
do is pick them out when you find them. Don’t leave
your boots on the ground; they like to get in boots.
Sibbald who, by the way, is an Adelaidean and
one of the ablest men on the settlement, “didn’t
care about saying anything against Lane,” but
private letters to Adelaide to friends contain
warnings not to leave S. A. “just yet.”
Editor Bulletin.— Sir,—ln the interest-
of truth and justice let me protest against the state
ment in your issue of 26th ult. that the yacht
Wanderer was a piratical vessel on a blackbirding
cruise when Mr. Ben Boyd so mysteriously dis-
appeared in ’5l, as it has no foundation in fact.
The Wanderer (then on a voyage from Frisco to
Sydney) merely called in at San Christoval, and
Mr. Boyd went ashore with his gun and took a
kanaka boy with him; nothing was ever definitely
known as to his fate. The master (Ottwell)
then made for this coast and anchored off
Port Macquarie. There was no Government
pilot here then but the master of the Eliza-
beth Cohen (Mr. George Easton, now living
at Ballma), then lying in the port, was engaged
to bring her in, but as she drew much more water
than there was on the bar she hammered in
until she struck the rocks and became a total
wreck. The only other white men on board were
Messrs. Crawford, Baines, and Webster. She
had a splendid collection of valuables and curios
on board, over which many of the people here
exercised more than manorial rights, and several
mementoes of the affair still adorn the dwellings
of some present residents. The whole affair was a
most mysterious one, and one which will probably
never be solved.— Veritas, Port Macquarie, 29th
May, ’9l.
The discovery at Brunswick (Melb.) of the
remains of a child partially devoured by dogs
recalls a South Australian incident of long ago.
On the Robertsons’ station none but Scotchmen
were employed, and the pride of the clan was
MDougall, their unparalleled Hieland piper.
One fell night M'Dougall took an overdose of
whisky, and lay down near the piggeries to sleep
it off. Next morning, his boots and bag-pipes
alone remained. The Robertsons have never kept
pigs since.

M ll wraith says there is solid comfort for
Australians in the thought that vid Canada they
can go all the way to England in a British ship or
on British soil. One feels at home in Canada,
mt on crossing the border one is immediately
oonsoious of a foreign atmosphere, so to speak.’’
Which of course, simply means that in Ibyal
stagnant, syndicate-ridden Canada MTI wraith
was bomebody, but as soon as he got into Yankee-
land he found the great American nation didn’t
care two cents about him. Nothing so much
astonishes and disgusts the little-great man with a

A globe-trotter who visited N.S.W. Legis-
lative Council lately squared out and put his
impressions thus; “There’s Jacob and Pilcher
and a fellow with a double chin and an old cove
without eyebrows, and I’m blessed but they are
equal to anything in the old land? They ate so
downright earnest! Why, they take legislation
as seriously as if it were religion. There’s no
nonsense, no persiflage, no tiddlywinking about
them, or, for the matter of that; about the whole
body of lawmakers. I know what the Senates of
half-a-dozen countries are; have listened to them •
have known the boys; but I never witnessed
solemnity, profound feeling, sacred obligation,
momentous responsibility, such as I observed in
your Council.” That globe-trotter was present
when the Mining on Private Lands Bill was under
discussion.
M Ilwraith (just back from England) prides
himself on being a crack sailor, and used to keep
a small launch on Moreton Bay, where it was one
of the standing amusements of the inhabitants to
watch him running aground on the various shoals,
irm boatmen, too, used to make a regular income
towing Mac. off when he stuck tight. On© day
Jack Hamilton was on board, and when the boat
grounded, as usual, he elected to soull ashore in a
dingy rather than wait for relief; but as the Fat
Man and his partner, Smythe, refused like true
salts to desert the disabled ship, Jack left them
his revolver and a box of cartridges to while away
the time. During the next hour MTlwraith and
bmythe fired 100 rounds at a swan that swam
around the boat, and at last succeeded in killing
it with an oar. It was afterwards found to be a
tamo bird, with its wings cut, the pet of an old
lady in the neighbourhood.
Interviewed by the Review of Reviews
“Rolf Boldrewood ” (T. A. Browne. P.M at
Albury, N.S.W.) appears to have done his best
to live up to such celebrity, and swelled out like
the frog who tried to be an ox. In a series of
snapshot judgments he knocked rival reputations
flying; and when he paused to breathe he stood
alone on the pinnacle of Australian literary fame
Marcus Clarke was hit off in three words—“ A
clever cockney.’’ Poor Marcus ! the only first-
class man of letters that Australia has produced—
“a clever cockney !” Truly, authors—the little
ones—hate other authors—the bigger ones as
women hate the woman who v outshines them.
Why, there was more literary merit in Marcus’s
little finger than there is in Boldrewood’s whole
body. Clarke was an artist; Brown is only a
diarist. Hissingle workof valueis “Robbery Under
Arms ’’—and everything good in that is history,
everything bad is fiction. It is less a story than
the materials of a story. Its interest comes nob
from Mr. Brown, but in spite of him. His awk-
ward, lumbering style has been unable to rob the
facts of their fascination. But in the hands of
Marcus Clarke, with his fund of pathos, his
instinct of the dramatic, what might not “Rob-
bery Under Arms ’’ have become? Perhaps the
superior of His Natural Life,” that hasty pot-
boiler, yet the one memorable prose achievement
in the first hundred years of Australian literature.
FAIRLY EARNED.
Jim Patterson’s knighted 1
The few are delighted,
The many scarce trouble to swear.
The butcher’s a ranker
With Davies, the banker,
And Benjy, the boom-busted Mayor:
Not greater or lesser
The Queen —Heaven bless her !
Has granted him equal renown
With Sargood and Wrixon,
His bauble he’ll fix on,
In token of love for the Crown.
Jim Patterson’s knighted I
He lately invited
The Yorks to come out for a trip.
The joke he afforded
Is fitly rewarded —
“ Sir Jim ” is an excellent quip.
A proper requital
Is Patterson’s title ;
The Bulletin doesn’t object.
His game’s worth its candle,
His name’s worth its “ handle,”
And he doesn’t want common respect.

Darlinghurst Gaol.
No1.-A RIDE IN BLACK MARIA.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
I couldn’t pay the moderately large fine, any-
how. It might as well have been a million as far
as I was concerned.
“ This way,” said the policeman; and, very con-
siderately, they placed me in a yard at the rear of
the Police Court, instead of throwing me into the
large room in which were noisily congregated a
company of drunks, thieves and foul-mouthed
gentlemen. For which I was very thankful.
I had left a cab-fare in the “office,” hoping I
might pay my own freight up to Darlinghurst, and
this already isolating distinction somewhat com-
forted me—alas ! —though I confess I fancied I
could have “eaten without salt ” my persecutors,
and I would have been very “ willing ” in a round
or two with the arresting bobby, or even the beak
himself. ... mi m
“ However, I shan’t be here long. They 11 run
me down to my new retreat presently,” thought I.
But I didn’t know what care, attention, and
gratifying honors the officials pay to a Prisoner of
the Crown. And I was now. that personage.
Time : 11 a.m. About two hours later :
“ Want to send for your dinner ? ”
Sharp and harsh came the query through the
iron-gate.
“No.” . . .
Dinner? Would I ever feel like eating again?
The hours sped on in all the gloomy inaccessi-
bility of a jealously-guarded Czar. I could hear
the oaths of the gentlemen in the large room, and it
rather modified the mental satisfaction of my soli-
tary grandeur that they, too, were of exactly the
same official status as myself.
About 4 o’clock—“ This way ! ”
I walked out of that yard to find myself
“ mustered ” with a mixed lot of male and female
prisoners, perhaps two dozen in all. And incon-
tinently thrown—yes, thrown, for I moved slowly
and looked around for my cab !—into the Govern-
ment Coach—Black Maria.
Every city reader has probably seen the lady,
but, in compassion for the very probable ignorance
of country readers, I’ll try and describe the
points. Better still: You shall make one for
yourself.
Recipe : Two shallow railway-trucks ; end them
up and rivet them back-to-back ; put outsides on
them, with a fancy air-grating or two ; steps be-
hind ; high box-seat in front; four wheels, pole,
&c., and you’ll manage decently enough, if
you daub her all over as black as the character
of a politician. I had almost forgotten to tell you
to put in a little trap-door behind, so that the
bobby who rides on the step, even after he has
locked and bolted you in, can spit in at you if he
likes. Also, you can spit out at him, if you like.
Only he has the choice of keeping the trap shut.
You will thus see that Black Maria is a corn-
par tmented female. I believe the general rule is—
“ Ladies to the left and gents to the right.’ But
all the company must be got in somehow, and
well, we blended.
On one side were 15 men; on the other, three
men and five women. I was one of these fortu-
nate three.
One side of Black Maria is more accommodating
than the other —a way with many females who
have a blind side. The reason is that the coffin-
space under the box-seat helps out the accommoda-
tion of one side.
But the awfulness of that jam. It was the
Black Hole of Calcutta on wheels !
And I would here pause to ask—Where are the
Transit Commissioners? The jolting of that
caravan proved that it has very sensitive springs.
Jammed like pigs in a box, we bumped and
bumped and swayed and swayed till we really
longed for the end of the journey. At least I did.
Some day there’ll be an almighty smash, and
then, what an item for the papers ! But I hope
that only the policemen will he killed.
I got jolted fair on to the laps of two weather-
beaten ladies —their generic term, I understand, is
“ haybag ” —who conveyed large impressions of
rum, onions, tobacco, unwashiness, and that wild,
weird odour that, according to Zola, belongs to
the boudoirs of enterprising femininity.
I hadn’t room either to stand upright or sit
down modestly; had the space been there, the
jolts would have prevented the accomplishment of
either feat.
“ For the Lord’s sake, sit on me,” appealingly
shrieked one of the pungent ladies.
“Not even for His sake, madam,” said I
jerkily. I tried to say it politely and reverently,
but the hurricane motion wouldn’t permit it. I
suppose I hadn’t got my Black Maria-legs.
“ Here we are,” said a gentleman.
I heard a whistle sound, and in we lumbered
with a climb and a dive. The machine is backed
up, and the doors are flung open so suddenly that
we are literally shot out, rather mixed up as to
limbs. The police are sometimes zealously expe-
ditious, like schoolboys at the play-rush “ dis-
miss.”
They lined us up and counted us, so as to make
sure none had escaped en route. I wonder will
any marvellous magician of a criminal ever
achieve the ungallant feat of escaping from Black
Maria while in full tear?
“Down those steps”—but the officer needn’t
have said it, for the others all knew the way, and
I followed on, chiefly, I think, because I hadn’t
thanked the aromatic lady for her recent kind
offer. But I never got the chance' until now. If
she sees these lines, I hope she’ll accept my grati-
tude.
Women ahead, we were mustered again in a
dark passage, lest some balloon might have fished
us up and spirited us away ! .
Here we were sorted out: —(1) Committed ; (2)
Remanded ; (3) Sentenced.
I was a three-r.
There was an invisible clerk somewhere behind
a hole, who was the most inquisitive man I ever
knew, for a man who wouldn’t even introduce
himself! Asked me my name, age, country, reli-
gion, crime, everTbeen-in-before, diseases —well, I
forget now if he did ask me about my ailments.
Everyone displayed the greatest interest in me
—measured my height, weighed me, and I won’t
swear that they didn’t snap-camera me. _ And all
without even asking me to put a penny in the slot.
Here we bade the ladies adieu —“ so-long is the
correct expression. . , .
Away through an inner gate, into B wing.
“ Off with your coats and shirts and boots !”
Then they very kindly take charge—without
charge—of all your loose change, jewellery, knives,
pocketbooks, lead-pencils, pipes, keys, pawn-
tickets, papers—yes, and handkerchiefs ! A man
mustn’t get catarrh in Darlinghurst.
And after stripping you stark naked, and giving
you a prison'shirt, they have the gall to enquire
if you’re quite sure you haven't got any tobacco,
or pipes, or knives” about you! And the queer
marvel is that at least one of our squad had all ot
those articles, and matches into the bargain .
File up and march to the dispenser, who asks
how you feel very sympathetically, as the doctor
does afterwards, but all so swiftly as to take away
your breath and faculty for surprise, ihe two
operations were rushed through in less than 1U
minutes. ~
The boozey hands get pick-me-ups, and generally
“all hands ” are extemporaneously “ shaky.
Off to the bath-room, and here it is indeed
express—loo miles a minute, I should say.
I jump into the hot water and am just going to
dip, when —“Come out o’ that bath ; goin ter be
there all day ?” . , ,
“Time seems to be more valuable m here tnan
it is outside ?” I venture to observe to a gentleman
who is putting on the clothes that the Crown has
lent him. . ,
“ Gettin’ on ter five o’clock; screw wants ter
get’ome.” (“Screw” is the gaol vulgate for the
turnkey, or warder). . ,
They run us to the corridor of B wing, and
here again they count us ! Verily they are fond
of me at a moment’s notice, as it were. Lovei at
first sight cannot be nearly as speedy. Drafted
by threes, we are closed up in cells, inspected by
three warders —oh, they don’t neglect you ! and
locked in for the night.
I learn that our apartment —and any similar
one—is vernacularly known as “the peter.
Three long mats and three swags of blankets form
the chief appointments of “ the peter.”
My comrades discuss the qualities of the pick-
me-ups they got, and that adds to my sorrow, for
I hadn’t been “up to” that luxury. Next, they
produce short nose-warmers, weed, and matches,
and my cup of envious misery is full. .
Woe never had such leaden weight as during
that hour I sat on my bundle. At last, I unrolled
it, unbooted, and turned in “all standing. One
gentleman still “ ’ad a horful fit of the shakes,
and kept patronising the watercan.
“ Wos you on the boose when you nicked the
super?” (stole the watch).
“ Oh, no.” . .
The questioner was satisfied with that simple
reply and asked nothing more about the incident.
I wondered. The “nicker” seemed a decent
Scotchman, with an open, trustworthy counten-
ance. What did make him “nick the super ?
Hunger? Or did ho want rain-proof quarters for
awhile? ■ ,
I slept. “ Clang, clang, clang ! at quarter to
six, and I awoke, strangely refreshed. 1 had been
in the cell 12 hours and I had probably slept
I ”” 6 l’ve emptied the blooming water-can, and my
throat’s like a glue-pot,” said the Shaker.
He showed Scotty and me how to fold up out
blankets—“ make our beds 1’ I felt ravenously
hungry, and I said so.
“ You’ll be blooming lucky if you get yer ration
afore 12 to-day; they’ll split us all up into yards
directly, an’ we’re not on the ration-list yet, saia
Shaker. „ . , . , ' ,
The doors are unbolted, there s a rush into the
passage, where some 60 men are straggling along.
I am lined up in the corridor with 30 or so motleys,
and we stand at attention until arrive the
Deputy-Governor and Chief Warder. The muster-
roll is called, and each man files off as his name is
ticked off—except me. I stay where I am, not
knowing where to go. Nervously I ask the first
warder who passes.
“Stand over there !”—pointing to a doorway—
he thunders so fiercely that my blood leaps up to
my face, and then . ,
I understand it all. I am a Prisoner of the
Crown - 0000.
A Quick Calculating Table.
Mr. W, M. Pearson, who has been in the employ
of M‘Burney, Wallis & Co., the well-known byd-
ney wool and produce brokers, since they com-
menced business, has invented a quick way o
calculating the value of hides at the price per lb.
realised, with the trade allowance of six per cent,
tare. The plan has been printed and adopted by
the autioneering firms engaged in the trade, and
it is estimated that the time saved, under the new
method, is more than one half, as compared with
the old system of working out each item, the
invention, while proving a great boon to the pro-
duce trade in our city, will greatly facilitate the
early despatch of returns to owners.
The successful removal of Piles of the
most stubborn description is being effected by
Sharp’s new “ Cannalin ” treatment, large quan-
tities of which are being forwarded to sufferers
throughout the colonies. It is obtainable, with
advice, from 257 George-street, Sydney.

DE PROFUNDIS.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
On Life’s diverging paths we stood,
We vowed eternal love ;
We swore our faith was limitless
As the wide skies above.
Our voices told in notes of gold
Of love that never could grow cold,
Of faith so pure and courage bold,
And wit to win and strength to hold
The hearts we loved of old.
Now, in the desolate to-day,
Our lonely paths are still
Divided by the iron sway
Of Destiny’s harsh will.
Eastward or west, to toil addressed
I strive and labour without rest,
To gain the prize I deem most blest.
To lay my head upon the breast
Of her I loved of old.
In the dim future’s avenue
I see her smiling there ;
I see the sunlight falling through
The leaves—upon her hair.
Oh, Destiny ! relax thy will—
Help me my promise to fulfil.
Oh, Qod ! turn back thy ceaseless mill,
I swear I will possess her still—
Her whom I loved of old.
CLEONR.
Moderator Rentoul, on his last trip from
Europe by mail steamer, was asked to preach to
the second-class passengers, but languidly replied
that iti was “ too hot.” It is a comfort to think
that, if this is any guide, there are no sermons
down below. Hell is gradually being shorn of its
terrors.
-^ n . alleged bigamist, now awaiting trial in
Maoriland, differs a little from ordinary bigamists,
inasmuch as his first wife is said to be also his
half-sister. The lady, it is alleged, married him
under an assumed name, so as to avoid notice,
and then they lived unhappily for six months, at
the end of which period the husband fled and
married somebody else’s sister. Some more than
usually cheerful revelations are hoped for.
Yarra Bend Asylum (Melb.) is haunted by
a cured patient who fell in love with a stalwart
female warder during his term in the asylum, and
Insists ° n breakin K ’ n again to “see his girl.”
When he can’t get in to see his girl he sings love-
ballads in a minor key over the wall and heaves
tender communications across. There is difficulty I
in finding a law to meet his ease, so he has been i
arrested for vagrancy and remanded pending an
inquiry into his sanity.
Parson Isitt, the Maoriland teetotaller,
tells an interesting story to show how Heaven
protects the dry, serious apostle. During the last
electoral campaign some of the liquor-party, he I
asserts, laid for him on a lonesome road, with a
view to giving him a wholesome and vigorous
correction with sticks. But, unfortunately, they
didn t know Pastor Isitt by sight, and he tripped
past on his light clerical toe unmolested. Then
another preacher came along, and the party some-
how decided that he was Isitt, and gave him
beans; and when the victim had finished receiving
that vegetable, he was turned loose to go his
bruised and miserable way, while the aggressors
went home rejoicing. This is the sort of divine
interposition which we are always hearing about;
one man is providentially saved, and, as a conse-
quence, another man is providentially walloped.
Only it is never the man who was providentially
walloped who tells the story.
Colemane & Sons’ Vic. Ointment is marvellous.
The other day there landed in Melbourne,
along with a, consignment of other destitute
Asiatics, a dirty and unabashed nigger with
absolutely nothing on save a rag round his waist.
He had no luggage, no money, no hat, no boots,
no home, prospects, letters of introduction to the
Governor—nothing, in fact, save the aforesaid
rag. The authorities were horrified when they
saw this apparition stepping ashore, and sent him
on board again while they hunted up some statute
under which he could be arrested and sent back.
But there wasn’t any. Had he been insane he
might have been captured and detained, but being
in his right mind, what there was of it, and with
his cummerbund on, there was no Act to meet
the case. So the Pathan and his rag came ashore,
and vanished somewhere down Lily Boulke-stleet,
and hasn’t been heard of since. Why he wasn’t
arrested for “ disorderly conduct ” doesn’t appear.
Dear Bulletin, —It is not uncommon to
see in democratic papers denunciations of those
who “receiverents from immoral houses.” Some-
times these pars, originate with Mr. Editor or his
contributors, sometimes they simply report how
Miss W. C. T. U. Skarekro blasted Tottie’s land-
lord. But where is the “ sweet reasonableness ”
of these denunciations? It has long since, by
failures innumerable, been shown to be impossible
to reform Tottie under our present “civili-
sation ” (of which she is a natural product).
Is she, then, to be turned out of house and com-
pelled to sleep under the Market verandah, or in
the Domain? Or is she to be let occupy dwellings
rent-free ? It seems to me it is no worse for a
landlord to accept blackmail from a golden-haired
nymph than from the Chief Gimlet Bearer of the
Social Purity Brigade. When we abolish the
rent-robber, the interest-robber, and the profit-
robber, Tottie will have a fair chance to make a
comfortable living in a decent way, and, in the
meantime, men ought not to be abused for taking
rent from her any more than for taking it from
others.— E. W. Jonson. [The point of the accu-
sation against those who draw rents from
“ immoral ” houses is not the fact that they
provide shelter for social outcasts, but that
they advantage themselves of those outcasts’
necessities to screw from them bigger rents than
could be charged to persons not under asocial
ban. Also, that in many cases Tottie’s grinding
landlord is a “moral” citizen whose professed
horror of the “social evil ’is a most impressive
sentiment.—Ed. B.]
“Every man can write at least one book,”every
man with brains has at least one good story to
tell; every man with or without brains moves in
a different circle and knows things unknown to
any other man. Write carefully and plainly on
one side of the paper only, obliterating every un-
necessary word ; then mail your work to The
Bulletin, which pays for accepted matter. In
certain of the so-called prize-competitions the
great prizes fall to the newspaper-proprietor, who
gets the guileless public to work for him for noth-
ing. Short stories, or ballads, especially on bush,
mining, sporting, social, or dramatic themes, are
preferred by The Bulletin ; 1360 words go to a
column. If you can possibly keep your story
within a column all the better Don’t write a
column on any subject if a half-column will do ;
don’t write half-a-column where a mere paragraph
is enough. “ Boil it down.”
Suggestions for illustration with or without
rough explanatory drawings will be gladly re-
ceived by The Bulletin.
