HIS ROYAL HIGHNESS THE
Prince Wales
The Prospectors of Mountain
Boon .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
John Stewart, riding his grey stallion and lead-
ing the horse his wife had used the day before,
passed through Sturt’s Meadows and entered the
narrow track leading to Fowler’s Gap. He had
left Broken Hill at dawn on the previous day,
and had camped a few hours at Gardiner’s Creek.
The time was just 3 a.m. The moon had set an
hour before, and the only light was the phos-
phorescent gleam of the mineral salts in the drifts
which the horses frequently shattered in their
stride. Then the fragments of encrusted salts
flow into the air in pieces like dead white glass,
and fell again on the rocky pathway with a ring-
ing noise. The slipping of the horses’-hoofs on
the slugs of ironstone and boulders of rotten
granite, the clear, deep, breathing of the animals,
and the creaking of the rider’s saddletree were,
with this, the only sounds to rescue the Gap from
an unbearable silence. The dew fell so heavily
that it made little rivulets on the smooth rock
walls. The flashes rising from the sombre drifts
showed the frequent fissures in the track, which
looked blacker even than reality, by reason of the
ghostly and intermittent light.
There is something awful in the Barrier in the
hours just before the dawn. When the wonderful
white moon shines on the salt deposits, its re-
flected brilliance is the brilliance of millions of
electric-lamps. But when the moon has set, the
darkness is the darkness of the great depths of the
sea.
Occasionally Stewart’s horse stumbled into a
colony of kangaroos, and the silence was broken
by the numberless thuds of their leapings as they
made for the plain—the unnatural sound of flesh
striking rock—at his stirrup at first, and then
fainter, fainter, down the slope of the range. A
great bat dashes into the eyes of the rider and
moves the leaden air a little, and then the steady
“flap-flap” of an owl’s wing breaks the silence.
But there is no light given to distinguish shapes,
and the sounds are all mysterious and unaccount-
able.
At other times the horseman had felt somewhat
nervous when passing through the long corridor
of the Gap, whose walls were so delicately balanced
that it seemed a word would bring them about the
ears of the speaker; but this morning the sur-
roundings suited his mood, and he enjoyed the
dreadful silence and the inexpressible darkness.
At last they passed the Gap, and the horses,
with a snort of relief, plunged into the knee-deep
sand of the plain. By-and-by they came to a
small, rugged rise, and on the top of this a mys-
terious light seemed to play. Stewart knew the
spot well enough Some crank who believed he
could strike a silver lode in the hungry, baked-
clay country had shafted the rise to water level,
and a flock of thirst-maddened sheep had gone
down the shaft, enticed by the mineralised water
at the bottom. Sheep-like—and human-like, for
that malt had followed their leader, and
had filled the shaft to within three feet of the
Surface.
There they lay, 20 feet of rotting flesh and
fleece. The exhalations from this mass of cor-
ruption loaded the air so that a light seemed to
Play over the shaft. Stewart’s eyes were tired of
the darkness, and he pulled up his horse and gazed
at the spectral flame. The gloom had impressed
him that night with an enhanced awfulness. He
had seen the darkness, and the gleams of the salt
in the drifts, and the vague broken shapes of the
night-birds of the Gap, and had seen them all
through a veil of deadly rage. Now, as he gazed
at the luminous belt of air above the dead sheep,
he saw two scenes—and through the same veil.
He saw himself in all the glory of young blood
leading his wife from the church at Echuca on
their wedding-day. And he saw, further back
again, the same girl clasping her arms round his
old self s neck, and heard her say to his old self,
“I'll marry nobody but you, Jack. I wouldn’t
have the best man alive —if you weren’t the best.”
And he saw his old self and his old self’s wife
burying their child in the quiet necropolis of the
Campaspe, and heard words of that time : “This
will only bring us closer together, dear.”
. As the scene passed out of his memory he
sighed his relief as one redeemed from Hell’s
agonies, but inexorable memory showed him the
scene of yesterday—the scene he had thought over
till_ every thought became a pain. He saw the
claim on Mountain Boon which he and his mate,
Foster, had prospected so successfully, and the
windlass turning under his own skilled power,
and the mullock-tip, and the hut, and Foster’s
tent a hundred yards away, and the dogs looking
greedily at the kangaroo-meat suspended from the
ridge-pole, and Foster’s pet, the great Jew-lizard,
laughing horribly in the deep, red dust. And he
saw himself two days ago, saddling the horses;
for his wife was setting out for Adelaide that day,
and they were to start before the sun was well up.
And at dawn, as he was leading in the mare, he
saw his wife and Foster together.
They had not thought the mare was to be
caught so easily. They believed the man they
wronged would be absent a long time.
Stewart takes the bridle off the mare, and she
returns to her meal of salt-bush ; while her master
approaches the pair under cover of the hut, the
deep dust silencing his footfalls.
Foster is standing in front of the hut, and
Stewart, always an honest man, compares his
physical charms with those of the younger man—
very much to his own disadvantage. Foster is a
rather taller and fleshier man than Stewart, and is
by far the finer animal, but, like most fine-animal
humans, he has little or no intellectualism.
Stewart proves his possession of high, though un-
cultured, intellect by his neglect to act at
once on his discovery. If he did not have
brains he would rush to the front of the hut
and kill his wife and her paramour by knife
or revolver. But Stewart is a man who recognises
fully, though unconsciously, that the exquisite joy
of revenge is more in the planning of it than in its
actual execution. So he catches his breath
sharply, as if he would strangle it for fear it
should betray him.
His wife is speaking. “ Oh, Harry,” she says,
with an intonation which has a subtle mixture of
fear and love and remorse and worship, “isn’t my
caring for you wrong ? Isn’t it a sin to poor
Jack?”
At this Stewart feels that he could go to her
and take her in his arms, bub Foster answers
jeeringly—laughing with the unconscious brutality
of the animal.
“ It’s a bit too late to play that game,” he says ;
“ why the baby 1 - ”
She stops him with, “For God’s sake don’t,
He might hear.”
Stewart, hearing this, catches at his throat as if
he were choking. Foster goes on in his brutal
fashion, delighting to play with this silly woman
whose whole life is merged in his :
“Look here, Bella, if you are goin’ to cut up
like this I won’t be here when you come back,
I’ll sell my share of the claim to Stewart and’’
She stops him with a little gasping cry, and
Stewart feels that she has thrown her arms around
the Judas he had known and trusted as his mate,
Blindly and silently the wronged man gropes his
way back to where the mare is quietly feeding,
He feels only a great pity for the woman, but
he has noted every jeering word uttered by loster,
and has devised a new torture for every sneer.
He bridles the mare and leads her back, and
having saddled both horses goes into the hut, and
takes his breakfast, silently. 1 hey do not notice
him he is always slow of speech, and evidently
very stupid. He gives them an opportunity to
bid each other good-bye without the restraining
influence of his presence and at seven o clockdie
setoff with his wifeon the long drearyr.de to the
Sitting here on horseback in the gloom this
morning he sees all these events pass before him,
again experiences the temptation to kill the
woman in the Gap, and his faint-heartedness when
they had come to the place he had marked for her
grave. So he had let her live, and had put her on
the Adelaide-bound train at Broken Hill, and had
even kissed her on the cheek at parting. He did
not love her enough to kill her-she seemed to him
so weakly, passively criminal, so unutterably
despicable, that he merely pitied her.
„ • 7 1 “,
The picture seen through the veil of old scenes—
widely apart, one from the other, but seemingly
contemporaneous—passed away, but as he turned
his horse s head from the grave of the sheep .and
took up the track, the air trembled and the
ground seemed to shiver with anticipation.
In that solemn hour, just before the dawn, when
man and beast and leaf are at their weakest, it
seems as if the whole world lay anxious under
sentence of death awaiting the coming of the
executioner. And then, as horses and rider turned
a lack-lustre eye on the earth and forgot even
revenge and hunger in the awful tension of the
moment, aspire of chrome shot up from the horizon.
The daily resurrection had come once again—the
world was reprieved for sixteen hours. Then,
with the rapidity characteristic of the latitude,
beams stretched fanwise over range and gully,
the sun leaped from behind the hills, and it was
day.
The light brought Stewart back to the planning
of his vengeance. Sorrow had given him inspira-
tion, and the revenge he planned was terrible in
its ingenuity. The grey stepped out with a vigour
born of the sunrise, and at 10 o’clock Stewart left
the main track and twisted through the salt bush
to the summit of Mountain Boon.
Foster acknowledged his mate’s return with a
cheerful nod, and an intimation that breakfast
was spoiling for the eating. Stewart brewed a
fresh billy of tea, and ate with the appetite be-
fitting his long night ride. Foster filled his pipe
and sat in the door of the hub, smoking medita-
tively.
“ More you drive on that lode, better it shows,”
he said briefly at last.
Stewart looked up inquiringly, and the Hand-
some Animal continued : “Don’t know how all
those Jew-lizards and things come in the well of
that shaft. S’pose they go after the water. Look
here, George, soon as we raise a few tons more I’m
going to Adelaide for a spell.’’
Stewart said “ Yes ” as if he had no interest in
the matter, but all the while the fires of jealousy
were burning within him. Going to Adelaide,
was he? —to meet the woman, of course !
Foster rattled on about his plans while the
elder man gloated over his prospective revenge on
the fine young bodily personality that had come
between him and his wife. And as one chattered
carelessly of his intended spree, the other re-
arranged his place of vengeance again and yet
again, and at each re-arrangement that vengeance
took a shape more terrible than the last.
According to the sensible custom pursued by the
prospectors of Mountain Boon, Foster slept from
noon until four o’clock. Stewart, who was sup-
posed to be resting, did not stay in the hut. He
did a little work about the shaft, and packed a
few necessaries in a valise, which he strapped on
his saddle as it lay on his bed-place in the hut.
Extraordinary work this for early afternoon in
the Barrier summer, but that which followed it
was even more extraordinary.
The grey stallion had just finished a journey of
nearly a hundred miles, and yet Stewart had
turned him out belled. At four o’clock he called
Foster and told him it was time to go below,
Foster got up grumblingly and accompanied his
mate to the shaft.
The usual procedure was this. Stewart, being
an old sailor-man and as active as a monkey in
the matter of swarming up and down ropes,
lowered Foster to the drive and then climbed
down the rope himself. On this day, as Foster
stepped into the green-hide bucket and said
“Lower away,” a stone fell from the heap of
mullock about the shaft and plunged into the
gloom of the well. Then a faint hiss arose.
“By the old Harry,” said Foster, “there’s
some uncanny reptiles down there. It’s about
250 feet deep. I wouldn’t like to be among ’em.”
“ I don’t suppose you would,” returned Stewart,
quietly, as the. windlass revolved slowly, and
Foster dropped into the shaft.
The lowest drive had been put in at a depth of
210 feet. Then, finding they had followed a
stringer, and that the real ore body was above
them, they had driven again at 40 feet. When
about 30 feet of rope had run off the wind-
lass drum, Stewart placed the loop of the brake-
rope over the handle and looked down. Fosters
eyes were turned to the shaft’s mouth, and he was
about to ask angrily the reason of the stoppage,
when he saw the burning eyes of his mate looking
into his very soul, and without knowing why he
grew sick with fear.
“I’m not going to lower any further,” said the
voice of Stewart, quavering and breaking with,
every convulsion of his passion. “ An’you can’t,
climb up the rope. You’re a dead man, sure-
enough,”
The horror of the situation rendered Foster
almost speechless. He could only say, gaspingly :
“ What are you going to do ?”
“Oh, you know what you’ve done, do you.
You’re goin’ to have a nice little spree with Bella,
in Adelaide, aren’t you?” And then savagely :
“You dirty fraud. You think you can steal my
wife and go free. By the Lord God, you can’t
—you’re booked. Now, what will you give if I
haul you up again ?”
Foster found his voice again and Answered

eagerly, “ The claim’s a fortune. I’ll give you my
share.”
Stewart laughed raspingly. “You’re not bad,”
he said; “ the claim’s mine. I can kill you and
take your share without any trouble.”
Foster shrieked.
“D’ye know anything about the claim that I
don’t know, then? Let’s see if you can offer
enough for your life.”
The doomed man, sick and giddy with his
awful position and the swaying of the rope,
croaked rather than said: “Yes, weke on the
wrong track for the big lode. If we just put a
few big blasts in the top drive in this side we’d
open out on the heaviest stuff,”
“A good idea,” said Stewart approvingly;
“we’ll do that ”
m, . ‘ , .. , , L , .
The man m the shaft began to pour out his
thanks, but the Nemesis above stopped him.
“ You needn’t be so thankful,” he said ; “ you’ll
never leave that shaft again. I’ll find the lode
with the shots, but you’ll stay below when they’re
fired. An’ now you can say your pnyrers. I’ll
give you 10 minutes an’ I’ve said my last word to
you, ’cept to tell you the time.” _
So saying, he took out his great steel watch and
placed it before him on the platform. Then he
released the rope from its fastenings on the drum
of the windlass, and taking a single turn with it
round a projecting timber, uttered the words,
wo minutes.
The man below had been driven almost mad by
his terror and was only saved from falling out of
the bucket by his fingers convulsively and in-
stmctively clutching the rope. He threatened and
raved and entreated adopting a more piteous tone
as the watch ticked out its time and the time-
keeper of Death called out the minutes.
Then Foster tried to climb the rope, but the
late above let it slip a few inches and the doomed
man dug his nails into the soft mica formation of
the shaft and felt life crumbling in bis fingers.
And he looked at the top drive, and it was but
10 feet below him ; but as far away as Mercy and
the stars. _
‘ Nine minutes,” said the Fate. The victim
ha,d broken out into weeping and praying a few
minutes earlier, but now the fount of his tears
had dried up and he trembled at the end of the
rope, his handsome, stupid face working con-
yulsively, and his dry lips trying to form unavail-
ing words.
The rope dropped a few inches, and the new
terror unlocking his lips he gasped sobbing—
“ Not yet—my God—oh, my God ! ” _
“ Ten minutes, ’ said Stewart decisively, freeing
the rope from the timber.
A shriek, the quick rush of a falling body, the
Binging of a rope cutting the denser air of the
shaft, then the half-liquid crash of flesh striking
rock and _ water, half inarticulate moans, the
sound of sinuous bodies disturbed and attacking
the invader; then silence.
Stewart bent oyer the shaft feeling the fierce
exultation of the tiger which has killed the hunter
who robbed her of her whelps. Love is a great
educator, but revenge is a greater. This com-
monplace, stolid, almost stupid man, inspired
by his wrongs, had reached an extraordinary
height of intellectuality. The retributive jus-
tice of which he had been the instrument
raised him above the level of men. The pall
of the hereafter was for a moment lifted, and he
saw the light behind the light of the sun. He
saw that said “Vengeance is mine,”
because vengeance is the greatest pleasure, and he
knew that by working his own revenge he had
made himself a god.
Stewart returned from the hut with two new
ropes and a long fuse coiled around his shoulders,
and staggering under the burden of a green-hide
bucket full of cartridges,
“Raekarock’s a good sexton,” he said to him-
self, cheerfully, as he deposited his burden on the
mullock-tip.
Slinging his bucket on one of the ropes, he
lowered it down the shaft until it swayed gently
on a level with the entrance of the drive. Then
he fastened the other and stronger rope, which
was knotted at frequent intervals, to the frame of
the windlass, coiled the fuse around his shoulders
and dropped into the shaft. He quickly reached
the drive and swung himself into the entrance.
Then he drew the bucket towards him, and after
lighting a candle, carried it along the drive. The
face already showed the value of the lode the blast
was intended to discover. It was only a spur,
but bunches of chloride lay on the floor, and the
ore in front was rich and rotten, and of the con-
sistency of crumbling cheese. Quickly the pick
made a chamber for the explosive—then Stewart
carefully placed the,,cartridges with their right
ends touching the fuse.
Aslip-a stumble-and the slight resultant
concussion would have blown him to fragments;
but he did not think of the danger . one
thing, he was too busy, and, besides, when a man
has lived long enough to crush his enemy it seems
terribly difficult to die. A perfect revenge makes
a man seem immortal to himself,
Stewart finished his perilous task, and after
extinguishing the candle groped his way towards
the shaft) uncoi i ing the fuse as he proceeded. He
reached the shaft, secured the knotted ropp, and
then, laying the fuse on the ground, prepared to
light it. But some indefinable desire to make his
revenge still more complete prompted him to
speak again with his enemy. Ho he hailed him :
“ Hulloa, Harry !”
A faint voice, wrung with pain, came from the
well. “I thought you’d gone, Jack. You’ve
murdered me—my thigh’s broken.”
“1 won’t make your misery long,” said the
other voice, very calmly and evenly ; “I’ve got a
20-minute fuse. I’ll say you couldn’t get away
from the blast in time.”
And then he added, softly, as if he hated his
own revenge and loved his enemy, “Good-bye!”
Foster did not answer. Either he had fainted or,
proud animal that he was, he refused to plead
again for lus life. Stewart lit the fuse and, swing-
ing himself on the rope, climbed into the day now
nearly at an end.
A roar as of the loudest thunder, puffs of smoke
from where the shaft had been, the earth vomit-
ing stones and dust, and then the dyke of hard
country surrounding the new lode falls away and
leaves a great green and white glitteringness of
ore, standing in a sharp and defined ridge like the
back of a razor,
Stewart, watching half-way down the slope of
Mountain Boon, saw that the hut and the tent
near the shaft were untouched. He could say he
had fled to the sanctuary of the tent on the first
heave of the ground, and had so escaped destruc-
tion.
Returning to the top of the mountain and find-
ing that the new “blow” exceeded his expecta-
tions he methodically pegged out new application
areas. Then he caught his horse, saddled it, and
mounted. When the dusk closed in his tired
horse was striding through the sand again, and
looking back the new mining magnate saw the
halt-wrecked hut on Mountain Boon, dimly out-
lined against the sky.
Near the entrance to Fowler’s Gap he heard the
bleating of a lamb deserted or lost by its mother.
He looked around him and found the little
creature lying in the dust just off the track. He
dismounted to take it up, and rode on his way
with the lamb nestling behind the valise.
He was always kind to animals.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
Khaled.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
In Marion Crawford’s novel of this name the love of a
woman gave the object of it a soul, which he did not
before possess.
I have a shrine, the fire is dim,
The zealot’s heart is sad,
No deity appears to him,
No Vision makes him glad.
But aye in loyal hope content
Pie waits her pleasure still,
On true devotion lowly bent
And subject to her will.
And will the goddess turn and deign
To smile upon her slave,
Or shall the torment and the pain
For ever vex his grave ?
Some idol every man must hold
As radiant, all-divine,
And I the bold am over-bold
Since, lady, you are mine.
I have no lordly roll of fame
To sway thy woman-heart;
I have not might thy soul to claim
And crush the meaner part.
And great thoughts surging in the soul
But feeble utterance find ;
And wide the gulfs for ever roll
Between the speech and mind.
But still I feel, the while I scan
Those glorious orbs of light,
That for a moment I am Man
And champion of the right.
All evil phantoms swiftly flee
Thy radiant glance before,
Thy voice the music of a sea
That laps no mortal shore.
Words can but dimly utter thought
But kindred souls commune
Sweet variations nature-wrought
On one familiar tune.
And only by thy side, sweet friend.
And suppliant at thy feet
The airs divine in softness blend
In harmony complete.
I said I had no roll of fame—
If some bold charm for thee
The earth shall as one voice proclaim
The deeds that mine shall be ;
Or if the poet’s thought delight
Thy 6wn true poet-soul,
Great thoughts for thee I’ll sing aright
And epic strains control.
Or, if the wealth of ages hold
But value in thy eyes.
Thy riches shall be manifold
And precious gems thy prize,
And glorious stores of colour, caught
In costly diadem,
Shall be with finer lustre fraught
When you have gazed on them.
But if to passions that consume
But friendship you can dole,
My baser nature I assume
Since you possess my soul;
A woman gave, by fate decreed,
To Khaled soul and throne,
But you reverse the nobler deed,
And rob me of mine own.

Dear Bulletin,— The truth about the
first 'Woman : Adam was told to take Ids pick
from among the animals as they filed past. He
noticed one particularly, by the almost human
intelligence in its face, and at once roped it in,
tamed it, and called it Woman. It has stayed
with his descendants ever since.— Heki.
The law against vagrancy seems to be
morally invalidated—in metropolitan districts
anyway—by the hotel free-lunch system. A Mel-
bourne wharf-lodger was last week sentenced to
twelve months’ imprisonment as one having no
visible means of support, whereas the only evi-
dence against him showed that he did support him-
self efficiently by “scoffing” the free, easy, and
accessible victuals supplied by philanthropic
publicans. Meat, bread, cheese, and tempting
little slabs of mystery are placed on bars and
tables for the benefit of customers, and the gentle-
man aforesaid was the hungriest customer on
record. The fact that he dispensed with liquid
refreshment was scarcely to the point. Perhaps
his medical man had ordered him to drink nothing
but Yarra water. He saw means of support for
which there was no direct charge, and he took ’em.
He had committed no statute crime j he was
plump, cheerful, and contented—doing well, in
fact —yet the Law arrogantly ruled that his real
social condition was invisible, and dropped the
poor joker into durance vile.

“The Last of Six
Tales of the Austral Tropics.
By ERNEST PAVENC3.
With Prefatory Note hy Molf Boldrewood.
of tlie Press s
Opinions
“ The volume takes its name from one of the sketches, ‘ The Last of Six,’ though in point of merit they all stand pretty well on a par. The
author who is familiar with life in Northern Queensland, writes with vigour about the doings of the whites and the natives in these faraway
parts ’ A strong vein of mysticism runs through his narratives, and there are ghosts in almost every chapter, and for those who are fond of
realistic work the volume will prove attractive reading.”—S.M. Herald, July 8, 1893.
“ No writer has given the world a more vivid description of Australia and its wilderness than Ernest Favenc, and the secret of this is that
he has bv many years’ experience of travel and bush life, acquired a knowledge of this continent which is possessed by scarcely any other writer.
He too possesses great descriptive powers. Whatever he puts pen to is certain to be good. In the stories under notice he has given us some of
his best’work. That they will be widely read is certain.”— Sydney Mail, July 8, 1893.
“ They present with forceand vividness, as well as with the accuracy arising from long contact and keen observation, striking pictures of
parts of Australia unfamiliar to most of its inhabitants ; they tell, sometimes with remarkable power, of incidents such as happen in wild places
when human hearts are stirred by strong passions, and of weird imaginings that move the minds of lonely men. The Last of Six l is a terrible
tkle of cannibalism, treachery, and revenge upon the dead, which in other hands might be gross and hideous, but which Mr Favenc makes of
thrhlluff interest ‘The Haunt of the Jinkarras’ suggests Rider Haggard and his imitators to some extent, but is itself relieved from the
suspicion of imitation by faithful local colour and a grotesquerie of conception, in which Mr. Favenc here and there approaches Poe. This
characteristic is especially noticeable in ‘ Spirit Led,’ which the great American might have written. A Cup of Cold Water is, perhaps, the
best talc in a collection that should be widely read, and which forms a worthy addition to .purely Australian literature. -Daily Telegraph,
July 15, 1893.
“ ‘ The Last of Six •’ and other stories. By Ernest Favenc. (Sydney: The Bulletin Newspaper Co ) This is a collection of short but
sketches bv Mr Favenc, the well-known explorer, who has a rare collection of Australian legendary lore. All the stories here are good,
and we can heartily endorse the recommendatory notice by Rolf Boldrewood prefixed to this collection. The present issue forms the third of The
Bulletin series of Australian reminiscences.”— Australian Star, July 15,1893.
